Tuesday, 16th March, 1937.

ad-
The Senate-which had stood
journed since Thursday, 17th December-met at a quarter after Three of the
Clock, Colonel SINCLAIR (Deputy-
Speaker) in the Chair.
Papers Presented to Parliament.
The following Papers were presented
during the Recess :-

I have received the King's Command
to convey to the Senate of Northern
Ireland the thanks of His Majesty and
of Her Majesty the Queen for the expression of loyalty and devotion conveyed in their loyal and dutiful
Address, and I am to assure them of
His Majesty's deep appreciation of the
confidence of the Senate of Northern
Ireland in him.
PRIVATE BUSINESS.
Armstrong's Divorce Bill.
Brought from the House of Commons,
and read a First time.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have read a Second
time Armstrong's Divorce Bill; they
have passed the Unemployment Assistance
(Temporary Provisions) Bill, to which
they desire the concurrence of this House.
Unemployment Assistance (Temporary
Provisions) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long).
Viscount CHARLEMONT: In moving
the Second Reading of this Bill I should
like to say that it is a Finance Measure,
the purpose of which, I am sure, is familiar to hon. Members, who will recollect
that similar Measures were passed by them
in 1935 and in 1936. It is proposed by
means of the Bill to extend until the
31st day of this month the powers
accorded to the Government by the
Unemployment (Temporary Provisions)
(No. 2) Act, to recoup to local authorities
the approximate cost to them of continuing to provide poor relief in consequence of the postponement of the
Second Appointed Day under the Unemployment Assistance Act, 1934. I beg
to move.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committeed."(Viscount Charlemont).
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered, That the Bill be read the
Third time on Thursday next.
Ancient Monuments Bill [S.]
Order for Second Reading read
Viscount CHARLEMONT: This Bill,
as hon. Members will remember, was
introduced last session, but did not proceed beyond a Second Reading as in the
time available there was no opportunity
to consider adequately how far it would
be possible to amend the Measure in order
to meet certain views expressed in the
course of debate in this House.
In order to avoid repetition, I will only
deal on this occasion with the leading
points in which this Bill differs from the
one which has already been before you.
In Clauses 2 and 3, dealing with the
power of the Ministry of Finance to
enter upon lands for purposes of investigation, and to control, by a system of
licensing, excavations undertaken for
archæological purposes, certain minor
changes have been made for the purpose of
securing all due consideration to the
owner or occupier of the land. Thus,
plantations have been added to the
classes of property which may not be
entered by the Ministry for investigation without the consent of the owner,
and there is a like protection for burial
grounds in which rights of burial are
exercisable.
In Section 4 the opportunity has been
taken to review and consolidate the provisions of Section 18 of the original Act
of 1926, dealing with the reporting of
archæological finds. The definition of
"archæological object" has been amended
in a manner suggested by legal Members
of this House. As in last session's Bill,
a finder is required to report and produce his find, the persons specified for
reception of this report being the Secre-
63 Ancient Monuments Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Viscount Charlemont]
tary of the Ancient Monuments Advisory
Council or the officer in charge of the
nearest R.U.C. station. The requirement of reporting to the police was
formerly embodied in Regulations, but
now has been included in the Statute
itself. By a new provision, if the find
is not readily transportable, as in the
case, for instance, of a dug-out canoe or
a fragile object, the finder is allowed to
specify the fact in his report and state
where the object can be viewed.
In order that opportunity may be
given to scientific persons interested to
examine and obtain information as to the
circumstances of discovery of a find, and
that museums may have the opportunity
of acquiring it, a stay of 21 days, as compared with the three months in the Bill
of last session, has been imposed on the
disposal of the find, and a penalty is
imposed on a breach of this regulation.
This penalty in last year's Bill included
imprisonment as an alternative to a fine,
but this has now been reduced to fine
only. It will be noticed, also, that a
person who acquires a find during the
period of stay is liable to a penalty
equally with the finder who disposes of
it. This provision is necessary, because
it is the experience of the Ministry of
Finance and of the museums and learned
societies that a certain kind of collector
or dealer cares little whether there is a
proper scientific report of a find as compared with the advantage of exclusive
possession, or the prospect of a profitable.
resale.
The finder, who is often an illiterate
person, usually receives much less from
this class of acquirer than he would get.
from a museum. The find is spirited
away, its place and circumstances of
finding may never be adequately
reported, and the loss to knowledge, the
prevention of which is the sole purpose
of this section, is frequently great. There
is nothing so regrettable in the eyes of
any serious scholar as the presentation
of an antiquity with no record, or only
the vaguest record, of the circumstances
of finding. These things are not mere
curios.
They are materials for the scientific
study of a period of history for which
there are often no other records. If the
written record is to be gathered and protected, so also should the unwritten.
Three weeks is not a long stay, and will
hardly cause any recognisable loss. It
will, as has been said, in most cases act
to the advantage of the finder, and the
vandal or profiteer deserves no particular
protection or consideration. Under the
operation of the existing provision for
report of finds, many finders have received an immensely better price from
the museum than they would have got
from a collector or dealer.
Generally in the revised Bill penalties
for non-compliance with Ancient Monuments legislation have been modified.
The Ministry has been able to make
this concession as the result of its experience of the last eleven years, during
which it has not found it necessary to
institute any prosecution under the Act
of 1926. So far, at any rate, it has
found that, on being acquainted with the
law, finders usually show a readiness to
comply with it, and should there be
recalcitrance in future, the mitigated
penalties will probably suffice to deal
with it. The remainder of the Bill is
subst ntially already before this
House. I beg to move, "That this Bill
be now read a Second time."
as
Mr. LESLIE: I think the House will
agree with me when I suggest that the
delay over this Bill to which the noble
Viscount referred at the commencement
of his speech has been amply justified,
for, to my mind at all events, the Bill
before us this afternoon is in many
respects, and many important respects, a
great improvement on the Bill which was
introduced last session. The noble
Viscount referred to the fact that the
possibility of imprisonment suggested last
session has now been abandoned. I am
very glad that that is so, for I cannot
conceive any Parliament in its senses in
the twentieth century, having recently
dealt with a Census Bill which required
certain information from the citizens and
did not see any necessity to impose imprisonment in default of such information
being forthcoming, allowing imprisonment for a breach of regulations in connection with ancient monuments.
There is no need to repeat what was
said last year, and I will therefore draw
attention to two or three of the Clauses.
First of all, in Clause 1, I notice a new
provision, and it is really rather an
amusing little provision. It is in subclause (2), of Clause 1, where wɔ for the
65 Ancient Monuments Bill-

16 MARCH 1937
Second Reading
first time get a suggested amendment to
the Schedule of the Act of 1926. The
Schedule of the Act of 1926 stated what
bodies should be represented on what
was then the Ancient Monuments Committee. That Act gave distinct power
to His Grace the Governor to alter the
constitution of the Schedule as he saw
fit. The same Section provided for the
appointment of the individual Member by
the Ministry of Finance, and one 18
inclined to wonder why that course cannot be followed now without further
legislation.
The proposal as it comes to us in the
sub-clause I have referred to seems to me
to amount to the constitution, let us
say, of a College of Curators from which
the Ministry of Finance is to make the
selection. When you come to constitute
a new body it seems to me at first sight
that one is rather infringing a power
given to His Grace only. I do not think
it makes any practical difference, and I
would be the last to suggest that these
curators would not be excellent members
of the Ancient Monuments Advisory
Council, but I do think some explanation
might be given to us at a later stage
as to why that very desirable object has
to be attained by this particular means.
Passing to Clauses 2 and 3, I may say at
once that those views I expressed last year,
both in this House and subsequently outside, have been very fully met. I am
quite satisfied with Clauses 2 and 3 as
they are now before us, and I think
some good results may be expected.
certainly agree that there is no provocation in either of those Clauses likely to
lead to the creation of a body of recalcitrant opinion in these matters.
I
Clause 4 is a distinctly difficult Clause.
I have to say that in mv judgment Clause
4 is still far too dictatorial in language.
It may not be so bad as its predecessor
of last year, but it is still not what I
could wish. What we want in this
matter is to secure willing coadjutors
amongst the public. We do not want to
make a helot of the man who has, am I
to say, the misfortune to find an archeological object. The dictatorial attitude.
was, I am afraid, rather exemplified in
the speech of the noble Viscount. He
spoke of a man being allowed to do this,
and he spoke also of the Ministry making
a concession. I do not think either of
those terms was at all applicable to the
circumstances.
ber that the edifice we desire to construes by this Clause is a house built on
sand We have not the least idea at this
moment as to what sort of archæological
objects are to be found, and we must
recognise that there will be very distinct
difficulty in finding whether a particular
ol ject is or is not an archæological object
within the meaning of the Act.
I think we must remem-
We have also to remember that these
objects must be presumed to have a
certain value. Very likely most of them
will have no value at all in excess of
the object in itself, or of the materials
of which it is composed, and if that be
so it will not, under the Bill, be an
archæological object at all. We are
really completely in a haze in matters
of that kind, and we can only do our
best to encourage people who find these
things, without any undue trouble to
themselves, to get expert opinion and to
allow expert opinion to be formed as to
what the object is. We want to
encourage that, but encouragement is
one thing and coercion is another.
There is another matter that was
mentioned last year, and that is the
question of the ownership of these
archæological objects. I am afraid it is
not quite recognised, even in Clause 4,
that the finder of an archæologcial object
is not necessarily the owner. It is the
rights of the owner that must be safeguarded, but happily this Parliament has
no power to deprive anybody of anything
that belongs to him except on payment
of compensation.
Passing from that, I want the House
to consider for a moment something
about the finder himself. I am going to
suggest to the House that the Clause 4
finder may belong to one of two classes.
He may be what we may describe as a
casual finder, the person to whom I think
the Clause is particularly directed, but
he may be the licensee of the Ministry
under an earlier Clause in the Bill. He
may be the man who has got a licence
to dig and excavate and so on. Is it
intended that the same provisions are to
apply to a man who has got a licence to
dig and excavate, it may be in his own
grounds, as to the purely casual person?
As the Bill stands there is no distinction
drawn between these two classes of
finders. I am inclined to think that is
B
Ancient Monuments Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
perhaps a matter which will merit consideration.
The Ministry propose to take power to
impose certain conditions when granting
these licences. We have to be careful
we do not do anything in Clause 4 which
will run counter to Clause 2, and the
suggestion now is to put a stay on the
transfer of the object itself, irrespective
of the property in it, of twenty-one days.
That in itself is a somewhat serious
matter. It is an interference with
property. I do not say, of course, that
twenty-one days is an unreasonably long
period. It can only be in most unusual
cases that it can have any serious effect,
but to restrict a man dealing with his
own property or an article of which he
has possession is in itself a
measure, and I am not at all certain it
is not sailing remarkably near the wind
as regards the powers of this Parliament.
However, I should be disposed to encourage a man to keep it for that twentyone days so that an expert may look
at it and the man himself may know
what it really is.
-
strong
But I think those who are responsible
for this Bill-I am speaking of the
archæological people must remember
that their Bill will have to stand the fire
of both Houses, and that while everybody is anxious to show such interest as
is possible to archæological research, there
are certain principles, the liberty of the
subject and the rights of property, which
cannot be allowed to be endangered in
order that some probably few persons
may ride their hobby horses through
other people's vegetable gardens. We
want to look for our allies to common
sense and common courtesy. These
attributes are exhibited to the full at
present by our archæological visitors,
and I think it is there and not to compulsion you must look for the help you
require. I consider this Clause will
require a certain amount of amendment,
perhaps merely of a verbal character, in
order to bring out more fully the principles to which I have alluded.
I do not think I need detain the House
with any commentary on the other
Clauses, but I should like to express my
satisfaction at the manner in which
Clause 9, which deals with the expenses
of this Act, is now before us. On the
whole I am quite in support of the Bill,
but I shall have to criticise and endeavour
to amend Clause 4, and I want to do it
in the most friendly spirit at the proper
time. I want to do it in order that
those principles I have referred tojustice to the real owner and the full
protection of the liberty of the subjectmay receive rather more attention than
they receive from Clause 4 as it stands
at present.
Captain PERCEVAL - MAXWELL:
First of all I should like to acknowledge
the readiness with which the noble
Viscount met some of the objections
raised to the Ancient Monuments Bill on
the last occasion it was before the House.
In his speech to-day I think he was
rather inclined to
suggest that the
Amendments which were then suggested
were not very material. I believe there
is nothing more material than the
liberty of the subject, and although we
in this House are anxious to preserve
these ancient monuments which have
been handed down to us and which are
valuable not only from an antiquarian
but from an historical point of view, it
is very important that they should be
preserved with the good wishes of the
owners of the property rather than
against their wishes. For that reason I
think this Bill is a vast improvement on
the Bill which was formerly before this
House.
There is very little, if anything, in
this Bill to which anyone can now
object, either on the grounds of harshness or executive interference with the
rights of property. The hon. and
learned Senator (Mr. Leslie) has pointed
out with his usual care some points in
the Bill which it may be desirable to
consider again on the Committee stage.
On one of the points he mentioned I do
not think I can agree with him. That
is his objection to the finder retaining
the object for twenty-one days. It is, of
course, an objection founded on the principle we have been discussing in this
House, the rights of private ownership,
but I think the hon. and learned Senator
will agree that the person who finds this
property is not necessarily the owner at
all.
In fact, the finder is usually a person
who is not the owner. Therefore it
seems to me that it is in the interests
Ancient Monuments Bill

Adjournment
16 MARCH 1937
of the owner of the land as well as the sufficient to enable the Act to be worked,
finder that the latter should not be able and, after all, that is what we all want.
to dispose of the object until after the
lapse of some time so that the owner may
have time to discover what his rights of
redress at law may be, and, if necessary,
to take such steps before the court as
will enable him to get his own property.
My objection to Clause 4 is founded
on much the same principle as that of
the hon. and learned Senator, but I think
there is a danger of a careless finder
losing the object, or being so careless
with its preservation that it is stolen
from him. In such a case neither the
Ancient Monuments Council nor the
owner could obtain any compensation.
Most of these objects are found by
labourers who are ploughing or working
on the land, and a labourer's cottage is
generally not the best place to preserve
an object of any intrinsic value. Therefore I suggest he should hand the object
over to the R.U.C. to be retained by
them in safe custody for the period of
twenty-one days. I propose to place an
Amendment on the Paper to give effect
to that suggestion.
The interference with the rights of
ownership, if there is any, is so trivial
that I do not think we would be justified
in resisting the proposal in the Bill. It
is important both from an historical and
archæological point of view that these
objects should be preserved, and to argue
that retention of an article for twentyone days is an infringement of the rights.
of ownership is too nice a distinction to
be any objection to this Clause.
very pleased to see that the noble
Viscount, in spite of the argument he
had about the meaning of the term
"archæological object," has inserted in
the Bill a definition to prevent any doubt
arising in the future. The definition
applies not only to this Bill but to the
former Act, and now I think there can
be no objection to this definition.
I am
The only trouble is that, as the hon.
and learned Senator has pointed out,
even the definition which has been
inserted is unsatisfactory from many
points of view, but I do not think we
can overcome that difficulty. I do not
think there is any way of defining an
archæological object sufficiently strictly
to prevent any possibility of doubt
arising, and in this case the definition is
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I think
the various points raised by the hon. and
learned Senators could best be considered
on the Committee stage. In my opinion
some more precise definition of owner
and finder might be an advantage. Many
years ago, when I was more ignorant
that I am now, I was walking across a
field in Norfolk, and I found a thin
arrow in the ground immediately underneath my feet. I quickly put it in my
pocket, because the owner of the ground
happened to be walking with me and I
was desirous that he should not see it.
I have it still. Something of that kind
might happen here, and it would be very
difficult to guard against it. As regards
the question of the liberty of the subject, it must be remembered that some
of the objects found might be of national
importance, and it might be a nice point
whether interference with the actual
owner of the land on which the object
was found is of more importance than the
interests of the community as a whole.
However, the Senate can be the judge of
that when the Committee stage is
reached.

Adjournment.
Mr. LONG: I propose to ask the House
to adjourn during pleasure to await the
receipt of the Consolidated Fund Bill if
the Commons dispose of it. I intend to
ask a quorum of the House to remain
until four o'clock. If we receive the Bill
by that time we can put it through all
stages to-day, which will obviate a meeting on Tuesday. If we have not received
the Bill by four o'clock we shall have to
meet on Tuesday at the usual time, and
on Wednesday at twelve o'clock for the
Royal Assent.
Captain PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
Does the Deputy-Leader propose to take
the Committee stage of the Ancient
Monuments Bill on Wednesday?
Mr. LONG: If we have to meet on
Tuesday I propose that that day shall
be substituted for Wednesday as respects

Questions to Minister.
.
THE DESTRUCTION OF RABBITS.
Captain PERCEVAL MAXWELL
asked the Leader of the House whether
the Minister of Agriculture has considered the use of Bacillus Myxomatosis
Cuniculi (Sanarelli) for reducing the
rabbit population; and, if so, what is the
result of his investigations.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): I am informed by my right
hon. Friend, the Minister of Agriculture,
that he has not considered the question
of destroying rabbits by the method referred to by the hon. and gallant Senator.
Inquiries which have been made indicate
that experiments conducted in Australia
show that the disease mentioned in the
Question, commonly known as "rabbit
myxomatosis," is a filterable virus disease
producing almost 100 per cent. mortality.
However, in Australia, where, of course,
rabbits are a particularly serious pest,
consideration of destruction by this means
does not appear to have got beyond the
experimental state.
It is understood that the disease, once
introduced, might be difficult to control
and would possibly lead to the extermination of the entire rabbit population.
This total extinction might, or might not,
be desirable, but the eradication might be
very dearly bought if the method led also
to the destruction of more useful animals
or plants. It will, therefore, be realised
that the letting loose in this country of
such a highly lethal agent would require
the most serious consideration. If the
hon. Senator wishes to pursue the matter
further, I would refer him to the article
which appeared in the issue of "Nature,"
dated 5th September, 1936, under the
title, "Control of Rabbit Infestation by
the Use of a Virus."
CO-ORDINATION OF WATER
SUPPLY.
Captain PERCEVAL - MAXWELL
asked the Leader of the House whether
the Government appointed a Committee
of Inquiry to investigate and report on
the co-ordination of water supply and
the provision of water in rural areas, with
particular regard to the danger that uncoordinated urban supply may exhaust the
supply in rural areas and lead to waste
of water resources.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I am informed by my right hon. Friend the
Minister of Home Affairs, that the Government has given careful consideration
to the suggestion contained in the hon.
and gallant Senator's question, and also
to those put forward by him on a previous
occasion to a somewhat similar effect, but
it has reached the conclusion that conditions in Northern Ireland are not such
as to call for an inquiry of the kind
mentioned.
Ice-Cream Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Viscount Charlemont]
Generally speaking, the requirements
of rural areas are met by wells and could
hardly be met in any other way having
regard to the distances between dwellings,
and there is no indication that the provision of gravitation supplies in towns has
interfered with these rural supplies.
Any considerable building developments in rural areas which would require
more than wells to provide a proper water
supply are usually in the neighbourhood
of towns which have a supply, and the
latter have power to provide water to outside areas by arrangement with the rural
district council concerned.
Sale of Ice-Cream Bill (S.)
Order for Second Reading read.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The Bill
which I am submitting to the House this
afternoon constitutes an interesting illustration of a significant change in our
views of the relationship in which such
matters as agriculture, nutrition, and
public health stand to one another.
Hitherto, for legislative purposes, it has
been the custom to consider such topics
in isolation and to deal with them by
separate enactments. To-day it has become increasingly difficult to deal effectively with problems of nutrition without
some regard to agricultural policy. Also
it has been realised that it is impracticable to divorce the consideration of
methods of production of foodstuffs from
that of standards of cleanliness which are
desirable on public health grounds.
For these reasons an analysis of the Bill
under the three heads: Nutrition, agriculture and public health, will, I think,
facilitate me in making clear its purposes
and the principles upon which it is based.
Clause 1 of the Bill enables the
Ministry of Agriculture to prescribe
minimum standards of milk fat and of
solids including milk fat, with which all
ice-cream sold in Northern Ireland must
comply. The Clause also seeks to prohibit the sale of ice-cream which contains
any fats other than milk fat, that is,
vegetable fats. These provisions which
are the basis of local legislation,
which is in operation in the County
Borough of Londonderry and
Borough of Bangor are designed to
the
ensure (1) that ice-cream shall not be
sold in this country unless it contains a
reasonable proportion of food values; and
(2) that these food values shall, as far as
possible, be derived from fresh whole
milk. Ice-cream is consumed largely by
the younger members of the community,
and it is, therefore, desirable that those
who purchase this commodity should
receive something which is not merely
pleasant to the taste, but is also wholesome and nutritious.
Class 3 seeks to provide that ice-cream
shall comply with a prescribed standard
governing the relation between its volume
and its weight. This provision may
require some explanation. The ingredients out of which the ice-cream is to be
made are, during the process of manufacture, subjected to a process of aeration
or "swelling" which has the effect of
increasing the volume of the final product without of course reducing the
proportion of milk and other solids which
are calculated on a weight basis. Thus,
of two portions of ice-cream equal in
volume and both complying with standards prescribed under the first clause,
one may have a considerably higher food
value than the other.
""
The object of this provision is to protect those who produce the former type
of ice-cream. A certain amount of
"swelling" is necessary in the production of palatable ice-cream, but it is considered desirable in the public interest
that some check upon excessive "swelling" or overrun "should be provided.
since otherwise the standards prescribed
under Clause 1 may not have the effect
they are designed to secure. It is, moreover, probable that those manufacturers
who rely mainly upon fresh milk for the
manufacture of ice-cream, subject their
ice-cream to a lesser degree of aeration
than their competitors. It is believed
that by restricting overrun "' it will be
possible to give a certain advantage to
those manufacturers who use fresh whole
milk as one of the main constituents of
their ice-cream.
""
Under Clause 8 the Ministry of Agriculture seeks authority to provide for the
sale of ice-cream of high quality under
the designation " premium." It will be
a condition of the licence issued under
Clause 8 that licence-holders who manufacture ice-cream shall, for the purpose
of providing the milk fat for such ice-
Ice-Cream Bill-
Second Reading
13 APRIL 1937
cream, utilise fresh whole milk of a standard of quality not less than Grade C.
These provisions will, it is hoped, incidentally conduce to the consumption of
ice-cream of a higher nutritional value.
Secondly, I come to the agricultural
aspect of this question. The manufacture of ice-cream, taking place as it
does mainly during the summer season,
forms a useful outlet for the surplus of
milk which producers find on their hands
at that time of the year. It is, therefore, desirable that the ice-cream manufacturers should receive every encouragement to use fresh milk of good quality,
and it is hoped that both the standards
prescribed under Clause 1 and the provisions in Clause 8 relating to "premium"
ice-cream will have the effect of affording
this encouragement.
It will be observed that in Clause 13
provision is made whereby contributions
may be made from the Milk Fund towards
schemes for encouraging the purchase of
ice-cream in the manufacture of which
milk of Grade A, Grade B, or Grade C
has been used. This will enable "premium" ice-cream to be advertised and
such publicity to be carried out as will
give an advantage to those ice-cream
manufacturers who use fresh milk in the
manufacture of their ice-cream.
Thirdly, I come to the consideration of
those provisions which relate to the public
health. Clause 1 seeks to make it an offence
against the Sale of Food and Drugs' Acts
to sell ice-cream which contains preservatives prohibited under regulations made
under the Public Health (Regulations as
to Food), Act, 1907. Clause 2 provides
that ice-cream shall not be sold unless it
complies with a prescribed standard of
cleanliness, and for the purpose of this
provision samples will be subjected to
bacteriological examination from time to
time.
Clause 4 provides for the registration
of premises used for the manufacture of
ice-cream. Clause 5 empowers local sanitary authorities to refuse to register, or
to cancel the registration of, premises if
they are satisfied that the public health
is likely to be endangered by the use of
those premises for the manufacture or
sale of ice-cream. Clause 4 also empowers
local sanitary authorities to make by-laws
for the regulation of premises used for
the manufacture or sale of ice-cream.
Clause 6 makes specific provision for
ensuring that ice-cream shall not be exposed to contamination during the process
of manufacture or sale.
I may say that the local authorities
have been consulted in regard to those
provisions of the Bill which especially
concern them and have expressed their
general approval. The more important
organisations of the ice-cream trade have
also been consulted and are generally in
agreement with the principles of the Bill.
I should like at this point to pay a
special tribute to the councils of the
Belfast County Borough, Londonderry
County Borough, and Bangor Borough,
which have in recent years obtained
powers under local Acts similar to those
which it is now proposed to confer upon
local authorities, generally. The experience of these councils has been of
inestimable value to the Government in
the preparation of this Bill, and their
pioneer work has enabled the Government
to propose the extension of similar provision to the rest of the Province with
greater confidence than would otherwise
have been the case.
Most of the other clauses of the Bill
relate to administrative machinery, and I
do not think that I need refer to them
in any detail. I might, however, mention Clause 2, which is designed to prevent ice-cream vendors from evading the
provisions of the Bill by selling their
product under some designation other
than ice-cream. I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
Mr. QUIGLEY: I would like to say
how pleased I am that this very important Bill, in the interests of the health
of our people, has been brought forward.
Clauses 4 and 6, and indeed all the
Clauses, are very necessary and very important. Two years ago I had occasion
to make an inquiry at a house in the
centre of the city of Belfast, where there
are a considerable number of ice-cream
vendors. I was asked into the kitchen,
and in that kitchen there were four icecream carts. I presume they had been
there over-night, and they were not at all
clean. This Bill will certainly have a
great effect in producing good, clean.
healthy ice-cream under proper conditions, and I have very great pleasure in
supporting the Second Reading.

Ancient Monuments Bill [S.]

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Ancient Monuments
Advisory Council).
Viscount CHARLEMONT: In regard
to Sub-section 2 of this Clause, the hon.
and learned Senator (Mr. Leslie) in his
Second Reading observations, asked for
some information as to why it was considered desirable to refer specifically to
Curators of Museums, seeing that under
the 1926 Act such persons could have
been appointed to the Committee by the
Minister of Finance or by the Governor
of Northern Ireland. The Curator of the
Belfast Museum does, in fact, sit on the
Committee at the present time by reason
of a nomination made by the Minister,
but it is considered that it would be more
appropriate to the importance of that
office that the holder of it should exofficio be a member of the Committee.
Moreover, interest in the educational
side of antiquities is being aroused in
Northern Ireland, and already the
County of Armagh has, since the year
1926, established a local museum on
sound lines fostered by the enthusiasm
of its curator, Mr. Patterson, who, I
may say, has been one of the foremost
voluntary helpers in assisting the Ancient
Monuments Committee in compiling a
record of antiquities in the County
Armagh. It is proposed under the
powers conferred by this Section to include the Armagh as well as the Belfast
Museum as one of those whose curators
will ex-officio become members of the
Council.
Mr. LESLIE: I am obliged to the
noble Viscount for the information.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.
CLAUSES 2 (Power of Ministry of
Finance to enter upon lands for purposes
of inspection), and 3 (Restriction of
excavation for archæological purposes),
ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 4 (Provisions as to archæological objects found).
(1) The finder of any archæological
object (in this section referred to as a
"finder") shall, within fourteen days
of such finding, report the circumstances of the finding and the character
of the object either to the secretary of
the Ancient Monuments Advisory
Council or to the officer in charge
of the Royal Ulster Constabulary station.
nearest to the place where such object
was found, and shall together with such
report produce or forward the object
found:
Provided, however, that should the
object be of such a nature as not to be
readily portable, the finder shall so
specify in his report and shall state
where the object can be viewed.
(2) The finder shall not, until after
the expiration of twenty-one days from
the date of furnishing such report to
the secretary of the Council or the
police, as the case may be, part with
the possession of such object otherwise
than in accordance with the provisions
of this section.
(3) A finder who, without reasonable cause, acts in contravention
of any provision
provision of this section,
or
any other person who knowingly obtains possession of any
such object from the finder within the
period of twenty-one days mentioned in
the last preceding sub-section, shall be
guilty of an offence, and shall be liable
on summary conviction to a fine not
exceeding five pounds.
*
*
*
*
(6) The Ministry of Finance may
make regulations-
(a) prescribing the action to be taken
by the Ancient Monuments Advisory Council or the officer in charge
of the Royal Ulster Constabulary
station in regard to archæological
objects produced or received and
reports furnished;
(b) prescribing the body or bodies in
whose custody archæological objects
may be deposited;
Ancient Monuments Bill-
Committee
13 APRIL 1937
(c) generally for carrying this section
into effect.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 4, Sub-clause (1), to leave out
from the word "to" in line 9 to the end
of the Sub-clause and insert instead thereof the words-
"An officer in charge of a Royal
Ulster Constabulary station, and, if
such finder does not deposit or forward
such object with the report, he shall
state where and when persons authorised in that behalf by the Ministry of
Finance or the Ancient Monuments
Advisory Council may inspect the
object."
This Amendment, like all the other
Amendments I have on the Paper, is
directed to do all that can properly be
done by Parliament to assist the Ancient
Monuments Advisory Council, but at the
same time to safeguard most strictly the
liberty of the subject and the rights of
property. I think I must ask the Committee to remember that the person who
finds an archæological object is not necessarily a convict on ticket-of-leave, and,
therefore, it is undesirable to treat him
as though he were. Our finder may quite
possibly be, let us say, a churchwarden
with access to the church safe, where he
can put small things. He might be a
banker with a strong room of his own, or
he might be anybody else in possession of
a fire- and thief-proof safe. Almost anybody might find an archæological object,
but everybody who comes across something
that may or may not be such an object is
not bound to "find" it.
In the Bill there is no definition of
finding, and there may be people who, if
they are in any way annoyed, will simply
see the pin and let it lie, and that will not
be very helpful. I want to lead the
finder in the path that the Advisory Committee desire him to tread, but I want
to abstain most carefully from coercing
him or threatening to coerce him, because I am quite satisfied that after one
or two cases of coercion there will not be
any more finders. That is simply human
nature, and it is our experience in other
matters.
In order to attain my object I propose
to leave out seven lines of print and
substitute the five lines which appear as
the first Amendment on the Paper. First
of all, I want to show what I want to
leave out and why I want to leave it out,
and then I want to show what I propose
to put in, and why I want to put it in.
The Bill requires that a find has to be
reported to the Royal Ulster Constabulary
station nearest to the place where such
object was found. I submit that
that is simply a silly proposal. What
on earth does it matter where the police
office stands in relation to the spot where
the object is found? It may not have
occurred to whoever is behind this particular proposal that a man living in
Armoy may find an archæological object
in a sheep mountain some fifteen miles
away. He does not know what the
nearest police station is, and he would
naturally go home and report to the
barracks in Armoy, a few yards from his
own residence. There can be no object
in making the finder report to the
barracks nearest to where the object
happened to be found.
Again, in the Bill a man is required,
with his report, to produce or forward
the object found. I am not going to say
anything about forwarding, but I will
say a great deal about producing. To
produce an object requires a man's
attendance at the police barracks. We
have no business to require him to attend
at the barracks. It is a distinct invasion
of his liberty to say to him, "Because
you have had the misfortune to pick up
something to which other people attach
importance you must take it to the
barracks." There is no statutory obligation on a man who picks up anything in
the street to take it to the police barracks.
He not infrequently takes it to the post
office and there sticks up a notice.
you did impose that obligation on him
what would you achieve?
If
For example, I will regard this Committee as a police barracks, and I produce
this pencil. I have produced the pencil,
the Committee has seen it, and I now
put it in my pocket and take it away.
I do not see how, if that pencil were an
archæological object, the desire of the
Advisory Council that archæological
objects should be inspected and examined
is going to be attained. The finder
produces the object and takes it away
again, and that is that. He is bound to
keep it for twenty-one days, but he is not
bound to show it to anybody. He will
have satisfied the law if he produces it.
That seems to me to be absurd.
Ancient Monuments Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
Then we have a proviso which provides
that if the object is not readily portable
the finder shall say so and say where it
can be found. We are not told who is
to view it; whether charabanc trips are
to be run to take people to see it. We
are not given any light as to that, but
the question of whether the thing is or
is not readily portable is left to the man
himself. It is one of the few things in
which he is given an option. I think
these things will not be found helpful,
but that, on the contrary, they will be
found to be oppressive.
For that I propose to substitute that the
report should be made to an officer in charge
of a Royal Ulster Constabulary station,
but I leave the choice of station to the
finder himself. As I want to encourage
the finder to leave the object in the
barracks or send it to the Advisory
Council, I go on to say that if such finder
does not deposit-not produce or forward such object with the report he shall
state where and when it may be inspected
by people authorised by the Ministry or
the Ancient Monuments Advisory Council.
That seems to me to be a perfectly workable plan which is not likely to give any
offence to anybody, and one which, I
hope, will do some good. I think it will
be found to be more helpful than the
words in the text, and will be free from
the objection to which those words in the
text are liable.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The Clause
as it appears in the Bill makes it mandatory on the finder of any object not only
to report it, but to hand over the find to
the secretary of the Ancient Monuments
Advisory Council or a police officer unless
the find is an article like a canoe or a
fragile piece of pottery, which is not
readily portable. What the mover of the
first Amendment seeks to effect is to
remove from the Clause the requirement
that makes it mandatory to deposit the
find and leave it to the finder's discretion.
In fact, it almost restores the position
that exists at present under Section 18 of
the 1926 Act. The Ministry, however,
from actual experience sets great store on
the requirement that the object found
should actually be handed over to proper
custody in order that there may be no
doubt of its being available for scrutiny
by experts. It undoubtedly strengthens
the new provision under which the finder
of the object must not part with it within
a period of twenty-one days. I regret
that the Government is not prepared to
accept the Amendment in toto, but I think
we might possibly agree to substitute the
word "deposit" for the word "produce."
Mr. LESLIE : If I understood the
noble Viscount correctly he asserted that
the Bill requires that the object shall be
left at the police barracks. If he did, in
fact, say that I am afraid I must ask him
to refer us to the passage in the Bill where
that requirement occurs. I cannot find
it.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I think the
best thing to do under the circumstances
would be for the hon. and learned
Member to bring up his Amendment
again on the Report Stage, because I have
not had an opportunity to study the Bill
with the same care that he has studied it.
Captain PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I

which I foresaw and which I intended to

Amendment unnecessary.
The two objections I have to producing
the object and then taking it away is,
first, as Senator Leslie pointed out, that
the finder might not be a man with a
large house and plenty of room in which
to safely keep the object. He might be
a very poor man who did not possess a
safe, and, therefore, I think it is better
that he should have a chance of handing
over his valuable find to someone who will
have a place available in which to lock up
the object, not only for the sake of preventing a man having the custody of a
valuable object of which he did not wish
to take charge, but also for the preservation of that object itself. For these
reasons I would support any suggestion
coming from the Government that the

103 Ancient Monuments Bill---

THE SENATE
Amendments
[Mr. Leslie]
The Amendment, which I am moving
in the House this afternoon, is for all
practical purposes the same as that which
I brought forward when the House was
in Committee the other day, and which
I withdrew, by leave of the Committee of
course, in order that the noble Viscount
might have a further opportunity of looking into the various points that had been
raised. It would appear from the Order
Paper that the noble Viscount used that
opportunity, and hence the Amendment
which appears in his name on the Paper.
That, however, is only part of the
Amendment which I have to move.
It may be remembered that I said when
speaking on the Second Reading of this
Bill, that although I thought the Clause
before us required amendment, I hoped to
be able to amend it in the most friendly
spirit possible, and with a view to giving
the archæological people every possible
assistance in so far as that could be done
without violating established principle.
Therefore, looking into the matter further
after the debates in Committee on the
Amendment now before the House, I
came to the conclusion that it might be
possible, if my Amendment on the Paper
went through, to ask the House after the
Third Reading to amend the period of
time in line 6, on page 3, from fourteen
days to seven days so as to reduce the
period during which a man must exercise
his option, and so assist the Ancient
Monuments Advisory Council in preventing the spiriting away of archæological
objects before there has been time to have
them examined by experts. It seemed to
me that that alteration, which cannot be
made this afternoon, would be of a great
deal more help than following the exact
words of the text.
As the House knows, what I want to
do is to avoid compulsion in this matter.
I want to try persuasion, for I am quite
sure persuasion is the more powerful
weapon of the two. If the House insists
that compulsion is to be exercised I am
afraid anybody outside who may happen
to read the debate or the Bill will say
this is an example of Hitlerism, and if
there is any sort of publication of the
details of the Bill hon. Members who sit
in another place may be required to explain the matter to their constituents,
and I do not think those hon. Members
would thank this House for casting that
responsibilty upon them. That is one
point, the avoidance of compulsion and
requiring a man to go from where he may
live to the police barracks. We shall
have got rid of the requirement that the
report is to be made to the barracks
nearest to the place where the object was
found, but we have not got rid of the
other requirement; that he shall go to the
police barracks and report the finding of
the object. It may be that the nearest
police barracks to his house is five or six
miles away. Therefore, if you require
his personal attendance you are interfering with his personal liberty. So much
for that.
There remains the other point, as to
whether, if you require him to deposit the
object-I think we are all agreed that the
word "deposit" must be used instead of
the word "produce"-you are in effect
taking possession of property without
compensation, and that the Government
of Ireland Act, as we know, does not permit this Parliament to do. Nothing is
said in that Act as regards the confiscation being a permanent deprivation of the
property; all it says is "take any property
without compensation." Although the
finder of this article may not be, and
very probably is not, the owner of it, yet
other than the true owner he is the
owner. It is in his possession. He is in
exactly the same position as the celebrated chimney sweep whose name will
be familiar to some Members of this
House, particularly the hon. and gallant
Member opposite (Captain Perceval-
Maxwell), and who I may, perhaps,
briefly refer to in the words of one of
those little doggerels one used to learn
in the following terms:-
Armory, by seeking trover,
Showed he was not mad,
For, barring than the rightful
owner,
His title was not bad.
That is the title of which it was proposed to deprive him, however, temporarily. All these difficulties disappear
if you follow the Amendment and let all
these things be done by agreement and
not by compulsion. Following the
Amendment further, it will be seen that
I get rid of the distinction between that
which is readily portable and that which
is not, because we have no definition of
what is readily portable. I also define a
little more clearly than is done in the
Ancient Monuments Bill-
Amendments
20 APRIL 1937
Bill the people who would be entitled to
inspect the object. If this Amendment
is accepted we shall have a more workable
provision than we have at present, and it
will be more so if at a later stage we
alter fourteen days to seven.
Captain PERCEVAL - MAXWELL:
A great deal of what I sought to do the
last time this Bill was before the House
will be effected by the Amendment which
is coming on later. Although I have a
great deal of sympathy with the Amendment which has been moved by the hon.
and learned Senator, I do not think I
can quite agree with all he has said.
Some of the things he has said to-day
seem to me to be slightly inconsistent
with what he has said on previous occasions. It is perfectly true that this Bill
imposes some compulsion, but then any
Bill must produce some measure of compulsion and must interfere with rights
and liberties to some extent. Otherwise
all the Ancient Monuments Council
would have to do would be to issue a
notice in the Press. The only thing we
can disagree about is the exact amount
of compulsion. It seems to me that in
asking the finder, who is not necessarily
the owner-in fact, the hon. and learned
Member stressed the point that the finder
will probably very seldom be the ownerto hand the object to a police station
instead of retaining it in his own house
is not exercising a very great deal of
compulsion if the object is an article of
national or archeological importance. I
will go even further and say that if the
object is not owned by the man who finds
it the best thing he can do from both a
moral and legal point of view is to hand
it over to some one who will look after it
until the owner can be ascertained.
Most of these objects, I understand, are
found in circumstances under which it is
very difficult to ascertain the owner. I
am not going to tire the House with a dissertation on the law of estovers, but a
great many of these objects are found in
turf bogs, and there is more litigation
over turf bogs in Ireland than perhaps
over anything else, or there has been in
times past. In any case, the law of
estovers is very complicated. Then there
are scattered over the countryside small
forts which used to be owned by landlords. These forts have now been handed
over to tenants, but, not being agricultural land, they remain in the hands
of the landlords. Imagine how difficult
it will be in ten years' time to decide who
is the owner of these forts.
Then again, many of these objects are
found on the seashore, washed in by the
tide, and it is not very easy for anyone
picking up an object on the seashore to
find out who the owner is. The sensible
thing to do, and the thing that is done
nine times out of ten, without any compulsion, is for the finder, knowing the
thing is not his own, to hand it over to
a police station. Not long ago a wallet
of money was found in Downpatrick, and
it was handed over to the police station.
It does not put any hardship on a person
who finds an object, and if he has any
interest in the object he will send it to
the Ancient Monuments Advisory Council or to a museum, or have it inspected
by someone who knows something about
it, simply out of mere curiosity.
Therefore the only person who can be
damaged in any way is the person who
definitely wishes to dispose of the object
before it can be seen by a person who is
interested, and those are the very people
to whom this Bill attempts to apply some
compulsion. It seems only natural that
some sort of compulsion should be placed
on people who find these objects so that
the Ancient Monuments Advisory Council
shall have a chance of inspecting them.
This seems to be the method most consistent with the usual practice in these
cases, which is to hand over the article
found to the police for the owner to be
ascertained. Very little interference
with private liberty is caused by the Bill,
and such little as there is seems to me to
be definitely in favour of the owner;
certainly as much in favour of him as
against him.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The hon.
and gallant Senator who has just spoken.
has dealt with the amendment from a
common sense point of view, and I will
endeavour to reply to it on legal grounds.
The hon. and learned Senator has
referred to the provisions of section five
of the Government of Ireland Act which
prohibits the Parliament of Northern
Ireland from making laws, the effect of
which is to take property without compensation. The Government is fully
alive to the implications of section five,
but cannot agree that it was ever
107 Ancient Monuments Bill-

THE SENATE
Amendments
[Viscount Charlemont]
intended to cover a case like the one we
are at present considering. Here there
is no intention to take any property.
Notwithstanding the fact that under the
Bill the finder of an archaeological object
will be obliged to deposit it with the
Secretary of the Ancient Monuments
Advisory Council or the officer in charge
of a Royal Ulster Constabulary station,
the property in the object does not pass
to the Government.
The case of Armory against Delamirie,
which the hon. and learned Senator has
cited, is an authority for the proposition
that if the person with whom the object
is deposited refuses, after the expiration
of twenty-one days, to return the object
to the finder, the finder has a good cause
of action against that person.
As we are considering an archæological
Bill, is it not very appropriate that the
hon. and learned Senator should have
delved into law reports and unearthed
the first object really unearthed under
this Bill, an object over 200 years old,
because it happened in 1722, and under
compulsion he is presenting the same for
the admiration of everyone in this House.
I should repeat what I said to the hon.
and learned Senator on this day week,
that "Under Section 9 of the original
Act there is a provision for the payment
of compensation for any Act done by the
Ministry involving interference with
private rights of property, and this
section would be applicable in cases
coming under Clause 4 of the Bill."
an
It has been pointed out that the time
for which the secretary of the Ancient
Monuments Advisory Council, or the
officer in charge of the Royal Ulster
Constabulary Station, may retain
archæological object is not specifically
stated in the Clause, although it is
clearly the intention that the object
should not be retained for more than 21
days. I propose, therefore, at a later
stage to move an Amendment to make
it clear that the object may be retained
for such a period, not exceeding 21 days,
as may be prescribed by regulations.
I hope I have shown that the Government is within its rights in retaining
the element of compulsion to which
exception is taken by the hon. and
learned Member. The reason why his
Amendment cannot be accepted is that
the Government is anxious that objects
discovered shall be kept safe from
damage and readily available for inspection by experts.
Mr. LESLIE: I am sure it must
have struck the House that this Bill has
affiorded opportunities for some entertaining conversation between one side
and the other. I must not reply to the
speech of the hon. and gallant Member
(Captain Perceval-Maxwell) who charged
me with inconsistency by suggesting
irrelevancy, but rather to the remarks
of the noble Viscount. I hope he will
forgive me for congratulating him on the
accuracy of the research of his advisers
as to the date
which this very
on
important principle was laid down. Bit
by bit the noble Viscount is cutting the
ground from under my feet. He is now
prepared to move at a later stage that
the object is not to be retained for more
than 21 days. That certainly disposes
of any argument I could have founded
on the Government of Ireland Act, without prejudice to the general question.
I presume the noble Viscount will tell
us he does not want the alteration of 14
days to seven days, and if we may clearly
understand, first, that the object is not
to be retained against the will of the
depositor for more than 21 days, and,
second, that any expense he is put to in
depositing the object will be refunded to
him, then I could probably ask the
House to allow me to withdraw the
Amendment. Otherwise I could not
do so.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I would
suggest that the hon. and learned
Senator should withdraw his Amendment
until he sees mine at a later stage.
Mr. LESLIE: I think it will be
agreed that that will serve convenience.

THE LATE
COLONEL H. A. PAKENHAM
Viscount CHARLEMONT: Before
formally moving the Adjournment of the
House I should like to say a few words
deploring the death of Colonel Pakenham,
which occurred since the Senate last met.
I am sure this House will join with me
in expressing our great regret at his
passing. Colonel Pakenham was for
many years an assiduous Member of this
House, and he also took an important
part in public affairs outside, chiefly, I
think, in connection with Boy Scouts.
Any movement that had for its object
the good of his country he was always
willing to assist to the best of his ability.
Mr. LESLIE: I should like, if I may,
to follow the noble Viscount in paying
tribute to the memory of our departed
friend. I had necessarily known Colonel
Pakenham more or less for a great many
years. He was, as a matter of fact, my
immediate predecessor in the Sheriffship
of the County of Antrim a good many
years ago now, and he was for many
years a valued colleague of my own on
the Grand Jury of the County. The
noble Viscount has referred briefly to
some of the public activities in which he
took part. There were a number, but I
shall think of him as identified with the
cause of those soldiers wounded in the
war who are not yet restored to health.
Those he took a very great interest in,
and he extended the very friendly
hospitality of his beautiful home to
parties of such soldiers on more than one
occasion.
I understand that he served for some
years as a member of the London County
Council, and that at one time he contested the City of Derry for membership
of the Imperial Parliament. But whatever he did, in all his ways and works,
I can honestly say that he was in reality
one of the most kindly men I ever met.
He was a Guardsman, and that perhaps
attracted some notice when he served
during the late war, but everybody
always came to the conclusion that they
liked him, and that he was fair and just
to all men. He leaves a very fragant
memory behind him. (Hon. Members:
Hear, hear.)
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I now beg
to move, "That this House do now
adjourn until Thursday next."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at five
minutes to Four o'clock
until Thursday next.

Question to Minister.
THIRD-PARTY MOTOR INSURANCE
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Barclay :
To ask the Leader of the House,
whether it is the intention of the Government to introduce legislation to
amend the laws dealing with thirdparty insurance, in order to make it
compulsory for all insurance firms to
contribute to a pool from which
hospitals and
and victims of non-stop
motorists could be compensated.
Mr. BARCLAY: My object in asking
this question is to see if it would be possible to have the law regarding thirdparty motor insurance altered so that
persons who are injured by motorists who
do not stop, and hospitals which treat the
injured persons, may be properly compensated. My reason for putting down this
question is because I read in the Press
some time ago of a working man who was
going home from his work when he was
overtaken by a motor-car driven by the
type of driver I have described in my
question as a non-stop motorist. This car
struck the man, and he was knocked to
the ground. The motorist drove on
and did not stop to give any assistance to the man, who was subsequently found by a passer-by. He was
taken to hospital, where they did everything possible to patch him up, but he
was found to be suffering from a
fractured spine. After he had been in
hospital for a considerable time they
sent him home to his wife and family,
and he is to-day lying on his back, with
a wife and five children dependent upon
him. He has received no compensation,
because the person who knocked him
down could not be found.
This poor man tried to get National
Health Insurance benefit, but failed to
do so.
He then tried to get unemployment benefit, but the authorities said it
was a case for compensation, and that
therefore he was not legally entitled to
either National Health Insurance or unemployment benefit. There he is, lying
helpless in bed with a fractured spine,
Oral Answers
Ancient Monuments Bill
22 APRIL 1937
and with a wife and five children. As
the law stands at present the motorist
who knocks a person down on the road
must be found before the injured person
can claim compensation. My suggestion
is that the law should be altered so that
insurance companies would be compelled.
to contribute to a pool, so that if a
motor driver, who knocked down and injured a person got away, there would be
a fund from which to compensate the
injured party, or his dependents if he
was killed, and also the hospital which
rendered its services to such a person.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): I am informed by my right
hon. Friend, the Minister of Home
Affairs, that it is not the intention of the
Government to promote any legislation
on
the lines suggested by the hon.
Member.
Parliament took steps in the Motor
Vehicles and Road Traffic Act of 1930,
as amended by the Act of 1934, to ensure that as far as is possible persons
injured by motor vehicles will be compensated, and an obligation was placed
on insurance companies to pay expenses
reasonably incurred by hospitals in
affording treatment to persons injured by
motor vehicles on public roads, the
maximum amount payable in respect of
any person being £50.
expenses
These enactments afford a valuable
measure of assistance to the public and
to hospitals, and it is not clear that any
justification exists for transferring to
motorists as a body, through the insurance companies, the responsibility for
compensating the injured, and defraying
the
of treating persons injured
by motorists who fail to stop when they
are involved in accidents. Such cases, I
am glad to say, are rare in Northern
Ireland, though the case mentioned by
the hon. Member is an exceedingly sad
one. But my right hon. Friend, the
Minister of Home Affairs, fears that
legislation of the kind suggested in the
question might lead to fraudulent
claims being made.

the Lord Mayor of Belfast, mayors, chair-

Measure is intended solely to avoid the

Question to Minister.
STATISTICS OF MOTOR
ACCIDENTS.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the number of accidents
caused by motor vehicles in Northern
Ireland have increased or decreased
during the period 1934, to the present
date; what proportion these accidents
bear to the number of motor vehicles
licensed during this period; and how
these figures compare with similar
figures for the same period in England during which the use of pedestrian
crossings and the thirty-mile limit in
built-up areas have been widely
developed in the latter country.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I put down
the question standing in my name not
out of any idle curiosity but because, in
common with most people, I have been
very seriously concerned about the
growth of motor accidents in recent
years. Various experiments have taken
place in England towards the regulation
of road traffic, and those experiments
have now reached a point when the
results in England, and the results in
this country, where most of the English
experiments have not been tried, might
be compared and might possibly afford
valuable data with regard to our future
policy towards road traffic. Briefly, in
England the intensive campaign started
by the present Minister of Transport
consists of five factors, namely, (1) road
improvement; (2) intensive propaganda
of "Safety First"; (3) limitation of
speed in built-up areas; (4) examination
of drivers as to efficiency before
driving licence is issued; (5) that lavish
institution known as pedestrian crossings.
a
In this country, while there has been
a propaganda of "Safety First," and
road improvement has taken place, the
other experiments tried in England have
not been adopted here. Therefore, if
we find that our record in the North of
Ireland is as good as or is better
than the English record it will be a
strong presumption that the experiments
in England have not been successful, and
therefore we need waste no time considering them. If, on the contrary, our
record is worse than that of England
it raises a very strong presumption that
the English experiments have been
successful, and that very careful consideration should be given to their
adoption here. I use the word "presumption" advisedly because, of course,
there are other factors which enter inte
the problem and which are not so easily
comparable, but, on the whole, I think
there will be a very strong presumption
one way or the other.
Oral Answers
Local Government Bill
27 APRIL 1937
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): The number of accidents
caused by motor vehicles and the number
of vehicles licensed in Northern Ireland
during the three years 1934, 1935 and
1936 were
follows:-
as
-1934
accidents; 39,800 motor vehicles.
671 accidents; 41,900 motor vehicles.
1936 - 665 accidents; 46,900 motor
vehicles.

1935-
The hon. Senator can see from these
figures that although the number of
accidents has increased the increase has
not been proportional to the increase in
the number of motor vehicles. During
the year 1934 for every 1,000 vehicles
licensed 15.3 accidents were reported. In
the year 1935 the number of accidents
rose to 16 for each 1,000 vehicles, but
during the year 1936 the proportion was
only 14.2 for each 1,000.
I regret that I am not able to give the
hon. Senator comparable figures for
Great Britain, as the statistics published
do not show the number of accidents
caused by motor vehicles.
The figures published for Great Britain.
give the number of accidents attributable
to motor vehicles, but it seems clear that,
in addition to accidents caused by motor
vehicles, they include all accidents in
which motor vehicles were involved,
irrespective of whether the accidents were
caused by pedestrians or motor vehicles.
They are as follow:-1934 144,709
accidents; 2,405,390 motor vehicles
licensed-60.1 accidents per 1,000 vehicles.
1935-132,339 accidents; 2,511,000 motor
vehicles licensed-52.7 accidents per 1,000
vehicles. 1936 134,085 accidents;
2,706,560 motor vehicles licensed-49.5
accidents per 1,000 vehicles.

Question to Minister.
PHYSICAL FITNESS CAMPAIGN.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is the intention of the
Government to take measures to
improve the physical fitness of the
people on similar lines to those taken
by the Imperial Government.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
noticed some time ago a report in the
English Press about a physical fitness
campaign that had been instituted there,
and to which the British Government has
made a grant. Apparently nothing is
being done on this side to similarly
improve the physical condition of the
people here. Indeed, I have been waiting
to see if the "Justice for Ulster" Committee would take some steps to see that
we had an opportunity of deriving some.
benefit from the scheme, but up to the
present nothing in that direction seems
to have been done. I, therefore, put this
question on the Paper to see if the noble
Leader of the House could give us any
information on the subject. Everyone
will admit that we have a splendid type
of people in Northern Ireland, and
personally I feel very proud of them, and
if anything can be done by the Government to improve their physical fitness it
should be done.
Undoubtedly the Government has
already done a great deal for the social
wellbeing of the people of Northern
Ireland. They have passed legislation
for the preservation of bird life, and for
the improvement of cattle and horses;
indeed, we have a Bill coming before us
to-day for the improvement of horsebreeding. When the Government show
such an interest in these things surely
they will agree that the welfare and
fitness of human beings deserves some consideration. I know that much has been
29 APRIL 1937
Oral Answers

Petroleum Bill
Affairs as to the present position in
Northern Ireland in respect of facilities
for physical training. This information,
when available, will be carefully studied
with a view to considering the best
measures to take to meet the needs of the
Province.
done to combat tuberculosis in the
Province and to provide proper housing
accommodation for the people, so that it
will no longer be necessary for them to
live in slums. There is a great deal to the
credit of the Government in that respect,
but I think much more could be done.
For instance, the Government might
introduce legislation to make compulsory
the separation of those suffering from
tuberculosis from other members of the
household. In the
poorer districts one
very often sees a person in an advanced
state of consumption living in a small
home with children and other adults, and
in my opinion persons suffering from
tuberculosis should be compulsorily
separated from other members of the
family.
There is another terrible disease which
is not very often mentioned, and that is
venereal disease. While consumption is a
terrible disease I believe the other is much
more serious. It is not the sufferer alone
who has to be taken into consideration,
but it is the serious effect the disease has
on the off-spring. We have in Belfast a
considerable number of cases of blindness,
epilepsy, and Bright's disease, which I
believe are largely caused by the disease
I have referred to. I do not know what
the Government can do in this matter,
but if any steps could be taken to
minimise this scourge it would be of
tremendous benefit to the community. I
bring this question before the House in
order to find out, if I can, what the
Government intend to do. As a physical
fitness scheme is being launched on the
other side of the Channel I think something similar should be done for the
people of Northern Ireland. I sometimes
read in the Press of murders, kidnappings,
assaults, and other crimes that occur on
the other side of the water, and we know
how clear we are of such crimes in this
country. Therefore, we are entitled to
be very proud of our people, and anything
that can be done for their benefit and to
improve their physical fitness should be
done very readily by the Government.

Petroleum (Transfer of Licences) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The provisions of this Bill are similar to those
of a Measure recently enacted at Westminster. Its purpose is to enable local
authorities and my right hon. Friend, the
Minister of Home Affairs, to transfer
licences to store petroleum spirit upon
payment of a fee of two shillings and
sixpence.
the
The present position is that, by oversight, the existing enactments concerning the issue of licences for the storage
of petroleum spirit do not provide for the
transfer of such licences when
premises in respect of which they are
issued change their owners. As a result,
the annual licence fee prescribed has been
paid in respect of some premises twice in
the course of a few months. I beg to
move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
