Arbitration Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Viscount CHARLEMONT: If hon.
Members will look at the memorandum
affixed to the Bill they will see that the
law governing arbitration in Northern
Ireland is laid down in three Statutes,
the most recent of which was passed over
80 years ago. I may add that the
Common Law Procedure Act of 1856 was
limited in scope and is not availed of to
any great extent. In England it became
necessary, owing to the great increase in
recourse to arbitration, due to the rapid
expansion of commerce, to provide better
facilities for arbitration as early as 1889,
was
when the first general Arbitration Act
passed. The provisions of the
Arbitration Act of 1934 were the result
of a detailed inquiry made by the
McKinnon Commission. That Act is,
however, merely an amending Act.
The present Bill is based upon these
two Acts. Hon. Members will appreciate
that it is most desirable that the law here
should be on all fours with the law in
England, in view of the importance of
our commercial intercourse with that
country. This is, perhaps, particularly
so in the case of insurance, as commercial
policies generally include a Clause to the
effect that should a dispute arise it shall,
in the first place, be submitted to arbitration, which means in practice, arbitration
under Acts between 250 and 80 years old,
a fact which has its reaction in the
amount of the premiums payable.
Furthermore, the correspondence of the
law in both countries will make available
to our courts the benefit of the decisions
of English courts, and will enable
students and practitioners of law here to
make use of English text books on
arbitration.
This is a purely legal Bill, and the
Clauses are technical. It is, as I have
explained, a codification of the English
Acts of 1889 and 1934. It has been
approved by the
the Incorporated Law
Society and by the Council of the Bar of
Northern Ireland, and in these circumstances I do not think that hon. Members
will desire me to explain its provisions in
detail. I should, however, like to
mention one or two points. If hon.
Members will look at Clause 1 they will
see that sub-section (2) provides that an
arbitration agreement shall, unless
contrary intention is expressed in the
agreement, be deemed to include the provisions set out in the First Schedule to
the Bill. This will obviate the necessity
of setting out all these provisions in the
agreement. It will be sufficient for the
parties to agree to refer the matter to
arbitration, and the provisions will automatically apply.
a
I should also like to draw attention to
the fact that under existing law in
Northern Ireland the only way in which
a disputed question of law can be decided
when a case is submitted to arbitration is
by stating the whole of the award as a
special case which goes to the courts after
Arbitration Bill-
Second Reading
29 APRIL 1937
the award is made up. The Measure now
before this House will enable the
arbitrator to state any one point for the
court in his final award, or to state an
interim case for the decision of the court.
Another improvement of system effected
by the Bill is that whereas at present, in
the absence of a written agreement to
the contrary, both parties must pay their
own costs, the present Bill provides that
an arbitrator shall have power to give
costs, together with a provision for the
taxation of those costs and a proper
method of enforcing awards.
I am sure the Bill will commend itself
to hon. Members as a useful Measure
calculated to benefit the commercial community. I beg to move, "That the Bill
be now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: It is really very difficult, especially, perhaps, after listening
to the noble Viscount, to add anything
of real value to the admirably brief but
extremely effective synopsis of the
Measure which appears on the cover of
the Bill before us. Nevertheless, it
seems to me that the Bill is one of such
importance that it would be unbecoming
of this House to pass it sub silentio, and
without having some little regard to the
provisions of the various Clauses of the
Bill. The Bill itself does not, to my
mind, contain anything in any way
revolutionary. It is directed to the
regularisation and simplification of an
existing practice which has grown up
amongst us during the last century or so
and has thriven, one may say, with increasing vigour as the years went by.
Accordingly this Bill will, as the noble
Viscount hinted just now, provide something in the nature of a remedy for
something more than an inconvenience
which had been experienced for years,
admitted on all hands, and at times
rather bitterly complained about.
I do not myself believe for a moment
that the preference which exists to-day,
particularly perhaps in the realms of
commerce, for the settlement of difficulties by arbitration rather than directly
by the law courts owes its origin to any
lack of confidence in His Majesty's
Judges, whether judges of the High
Court or of less authority, but I do think
that there may be some mistrust of
juries. I do not mean anv reflection on
the integrity of the individual juryman;
that is not what is in my mind at all,
but I do think there is a feeling that the
average citizen is not always fully
capable of dealing satisfactorily with
matters in dispute which bristle very
often with technicalities entirely outside
the ken of ordinary people. Hence it
follows that people in dispute one with
the other look for some other method of
settling their differences.
Looking through the Bill, I cannot
find any trace anywhere that the
supremacy, even the omnipotence, of the
law has been in any way abated, but I do
find that while that has been maintained
there is a distinct recognition, and more
perhaps than a recognition-a distinct
preservation and even encouragement of
the exercise of the right to resort to some
extracurial tribunal.
The key-note of the Bill is development
by way of application, and again I am
almost using the same words as the noble
Viscount when he spoke of the development of the English Acts of 1889 and
1934. He also reminded us that the first
Statute we had in Ireland dealing with
the subject was one of the time of William
III in 1698, and it seems to me that the
opening words of that Statute so clearly
exemplify or illustrate what it is that is
required to be done that I am going to
presume to read to the House a short
passage from the commencement of that
Act. The Act begins as follows:-
"Whereas it hath been found by
experience that reference made by
rule of court hath contributed much
to the ease of the subject in determining controversies, because the parties
become thereby obliged to perform the
award of arbitrators chosen by themselves, under the penalty of imprisonment for their contempt, in case they
refuse to perform the same: Now for
promoting trade, and rendering the
awards of arbitrators the more effective
in all cases, for the final determination
of controversies referred to them by
merchants and traders, or others, containing matters of account or trade, or
other matters, be it enacted," and so
forth.
I venture to suggest that that passage
leads us to see exactly what is to be the
key-note of further legislation such as
that before us; that is to say, the
elimination of that Rule of Court which
occupies the forefront of the old Act and
also its alternative, that is to say, the
Arbitration Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
elaborate specification in every instrument of agreement to arbitration of the
various details. In fact, the development
proceeds and becomes merged in the
Statutes that have gone before, bringing
those Statutes into consonance with
modern desires. The method of application is arrived at by welding together the
English Acts of 1889 and 1934, and by
adding certain necessary ingredients.
The outstanding feature of the Act of
1889 was the sub-section which the noble
Viscount read; that is the sub-section
providing that every instrument of
arbitration-the expression "submission"
was used in the English Acts—should be
deemed to contain certain fundamental
provisions set out in the Schedule to the
Act. That provision is reproduced in the
Bill before us. The effect is to give
Statutory force and sanction to the inclusion in every agreement for arbitration of the fundamental principles laid
down in the Schedule. In our case it
will be the Schedule to our own Act,
which is made up by the old English
Schedule as amended by the later one.
At the same time liberty was given in
England in 1889, and will be continued
here, for the parties, if they so desire,
to eliminate from their particular
arbitration any conditions to which one
or other of them for some reason or other
objects.
It is, in fact, interesting to recall that
this method of legislation really owes its
origin to an Ulster jurist, because it was
the first Lord Cairns who introduced the
principle in the great Conveyancing Act
of 1871. Nearly all hon. Members who
have much concern with real property
will remember the awful expressions
dealing with "Right to convey", "Quiet
enjoyment", "Freedom from encumbrance", and "Further assurance",
which gave rise to very great prolixity
and extensive annoyance. These
swept away by the great statesman I have
mentioned when he provided in the Act
I have quoted that the words "beneficial
owner" should cover all those things.
That is an outstanding example of legislative sanction to implication.
welding together I have spoken of has
been very meticulously and skilfully
done in the Bill, and none of the other
necessary ingredients seem to be left out.
were
The
I am going to give the House two
examples, because it is of some importance that we should appreciate in this
House something of the way in which
Bills which come before us are, so to
speak, manufactured. I think it is also
desirable that those whom we indirectly
represent should understand that Bills
that pass through this House do not
pass through without something in the
nature of careful examination. I will
ask the House first to look at Clause
7 of the Bill before us. That is the
Clause which, as a matter of fact, is
based on Section 11 of the Act of 1889.
The House will see in lines 23 and 26
the three little words, "or the proceedings" occur. Those three words are not
in Section 11 of the Act of 1889. They
were added in the amending Act of
1934, and from there they have been
exhumed and fitted in their proper
place. That is what I venture to
describe as an example of welding.
The other example is something
rather different. I am taking the
second sub-clause of Clause 29 as my
example. It will be found on page 14.
That particular sub-clause is one of very
great importance, but I might ask the
House to reflect for a minute how very
easy it would have been to forget to
put it in!
One of the things which that
sub-clause effects is, as the hon. Senators
on my left will remember, to save the life
of that provision for arbitration which
occurs in the Unemployment Insurance
Consolidation Act which we passed last
autumn. That is one of the things that
sub-clause will do. Others I need not
mention.
I should venture to think that the Bill
will not have a very difficult passage in
Committee. I am afraid that I have
myself some few criticisms to offer, but
they are only four in number. In fact,
in one case, at all events, it is an observation rather than a criticism. As time
is short, more especially if the noble
Viscount wishes-as he may very well
wish and be able to do-to get the Bill
despatched through this House by next
Tuesday, I may ask leave to occupy a
few moments in indicating very briefly
what those criticisms will be. There are
three places in the Bill where I think
the English Act has been followed too
closely, and there is one place where
Arbitration Bill

be a
apparently—and I desire to speak very
guardedly about this one case, because
there
may
reason for the course
taken which has escaped my notice or
my research, but if a novel course has
been taken, as I am afraid it has, it is
a course I do not like.
found
by
That instance will be
reference to Clause 6, page 4, in the
proviso to Clause 6. The words "paragraph (b)" occur. That is to say, the
proviso is limited to paragraph (b), and
that would have the effect of restricting
the court from exercising the power as
regards paragraph (a) which is given as
regards paragraph (b). The court is not
so limited in the English Act of 1889,
nor in the Irish Civil Procedure Act
of 1856, if I read it correctly, is the
court so limited. Consequently I will
direct attention again to that matter in
Committee. As I say, there may be a
reason for it, but if there is I think we
require to be told what it is. The principle involved in that particular point
is of some importance. The practical
importance seems to be almost nil, because it is not a case that is ever likely
to arise. Still, the principle remains.
The next thing I want to ask about
is why it was decided that this Bill
should come into operation on a fixed
date? Why was that course taken
rather than the other of adopting "the
appointed day" procedure. I should
have been inclined to think that adopting the "appointed day" procedure
would have saved us the little awkwardness
which hon. Members will have
noticed when they compared the third
sub-clause of Clause 20 with the last two
items in the Fourth Schedule to the Bill.
There is a slight awkwardness there, but
it is inevitable if you maintain a fixed
date for commencement. It also seems
to me there might perhaps be some little
difficulty with Clause 25, which also
deals ith Rules of Court, but that is
purely a matter of machinery, and I do
not propose to deal with it again. There
may be a reason which we shall be told
later.
Further on in the Bill we find a
reference to perjury, with arrangements
for penalty and so on. I shall ask the
House in Committee to follow the course
we adopted when the Companies Consolidation Act of 1932 was before us,
and for the reasons I then gave I shall
ask the Committee to adopt the same
phraseology again. When we come to
the thirtieth Clause I am going to ask
that we may give the final order of the
boot to the word "submit" and insert
the word "refer." As to that, I think
that Mr. Attorney, speaking in another
place, has to some extent anticipated
me, and for this afternoon I will lay aside
my microscope.
Taking the Bill as a whole, I say it
will not only amend our law, but it will
give us uniformity with England, and it
will give us that uniformity in a consolidated shape, an advantage which
England does not at present enjoy. I
have great pleasure in supporting the
Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Mr. QUIGLEY: In connection with
this question I should like to say that we
all admire the splendid work of our
Ministry of Education. We have
system of education of which we
proud, and our thanks are certainly due
to the Education Minister. I am afraid,
however, there is one direction in which
we are lacking, and that is in the
department of domestic economy. I
understand that in England, Scotland
and Wales the girls in the national
schools are taught domestic economy by
specialised teachers appointed by the local
education authorities, and I cannot see
why some such arrangement could not be
carried out in Northern Ireland. Surely
it is most important that girls should be
given instruction in such subjects,
because in the course of time they will be
wives and mothers, and you all know
what a difference it makes if the wife is
a good cook and household manager.
Belfast we have a well-equipped domestic
economy training college in connection.
with the Municipal College of Technology.
It is turning out many teachers every
year, and it is sad to think that the
majority of those young ladies have to go
across the water to find situations. Their
services are, therefore, lost to us when they
might be utilised to great advantage. I
hope the Leader of the House will take
this matter into serious consideration and
see if anything can be done to give our
girls the same advantages in this respect
as those enjoyed by pupils in Great
Britain.
In
Mr. LONG: Domestic economy is a
compulsory subject in public elementary
schools in Northern Ireland (as I believe
is also the case in Great Britain) and is
taught in every school except where the
number of girls eligible to receive the
instruction is so small that a class cannot be formed, or where the accommodation is entirely unsuitable. The teachers
giving the instruction in all cases hold
qualifications which are regarded by the
Ministry as sufficient for the purpose.
The subject is included in the curriculum
for women students at Stranmillis and
St. Mary's Training Colleges, Belfast,
and a student who is successful in the
course provided at either of these colleges
is regarded as fully qualified to give the
instruction in a public elementary school.
There are also in many centres alternative arrangements whereby domestic
economy teachers, normally engaged in
teaching technical school classes, may
further be employed in giving instruction
to public elementary school pupils, and
the Ministry makes a special grant to the
local education authorities in respect of
such instruction; in the session 1935-36,
5,778 girls in 158 public elementary
schools received instruction under these
latter
Such
arrangements.
teachers
invariably hold the teaching diploma of
a recognised domestic economy training
college.
In addition, the Ministry is prepared
to recognise, in what is defined as a
"large" public elementary school, in
which the amount of instruction in
domestic economy and needlework is
sufficient to occupy the full time of a
teacher, the appointment of an assistant
teacher on the ordinary full-time staff of
that school who holds the teaching
diploma of a recognised domestic economy
training college, but this provision has
not yet been availed of by the school
managers concerned.
Mr. QUIGLEY: Arising out of that
answer, may I ask the Parliamentary
Secretary whether he does not consider
that specialised teachers in domestic subjects should be appointed to take charge,
say, of several schools, thus placing our
schools in line with those across the
water. We all know that the ordinary
elementary school teacher is not
specialised and does not hold a diploma
in domestic subjects.
Mr. LESLIE: Further arising out of
that, may I ask if there is any proposal
to provide funds for public elementary
schools in country districts, under private
management, which would enable those
schools to incur the expense of employing
the super-qualified teachers to which the
hon. Senator has referred?
Mr. LONG: I am not in a position to
answer these two supplementary questions,
but I will make inquiries into the matter.

THE SENATE
FIRST AID AND AIR RAID
PRECAUTIONS.
The following question also stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Quigley:
To ask the Leader of the House whether
the Government will consider the advisability of including in the curriculum of
public elementary schools in Northern
Ireland instruction in first aid and air
raid precautions, so that the pupils may
become qualified to render assistance in
cases of accidents and air attack.
Mr. QUIGLEY: I think all the Members of this House will recognise that
ability to render first aid is most
important. In many cases of accident,
especially drowning, a knowledge of such
aid might be the means of saving
valuable life. I know that there are
classes in the city for instruction in
first aid, and a great many people take
advantage of these classes. Girl Guides,
Boy Scouts and members of the Boys'
Brigade, are given such instruction, and
we must acknowledge the services so
freely and willingly given by our medical.
men in this direction.
You all know the keen interest taken
in the subject by Senator Dr. Gibson,
and we were all delighted to see that he
was recently appointed director for
Northern Ireland of the British Red
Cross Society and Order of St. John of
Jerusalem in succession to the late Sir
Robert Kennedy. If arrangements were
made whereby the senior pupils, both
boys and girls in our public elementary
schools could be taught and qualify in
first aid it would be of service to the
community generally as well as benefit
to themselves. Then in the case of air
raids-and we have all been told of the
dreadful happenings in Spain-it is very
necessary that our young people should
be able to take precautions and render
assistance where required.
Mr. LONG: In reply to the hon.
Member, I may say that some training
in first aid is given in public elementary
schools in connection with the programme of instruction in hygiene.
The whole question of educating the
public on the subject of air raid precautions is at present receiving the careful
consideration of the Government and the
suggestion made by the hon. Member
will not be lost sight of.
I may, however, add that many people
feel that it is not altogether desirable to
present to children of tender years too
vivid an appreciation of the horrors of
war. I mention this to show that the
question is a debatable one which cannot be decided lightly.

Law Reform (Miscellaneous Provisions)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The purpose of this Bill
is to effect in Northern Ireland some
important reforms of law made recently
in England. Hon. Members will remember that when moving the Second Reading of the Arbitration Bill in this House
a few days ago the noble Viscount made
reference to certain important general
advantages which follow from the
similarity of law here to the law in
England. Those considerations apply
equally to the Measure now before the
House.
By the first part of this Bill it is proposed to enable the courts when winding
up an estate to deal with real estate in
a similar manner as if it were personal
property. In the absence of such provision in our law considerable delays and
heavy expense have often been incurred by
persons interested in the winding up of
estates. These provisions are similar to
those which were enacted by the English
Land Transfer Act of 1897.
The second part of the Bill deals with
the legal rights and liabilities of married
women and of husbands. Briefly, it puts
a married woman in the same position as
an unmarried woman as regards the control of her property and responsibility
for her debts, and it relieves married
of responsibility for debts contracted, or wrongs committed by their
wives. This part of the Bill follows an
English Law Reform Act of 1935.
men
The third part of the Bill is based
upon an English Law Reform Act of
1934 and deals with the effect of death
on certain causes of action.
By Clause 14 in the third part of the
Bill it is proposed to amend the law so
that if a man dies any subsisting right
of action, except those mentioned in the
proviso to sub-section (1), which he
could have brought in his lifetime will
survive him and it will be possible to
carry on that right of action for the
benefit of his estate. Similar actions can
also be continued against his personal
representative by persons to whom he
would have been liable if alive.
Clause 15, the remaining Clause in
this part of the Bill, enables illegitimate
and adopted children who can prove
dependency to benefit under the Fatal
Accidents Act, 1846 to 1908.
The fourth and last part of the Bill
deals with amendments of the law
relating to tort-feasors, that is, doers of
actionable wrongs, and with the law
relating to interest on debts and
damages. This, like the second part of
the Bill, follows the English Act of 1935.
The position under existing law is that
if a number of persons commit a tort
against another person, he must sue them
Law Reform Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
"
[Mr. Long]
them
together. He cannot sue
separately, and sometimes, as a consequence, the most substantial of the
wrongdoers escape action as their names
are not known when the suit is first filed.
One of the provisions of Clause 16 of the
Bill is that a judgment recovered against
one wrongdoer will not prevent action
against others who are equally liable.
The Clause also provides that a person
held liable together with other persons
in respect of damage will be able to
share the damages and costs awarded
equally with the other persons. The
present position is that a successful
plaintiff with a grudge can levy the
whole sum awarded him against one of
the defendants. Hon. Members
agree with me that the Amendment proposed is a very proper one.
will
Clause 17 is to enable courts of record
to order that interest shall be payable on
amounts due as debts or awarded as
damages. This provision is, I think, very
necessary because the ultimate judgment
may not be given until long after the
time from which a successful plaintiff is
entitled to be paid.
The Bill is somewhat technical in
character, but I hope that I have
succeeded in showing that the changes in
law which it is designed to achieve are
necessary improvements. I beg to move,
"That this Bill be now read a Second
- time."
Mr. LESLIE: I think that everybody
who has spoken on this Bill either in
another place or outside these Houses
altogether has said that this is essentially
a lawyers' Bill. I think I myself would
feel inclined to go further and say it is
a Bill for practising counsel and practising solicitors whose occupation lies in
Northern Ireland. I cannot but regret
that we have to proceed on an examination of the Bill this afternoon without
the assistance of an hon. Senator who
usually sits on my left when he is here
and who is a member of the Incorporated
Law Society of Northern Ireland. But I
believe I am right in saying that that
Society has already expressed its approval
of the Bill before us.
I cannot myself claim to have
made an exhaustive examination of
this particular Bill, but I may say
I have satisfied myself that not
only, as the Parliamentary Secretary
has told us, is it based on the English
Measures which are referred to in the
memorandum outside the Bill, but I have
also been able to identify in every case
the actual Clauses in the actual English
Acts which have given rise to the reproduction now before us.
I do not think there will be any desire
to examine this Bill very closely in Committee, but from questions that have
been put to me from outside, from people
who, perhaps, do not belong to the class
I referred to as those to whom the Bill
was of particular interest-from those
questions I think it will be as well to
ask, for the benefit of those people-not
for the benefit of any individual Member
of this House-three or four categorical
questions which I would ask the Parliamentary Secretary to answer at a later
stage. Whatever information he will
give will be of interest to the public who,
perhaps, will not have gleaned it or been
able to glean it for themselves from the
Bill.
The first of these little matters will
be found on the second page. I think
that in the second Clause, that is the
Clause dealing with the new arrangement for the devolution of real property,
the public will like to be told whether
they can rely upon it that no more
expense in the way of stamps or charges
will be laid upon the ultimate recipient
of that property.
Hon. Members will
find exemption from death duties
expressly set out, but the question of
stamps on conveyances and legal charges
is one upon which the public would like
to be assured.
The second point arises on page 6,
and I think again the same people will
want to be assured that there is nothing
in Clause 10 which will interfere with
what I might call prospective settlement;
that is the provision in a marriage.
settlement which has the effect of passing
after-acquired property to the trustees
of the settlement. The public will like
to be assured that there will be no
alteration as regards that matter. I
imagine it is a matter most people have
had experience of one way or another.
The next question is on page 7. I
think the House will agree that it is
almost unnecessary to put it, but the
question has been asked whether a will
is an instrument. It is, I think, not for
Law Reform Bill-
Second Reading
4 MAY 1937
The
me to answer that question.
remaining point is, I also think, rather
plainly dealt with in the Bill itself, but
the Bill will not be in the hands of the
public. The public will want to know
whether the provision in paragraph (c),
of Clause 16, as regards what I might
describe as the joint liability of joint
tort-feasors, really does apply and is
intended to apply to crime. The celebrated case of the two highwaymen will
be quoted by people who do not remember that it arose out of alleged contract
and not out of tort at all, but I think
something definite should be said outside
the Bill itself as to whether that particular paragraph does apply to crime.
There are two things in the Bill I
do not myself like very much. There
is one thing that is not in the Bill that
I should like to have seen there, and
that is an arrangement for interchangeability of Probate as between England
and Northern Ireland. I suppose one
will be told that that is outside the scope
of the Bill, but, after all, this is a
Miscellaneous Provisions Bill and I
think if such a change could be brought
about it would be welcomed by the
people of Northern Ireland.
I am not to be taken as an enthusiastic supporter of Clause 14. That relates
to the survival of an action after death,
and the reason I am not very enthusiastic
about it is because it has always seemed to
me that when a man is dead he is no
longer in a position to give evidence, and
therefore the court hearing the case is
deprived of the assistance of one who
might be a most material witness. On
the other hand, I do not think that
would be a sufficient reason to ask the
House to disagree with what has been
established in England, especially in
view of the safeguards provided in the
Bill.
I do not like the fourth sub-clause of
Clause 15, but I recognise it is impossible to make any other arrangement.
That is the Clause which prevents the
benefit of an amendment of the Fatal
Accidents Act being experienced until
1st January next, the date when the
Bill is to come into operation. It is
obvious, however much one may regret
the delay, that it is absolutely inevitable.
The only amendment I shall bring before
the House in Committee relates to a
small matter in Clause 10, paragraph (c)
of sub-section (3). Having regard to the
fact that a similar provision in the English law will come into operation in 1945,
I shall suggest we should adopt the same
date. I am going to say, in fact, that
there is no particular magic in ten years'
interval, and, of the two alternatives, it
would be better to reduce the ten years to
eight and follow the English Act. Taking
the Bill as a whole, it should be supported,
and it will receive something in the
nature of acclamation rather than
merely qualified approval.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
Advertisements Regulation Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: I think hon. Members will agree that the objects of this
Bill are unexceptionable. They are,
briefly, to increase the powers of local
authorities, firstly, to prevent danger to
the public from advertisement hoardings, and, secondly, to enable them to
preserve the countryside from disfigurement by such hoardings and by advertisements affixed to trees. As the law
stands at present, local authorities are
able to make bylaws to regulate the
erection of hoardings over 12 feet high,
and to prevent the exhibition of advertisements in such a manner as to affect
adversely the amenities of public parks
and pleasure grounds, or to disfigure
the beauty of a landscape.
It is proposed by the Bill now before
the House to extend these powers to
enable local authorities to control all
hoardings and to prohibit in certain
areas the exhibition of any advertisements whatsoever. The only areas in
respect of which such prohibition could
be effected would be of a kind that it
could reasonably be held that any advertisement in them would constitute
disfigurement. As a safeguard, the
approval of the Ministry of Home
Affairs would first have to be obtained.
a
By sub-section 5 of paragraph 1 it is
intended to exclude from the operations
of bylaws made by local authorities under
the Bill the properties of railways and,
except where such properties are within
areas administered by rural district
councils, the properties of harbour, dock
Law Reform Bill-

Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
and canal undertakings.
This is in
accordance with the existing Acts.
It is proposed that in county boroughs,
and
boroughs,
urban districts the
councils will be the only local authorities
concerned with the administration of the
Bill. In the counties the county council
is authorised to delegate its functions to
rural district councils, although it would
at any time be able to withdraw delegations so made.
Should hoardings be erected or advertisements exhibited in any
area where
they are prohibited by bylaw, the local
authority may
require the owner to
remove them on fourteen days' notice,
and may remove them itself should he
not do so. The maximum penalty for
an offence against a bylaw is £5, with
an addition of 40s for each day the
offence continues. This is also the
penalty under the existing Act of 1907.
To avoid hardship on owners of hoardings and on advertisers, local authorities
are required to exempt for three years
from the operation of bylaws any hoardings in existence at the time the bylaws
are made, and are also required to pay
reasonable compensation for any loss.
sustained through their operation.

Mr. LESLIE: The Parliamentary
Secretary stated at the outset of his
introductory speech that this was a Bill
which would be received with practically
universal sympathy. With that I
entirely concur. A Bill of this sort has
been asked for by county councils and
private persons for a long time, and now
the Bill is before us. But I think the
question we have to consider is whether
the Bill before us to-day is the Bill we
want, and if not, whether it can be made
what we want by means of amendment
in the ordinary way, or whether it would
be better to bring in a somewhat different
Bill. As to that, we shall probably get
light during the course of the debate,
and for the convenience of debate I will
move that the word "now" be omitted
from the Parliamentary Secretary's
Motion, and the words "this day six
months" added at the end. I will do
that for the convenience of debate in order
that the House may have an opportunity
of considering the Bill rather perhaps in
detail, and of forming an opinion as to
whether any delay with the Measure is
likely to compensate for, in fact, itself.
That is to say, whether it will be better
to proceed by amendment or by means of
a new Bill.
I am bound to say I think that the Bill
itself is rather difficult of understanding. If it is difficult to us, with the
Debates of another place before us, and
with the two English Statutes of 1907
and 1925 at our elbows, how much more
difficult is it likely to be for the county
councils and rural district councils, who
will have to administer it? I say, "have
to administer it," but that is, perhaps,
too strong a term, for they are not bound
to apply the Bill, and I am afraid I do
not think any local body will apply a
Bill which it does not itself thoroughly
understand. I should venture to suggest that the primary fault in the Bill
before us is the apparent forgetfulness
of the fact that the Bill is a consolidation,
not a development. In England the Act
of 1925 is a graft on the original Act
of 1907. Here it is proposed to repeal
the Act of 1907 and to re-enact it,
slightly altered, no doubt, with additional
provisions, but in doing that we have lost
what they get in England, because they
proceed by graft, and that is the word
"exhibition" in the Title. It may be
more easy in England to separate in one's
mind the two components, the advertisement itself and the hoarding or what-not
on which the advertisement is erected.
That seems to me to be our great
difficulty.
The Bill provides a definition for
advertisements, which is taken from the
later of the English Acts, and provides.
that it includes hoardings, but it does not
give much help as to what it is that constitutes an advertisement. I think we
shall find those two questions of the
hoarding which is to be erected and the
advertisement which is to be exhibited
keep running into each other, so as to
produce some temporary confusion to
such an extent that reference to the context does not always get us out of trouble.
Whatever we do we must get the expression "exhibition" into the Title, and
we must be very careful as to how we
proceed with "erections" throughout the
Bill. I am almost inclined to think it
might be better to give a definition of
"exhibiting an advertisement" as includ-
153 Advertisements Regulation Bill-

Second Reading
ing the hoarding on which it is displayed,
and leave out the reference to erections,
except in the first sub-clause of the first
Clause.
The Bill, if I may say so, has experienced somewhat severe treatment in
another place, and I am not altogether
sure that it has been very greatly
improved by the treatment it experienced.
In fact, I am afraid that in one place,
about the middle of the first Clause,
something resembling chaos has been
produced. That I will deal with when I
come to it. Now, as I have actually
moved what amounts to the postponement of the Bill, although I desire to
reserve my right to retreat if driven to
it, I will run through the Bill very
quickly to show what it is I object to
and what it is that would seem to justify
withdrawal and substitution.
were
We begin with sub-clause (1) of Clause
1. When this Bill was introduced into
another place the words, "for the purpose
of preventing danger to the public,"
were not there. Those words
inserted on the motion of the Minister
of Home Affairs himself, because he
pointed out that there was no explanation of the object of the Clause and no
indication of what was intended. I am
afraid I think that in making the
alteration in order to avoid one difficulty
the Bill was run into another. As the
words are before us to-day, "for the purpose of preventing danger to the public,'
we are left in doubt as to whether the
danger is to come from the structural
inefficiency of the hoarding-that, I
believe, is the intention the right hon.
Gentleman had in his mind- or whether
it is the hoarding that is to be prevented
from being a source of danger to the
public because it affects visibility at crossroads. That is one of the points the
House will have to consider. We shall
have to strengthen that provision before
we part with the Bill.
Passing to the next sub-clause, we get
at once the rather difficult question as
to whether the exhibition of an advertisement is intended to include the erection
of the hoarding. By virtue of the interpretation Clause you can read "exhibition on hoardings" if you like, but I do
not know that that helps you very much.
Then we get references to "highway" and
""
village.' The trouble of highway in
Ireland as opposed to England has been
before us more than once, and can easily
be got over by definition, but the question
of village is more difficult. We have in
Northern Ireland places which in
England would be regarded as villages,
but which here would be small towns,
and are towns except that they have no
As an
system of town government.
example, we have the town of Antrim,
which has town commissioners, and the
Bill will require amendment there.
Again, we are proposing to repeal the
Section of the Ancient Monuments Act
which deals with interference with the
amenities of any ancient monument. We
have slightly extended the proposal here,
because we have added historic or public
buildings, which did not appear in the
Section of the old Ancient Monuments
Act of 1926, but we do not provide that
before any bylaws are made to deal with
that particular matter the Ancient
Monuments Advisory Council ought to
be consulted. Again, in the very next
sub-clause we get this same difficulty of
the confusion between the hoarding and
the advertisement, and we get a provision
for exemption, for so long a period as
three years, of existing advertisements.
We also get what appears to be a suggestion that an advertisement at present
on a hoarding is going to stay there for
two years.
We are not told what is to
happen if it peels off-whether the man
has to get a new advertisement or not.
Passing to sub-clause (5), the one which
I have spoken of as having introduced
something in the nature of confusion.
When that sub-clause made its first
appearance in another place, the wording
of the Bill was that sub-clause (2)
should not apply. It was objected by
an hon. Member, speaking on behalf of
the railways, that leaving the matter just
like that only got rid of the question of
the exhibition of the advertisement,
and did not exempt the railway
companies from liability to local bylaws as regards their hoardings and
structures. He pointed out that the
railways alwavs had had control
of their own structures and so on, and
suggested that exemption should be continued. His proposal was attacked, and
I am afraid I have to tell the House how
the new proposal was defended by the
Minister of Home Affairs.
I find that during the debate he stated
that there was a certain amount of mis-

[Mr. Leslie]
understanding; that the Bill was based
on the 1907 Act, and that the railway

have had on the debates in another place
I do not know, but if any hon. Member

so-called exemption of the railways it is

into this composite Clause, as I propose

The new provisions are to apply only to
harbours and so on within a rural district. Presently we hear something about
Belfast, of which I will speak in a

that it would be almost fatal to the
objects of the Bill to allow some of our

is a larger harbour. There is Larne
Harbour, and speaking of Larne
Harbour one naturally thinks of the
family so long associated with it and to
whom it owes its origin.
If you will permit me to digress for a
moment, I am sure the House will share
the deep regret with which all learned.
from the newspapers this morning of
the passing of a member of that family.
Mr. Chaine did not move so much in the
public life of late years as he did
formerly, but anybody who knew him.
respected him very deeply, and we all
regret his passing.
Larne Harbour is in a rural
district, and I cannot see that
you can refuse to Larne Harbour the
exemption which you are granting to
Portrush, a much smaller town, and to
sundry other places which happen to be
in an urban district. Whether by means
of a new Bill or by amending the existing Bill we must provide that Larne
Harbour is to be a separate entity.
Passing to Belfast, it has always been
the custom throughout Acts of Parliament dealing with Belfast Harbour to
provide that the Belfast Harbour
Commissioners should have complete
dominion within their territory, and
suggestions were made in another place
to provide for a continuance of that very
desirable state of affairs. But in my
judgment very considerable confusion
has been produced. The Bill adopts the
provision that this section shall not
apply. Therefore, it seems to me there
is a very great difficulty in introducing a
reference to the Belfast Harbour Commissioners who certainly have jurisdiction over a dock, pier, quay, or landing
stage, into the section.
Clause 6 deals to a great extent with
Belfast Harbour bylaws in spite of what
we have in Clause 5 that the section is
not to apply to the exhibition of advertisements in a dock or quay, etcetera,
which is within any urban district.
think that is a matter which will require
attention and something in the nature
of alteration. We shall have, in fact, to
separate the railways from the harbours.
I
Passing on to Clause 3, but still thinking of Belfast Harbour, I think that
perhaps the exemption given to the
Harbour Commissioners is not sufficiently
expanded. I am thinking of certain silo
undertakings and aerodrome activities
which are in their infancy, and it is not
desirable to do anything that will have
the effect of restriction.
Now we come to another point which
requires very considerable attention. It
is whether a rural district council is or
is not to be permitted to administer the
Act. It can only do so with the consent
of the county council, but in connection
with that I want to deal with the question of finance. Expenses incurred by a
local authority are to be paid (a) in the
case of a council of a county, out of the
county fund as a county-at-large charge off
so much of the county as is not comprised
in any borough or urban district; (b) in
the case of the council of a county
borough, borough, urban or rural dis
trict, out of the rate or fund applicable
137 Advertisements Regulation Bill-

4 MAY 1937
Second Reading
by the council to defray the expenses incurred by them in the execution of the
Public Health (Ireland) Acts.
The rural district will not have any
expenses under this Act unless the
execution of the Act has been delegated
to them by the county council, but if
the execution of the Act is so delegated,
and if the Clause, such as it is, remains
in the Bill, the unfortunate ratepayers
in that rural district will apparently
have to pay twice over. Their own rate
fund will be liable for the expenses incurred in their area, and they will also
be liable to contribute towards the
county-at-large part of the expenditure.
That would seem to require very drastic
alteration.
Passing to Clause 5, that particular
Clause shows rather markedly the advantage we get from the definition of advertisement. I am afraid I do not
altogether like the definition of advertisement. It does not go quite far
enough, but we see the best of it there.
I want to go back for a moment to Clause
2, the new Clause that was not in the
original Bill, allowing bylaws to be made
providing that advertisements should not
be affixed to a tree. That Clause was
moved in another place by one who was
then a private Member, but who is a
private Member no longer. He is now
in a position to move things rather more
important than individual amendments,
but I do not think that Clause goes far
enough. I should rather like something
nearer to the Scottish Clause in the Act
of 1907, and I certainly do think we
want the wall, and fence and gate of that
Scottish Clause, even if we cannot go the
whole distance that Clause would take
us. That is rather a large matter and
one of the reasons why I think it might
be better to bring in a new Bill rather
than amend this one. At the same time
I am by no means wedded to the suggestion that we should postpone the Bill,
because I would like a suitable Measure
brought into operation as speedily as
possible.

part of the Motion", put, and agreed to.

Provided that the court may set
aside any appointment made in pursuance of paragraph (b) of this section.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 6, page 4, to leave out in line 21
the words, "paragraph (b) of." It may
be remembered that I drew the attention
of the House to this rather small matter
when speaking on the Second Reading.
The effect of leaving in the words which
I suggest should be taken out is to confine the power of the court to paragraph
(b), and to exclude the court from control exercised by means of setting aside
any appointment over paragraph (a). In
the last English Act of 1889 it is plainly
provided that the court shall have power
over both (a) and (b), because in that
Act the words "paragraph (b)" do not
occur in this sense. Looking back to the
old Common Law Procedure Act of 1856
-I only had access to the revised edition
-I was bound to admit that it may be
contended perhaps that the proviso at
the end of the corresponding Section was
only intended to apply to the latter part,
to what answers to paragraph (b) now
before us, and was not intended to apply
to paragraph (a). As to that I could
not form an opinion.
All I can say is that if we adopt
the words before us this afternoon we
shall be distinctly departing from the
English Act, and it is questionable, to
my mind, whether we shall be departing
6 MAY 1937
Arbitration Bill-

Committee
Mr. LESLIE: It is such a technical
point, and we have had the considered
view of the Government, that I do not
think the Committee will desire that I
should press the Amendment. It is
definitely admitted that in our view the
English Act is wrong, and that that is
the reason for our departing from it. I
think we must leave it at that. I ask
leave to withdraw the Amendment.
from the earlier Irish Act. That is to
say, we shall run the risk of depriving,
so far as it is in our power to deprive, the
court of certain power. Whether the
court wants that power or not is another
question. I cannot myself see any conceivable use which the court can make of
the power of controlling an appointment
under paragraph (a), but still they have
the power and it may be said they had it
under the old proviso of 1856. I do not
know how much weight the House will
attach to the proviso of 1856. It is
obviously an old one. It contains the
word "
however," which in a proviso now
only survives, I understand, as an archaeological object.
The question the Committee must consider is, is it right to run the risk of
taking away from the court a power
which, although they may never use it,
it is rather for the court than for Parliament to say should continue to exist or
not. In order to get a definite pronouncement on the subject I beg to move
the Amendment.
Mr. LONG: The words which the hon.
and learned Senator proposes to leave out
were considered during the drafting of
the Bill and were inserted deliberately.
While it is correct to say that in Section
Six of the Arbitration Act of 1889 the
proviso covers both paragraph (a) and
paragraph (b), I am advised that in
Section Sixteen of the Common Law Procedure Amendment Act (Ireland), 1856,
which Clause 6 of the Bill in effect reproduces, the proviso only applies to the
appointment by a party of his arbitrator
to act as sole arbitrator, and has no
reference to the substitution of a new
arbitrator under the first part of that
section.
This is the view expressed in "Russell
on Arbitration and Award," the leading
text-book on the subject, where it is also
stated that "there can be little doubt
that the draftsman of the Arbiration
Act, 1889, intended Section Six to be a
re-enactment of the provisions of this
Section of the Common Law Procedure
Act with verbal alterations only."
Russell also points out that the framers
of the Indian Arbitration Act had made
the proviso apply to an appointment
under paragraph (b) only. Therefore, I
cannot accept the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

CLAUSE 26 (Penalty for perjury).
Any person who wilfully and corruptly gives false evidence before any
referee, arbitrator or umpire shall be
guilty of perjury, as if the evidence.
had been given in open court, and may
be dealt with, prosecuted and punished
accordingly.

:-

Arbitration Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
Mr. LESLIE: This is, if one may say
30, rather one of the curiosities of the
Bill, and it is a curiosity not confined
to this particular Bill. It is dealing
with perjury. The Clause before us reenacts Section 22 of that Act of 1889 to
which we referred a few moments ago.
That Section in the Act of 1889 was
repealed in England by the Perjury Act
of 1911, and partly for such purposes as
those to which this Bill relates was
mopped up in the general law of perjury
as dealt with in that Act of 1911. That
Act of 1911 did not apply to Ireland,
and, therefore, we found when we were
consolidating the company law that it
was necessary to use words similar to
those I have on the Paper to-day in order
to secure that the offence of perjury
under our Companies Act was dealt with
and punished strictly in accordance with
the words of the indictment.
We inquired at that time as to what
words were used in indictments in Northern Ireland for perjury, and we found
that it was advisable to insert the words
which I have asked the Committee to use
again to-day. In fact, we were almost
driven to the conclusion that, strictly on
the book, you could not punish a man
for perjury unless you charged him with
wilful and corrupt perjury and convicted
him accordingly. It is rather an amusing position, but I think the change I am
suggesting is right, and it follows the
precedent Parliament established in this
country in the year 1922.
Mr. LONG: The Government accept
this Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 30, page 14, to leave out
the word "submit" in line 21,
and insert instead the word "refer."
The words before us are taken again
from the English Act of 1889, and,
as I pointed out the other day, throughout that Act the expressions "submit"
and "submission" are used, whereas in
this Bill we use the words "refer" or
"reference." Mr. Attorney, speaking
in another place about the Bill generally,
rather laid stress on the fact that it was
desirable not to use the words "submit"
or similar words because they always
raise a doubt as to what it was that was
submitted, whether you submitted the
matter, or whether you submitted to the
result of the arbitration. In every other
place in the Bill before us we have got
rid of "submit," and we should do the
same here. The objection to retaining
it is not that it is bad English-it is perfectly good English-but that it subjects
the word "refer" to unnecessary and uncalled for myosis, and does not correspond
as it should do.
The Committee will see what I mean
in the very first words in the Bill-a
"reference "-not a "submission ".
under an arbitration agreement. Again
in Clause 10, Sub-clause (2) on page 5,
it says disputes shall be "referred," and
I think again in Clause 15, in all cases
of "reference," and so on. Trivial as
the alteration may appear to be, I think
it would be desirable to make it, and I
beg to move accordingly.
Mr. LONG: The Government accept
the Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.

part of the Bill.

me possible that a testator may die

which the provisions of this Act and its

would happen would be that so far as

Clause would not come into operation

come into operation as regards the English

will of any testator who dies after the
thirty-first day of December, nineteen
We do it
hundred and forty-seven."
that way. We are postponing the possible operation here for two years as compared with England, and I cannot see
myself that there is any magic in waiting
for ten years.

do just as well, and the possible inconvenience of having a different provision
here from the provision in England is of
no negligible importance. I cannot see
that we should do any harm by altering
"forty-seven" to "forty-five," and we

Unemployment Insurance
(Additional Days and Waiting Period)
Order (Northern Ireland), 1937.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That the
Order in Council made by the Governor
of Northern Ireland on the 22nd March,
1937, under Section 65 of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland),
1936, for increasing the maximum number of additional days of benefit, other
than agricultural benefit, payable in a
benefit year, and for reducing, in relation to benefit other than agricultural
benefit, the number of days at the
beginning of a continuous period of
unemployment after which benefit
becomes payable, be hereby approved."
The Order which is the subject of the
present Motion was made by His Grace
the Governor in pursuance of Section 65
of the Unemployment Insurance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1936. Its purpose
was to apply to Northern Ireland the
provisions of an Order made on the 10th
March, 1937, by the Minister of Labour
in Great Britain with the approval of
Parliament, effecting
effecting two important
changes in the Unemployment Insurance
Scheme in Great Britain in relation to
the payment of unemployment benefit,
other than agricultural benefit. The
maximum number of days of benefit payable to applicants with good industrial
records was increased, and the "waiting
period" during which benefit is not payable at the beginning of a "continuous
period of unemployment" was reduced
from six to three days.
These changes were made by the
Minister of Labour in Great Britain on
the recommendation of the Unemployment Insurance Statutory Committee to
dispose of a surplus on the general
account of the Unemployment Fund
which the committee estimated would
amount to approximately two and a
quarter million pounds per annum for
the next eight years. The general
account of the fund is the source from
which the cost of benefit other than
agricultural benefit is met.
on
By the Order for which the approval
of the House is now sought, the Unemployment Insurance Scheme in Northern
Ireland has thus been maintained
parallel lines with the scheme in Great
Britain. Under the provisions of the
Unemployment Act (Northern Ireland),
1936, by virtue of which the Order has
been made, it would cease to have effect
on the expiration of twelve months from
the date on which it came into operation
unless at some time before the expiration
of that period it had been approved by
Parliament.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Unemployment Insurance
(Private Gardeners Inclusion) Order
(Northern Ireland), 1937.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, “That the
Order in Council made by the Lords
Justices for the Government of Northern
Ireland on the 22nd January, 1937,
under Section 65 of the Unemployment
Insurance Act (Northern Ireland), 1936,
for the inclusion of employment as a
private gardener among insurable
employments as employment in agriculture, be hereby approved."
When the Unemployment Insurance
Scheme was extended to agriculture,
gardeners employed in a trade or business or by a local authority were brought
within its scope. Private gardeners were
not, as in some respects their conditions
of employment and security of tenure
were more like those of domestic servants
than of other workers, but provision was
made for their inclusion if the Unemployment Insurance Statutory Committee decided that that course was
desirable. After hearing the evidence
the committee reported that it was desirable that they should be brought into
insurance under the same conditions as
agricultural workers, and an Order
169 Unemployment Insurance-

6 MAY 1937
Resolution Agreed to
providing for their inclusion was laid
before the Parliament of the United
Kingdom and approved by resolution of
both Houses.
Section 65 (1) (b) of the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland),
1936, provides as follows:
In the event of an Order being made
at any time under the Unemployment
Insurance (Agriculture) Act, 1936
(passed by the Parliament of the
United Kingdom), for the purpose of
including any class of employment as
a private gardener among insurable
employments as employment in agriculture, then in that event there shall
apply to Northern Ireland so much as
the Governor of Northern Ireland by
Order in Council declares applicable
of
the provisions contained in such
statutory order.
An Order in Council was made on
22nd January, 1937, including employment as a private gardener among
employments insurable under the Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1936, as being employment in
agriculture, and the Order came into
operation on the 1st February, 1937,
the same date as that on which the
corresponding Order in Great Britain
came into force. The Order in Council
was duly presented to Parliament, but
it is necessary that a resolution approving it should be passed by each House,
as provided for by Section 65 (4) of the
Unemployment Insurance Act (Northern Ireland), 1936.
The inclusion of private gardeners in
insurance as agricultural workers has not
met with any opposition so far as the
Ministry is aware, and has been
welcomed by many employers as well as
by the gardeners themselves.
Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to
know how this will affect the gardener
who is engaged by six different
employers during the week. Will he
have to have his card stamped by one
employer in the same way as a gardener
who is employed by one master for a
whole week. It would be a hardship to
a gardener who was employed by six
different persons in a week if he was not
to reap the same benefit as a gardener
who was employed by only one person
during that time.
Mr. LESLIE: I am rather glad the
hon. Member has called attention to this
matter again. We all know the answer
he is about to receive, but that is not
quite the point. It ought to be recognised how very expensive it is becoming
to employ a man for any purpose whatever. I think I am right in saying that
the combined insurance for health and
pension purposes of the ordinary man,
gardener, labourer, or what-not,
amounts to ls. 8d. a week; 10d. from the
man and 10d. from his employer. His
agricultural stamp will cost another 9d.,
or 4d. from each, so that a man has to
pay over ls. a week for employing a man
in an uninsured trade in the ordinary
sense of the word. If he was employed
in those trades before agriculture and
gardening came into the picture the
expense was far less. It ought not to be
forgotten that there is something in the
nature of a hindrance to employment due
to the introduction of unemployment
schemes.
There was, as the House will remember, a tax on male servants in England.
It never ran in this country, and that
tax is being abolished in the financial
arrangements that were made in Great
Britain during the present year, and I
think one of the reasons for its abolition
is the idea that it will help employment.
I do not, of course, in any way oppose
the Resolution to approve the Order.
Mr. HILL DICKSON: I understand
that the person who employs a man on
the first day of the week on which he is
employed, whether it be Monday or any
other day, has to stamp his card. There
are many gardeners employed by local
people who contract to go for one, two,
or three days a week to look after their
gardens. Are they liable, as contractors,
to have their card stamped?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN : I hope the
Government will give every consideration
to the private gardener. I have previously suggested to the Government
that private gardeners should be in-
Icluded in the insurance scheme. Like
other insured workers, they have got to
look to the future, and the Government
should see that these poor gardeners are
protected, and that they get the same
rights and benefits as their fellow
labourers. Senator Hill Dickson referred
Unemployment Insurance-
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
to gardeners who are employed by several
employers in the same week. The
employer who employs such a person on
the first day is responsible for stamping
his card, but many of these private
gardeners are so anxious to become insured that they are prepared to meet the
employer as regards the stamping of
their card. It should be the duty of
every broad-minded citizen to see that
these private gardeners are properly protected against unemployment and
possible starvation in the future, and
that as good citizens and hardworking
men they should be entitled to a pension
when they reach the appropriate age. I
sincerely trust the Government will give
the working man, and especially the
private gardener, the consideration he
deserves in this respect.

Question to Minister.
THE CLOSE SEASONS ACT.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to ask the Leader
of the House the following question,
which I think explains itself :
:-
To ask the Leader of the House
what steps have been, or are being,
Advertisements Regulation Bill 176
Oral Answer
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
taken to direct public attention to the
provision of the Close Seasons Act,
which came into operation on the 1st
January last, and to the Order restricting the taking of wild ducks recently
made by the Ministry of Home Affairs.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): I am glad that the hon. and
learned Senator has put down this
question, as I feel that it may help to
draw attention to recent legislation
relating to close seasons.
Arrangements have been made whereby printed slips will be affixed to all
gun and game licences issued during the
present year, giving full particulars of
the actual dates of the open seasons in
respect of the various species of game
and wild birds.
It is also proposed that a notice giving
the same particulars shall be given as
wide publicity as possible by insertion
in the public Press at a later date closer
to the opening of the shooting season.
I am glad to learn also that through
the courtesy of the British Broadcasting
Corporation a short talk is to be given
on the wireless by a member of the Wild
Birds Advisory Committee of Northern
Ireland who is eminently qualified to
speak on the subject. Such a talk will,
I am sure, appeal to all those who are
interested in the subject, and it may
also impress on the minds of others the
reasons why laws have to be passed from
time to time altering the close seasons of
various species of game and wild birds,
and the importance of ensuring that
those laws are observed.

[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Power of local authorities
to make bylaws for regulating advertisements).
(1) Subject to the provisions of this
Act, a local authority may, for the purpose of preventing danger to the
public, make bylaws for the regulation
and control of hoardings and similar
structures used or intended to be used
for the display of advertisements.
(2) Subject to the provisions of this
Act, a local authority may make bylaws
for regulating, restricting or preventing within their district or any part
thereof the exhibition of advertisements so as to disfigure or injuriously
affect-
(a) the amenities of a public park or
pleasure promenade or the natural
beauty of a landscape; or
(b) the view of rural scenery from a
highway or railway, or from any
public place or water; or
(c) the amenities of any village within
the district of a rural district
council; or
(d) the amenities of any historic or
public building or monument, or
of any place frequented by the
public solely or chiefly on account
of its beauty or historic interest.
(3) Bylaws made for the purposes of
sub-section (2) of this section mav provide that in any area within the district
of a local authority specified in that
behalf in the bylaw:-
(a) no advertisements shall be erected
or exhibited; or
(b) no advertisements shall be erected
or exhibited without the consent of
the local authority.
(4) A local authority, in making
bylaws under this section, shall provide for the exemption from the operation of such bylaws of any hoardings
and similar structures in use for advertising purposes at the time of the
making of the bylaws. and of any
advertisements exhibited at that time,
for such period, not being less than
three years from that time, as they may
think fit.
(5) This section shall not apply to
the exhibition of advertisements in or
upon any railway station, vard, platform or railway approach belonging to
a railway company, or excent within
the district of a rural district council,

Committee
upon any dock, quay, pier, landing
stage, wharf, lock or toll station
belonging to any harbour, dock or canal
undertaking.

would, of necessity, apply particularly to

Viscount CHARLEMONT: I regret I
cannot accept this Amendment. Section
16 of the Ancient Monuments Act
(Northern Ireland), 1926, which is being
repealed by this Bill, did not require local
authorities to consult the Ancient Monuments Advisory Committee when making
bylaws prohibiting or restricting the display of advertisements so as to affect
injuriously the amenities of any ancient
monument, and there is no apparent
reason why they should be required to
do so now. It is open to the Advisory
Committee, as it is to anyone else, to
make representations to a local authority
if they consider that bylaws ought to be
made to protect an ancient monument, or
if they think that proposed bylaws do not
go far enough in the direction of affording protection during the period the
bylaws are open for consideration.
Mr. LESLIE: It is not an Amendment I should desire to press.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause

CLAUSE 2 (Bylaws as to advertisements on trees).
Subject to the provisions of this Act
and without prejudice to the operation
of any bylaws made under section one
thereof, a local authority may make
bylaws for the purpose of providing
that no advertisements shall be affixed
to, or otherwise exhibited upon, any
tree.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, at the end of Clause 2 to insert
the words, "fence, hedge, wall, or building". The Clause as it stands gives
county councils power to make bylaws for
the exhibition of advertisements on trees.
The additional words come from what is
known as the Scottish Clause in the
English Act. I understand it has been
found to be a very useful Clause in
Scotland, and its purpose is to give a
rather wider power to county councils to
prohibit the exhibition of advertisements
on any tree, fence, hedge, wall, or building.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I am not
prepared to accept this Amendment.
The general provisions of the Bill appear
adequate to deal with advertisements
affixed to fences, hedges, walls, or buildings, and the only reason for making a
special provision relating to trees was
that advertisements affixed to trees seem
particularly liable to spoil the amenities
and might be injurious to trees.
Mr. LESLIE: I cannot agree with
the noble Viscount that walls, palings,
and so on, are adequately protected by
any other passage in the Bill. Perhaps
he will intimate to us a little more clearly
what he relies upon in order to get the
additional protection which is given, as
the mover of the Amendment suggested,
in the Clause particularly applicable to
Scotland which occurs in the English Act
of 1907. It seems to me that that Clause
does give us protection which I do not
myself find in this Bill. Therefore I
should feel disposed to support my hon.
Friend.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I think
Clause 1 gives all the protection necessary.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I wish I
were as confident as is the noble Viscount
that Clause 1 does give us that protection. It is possible it does, but if we
are going to protect a fence, hedge, wall,
or building, is there any reason why we
should not put it in this Clause?
Captain PERCEVAL - MAXWELL:
The very fact that trees are mentioned
is sometimes taken as an indication that
there is some particular reason for
mentioning them, and therefore there
cannot be much wrong in mentioning
fences, hedges, walls, and buildings. If
the Bill as it stands is sufficient to pro-
183 Advertisements Regulation Bill-

THE SENATE
Committee
[Captain Perceval-Maxwell]
tect trees there is no need to mention
them specially in this Clause. In drafting a Bill it is considered inadvisable to
mention unnecessary things, and unless
there is some special reason for mentioning trees there should be no objection to
specifically referring to a fence, hedge,
wall, or building.
Viscount
CHARLEMONT: If the
mover of the Amendment will agree to
withdraw it for the present I will undertake to consider the point at a later

CLAUSE 3 (Provisions as to local
authorities and their expenses).
*

(ii) where all or any of the powers of

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, to leave out paragraph (ii) of
sub-clause (2) of Clause 3. This Amendment is really consequential to the
Amendment on the Paper to omit Clause
4, and perhaps it would be convenient if
I put forward my arguments now on
both Amendments. Clause 4 is a permissive Clause permitting a county
council to delegate many of its powers
under this Act to rural district councils,
and the point is whether a rural district
council is a suitable body to exercise these
powers. In my mind there is some doubt
as to whether they are. In the first
place, it is always possible that a rural
district council might be embarrassed
in the administration of this Act by
what I might call local pressure. In the
second place, the administration of the
Act will require the services of clerks
of works which county councils possess
on their surveyors staffs, but I do not
think rural district councils would be
so well equipped. There is also the
chance that county councils would delegate powers to some, though not all,
rural district councils, the inhabitants of
whose areas might feel they were paying
for the expenses of administering the
Act twice over, once in their capacity as
ratepayers of the county council and
once in their capacity as ratepayers of
the rural district council. However,
that is not a big point, as the expenses
are not likely to amount to very much.
The main point is the danger of lack
of uniformity in the administration of
the Act. Supposing the powers of the
county council are delegated to a rural
district council which takes a very strict
view of its duties and allows practically
no advertisements at all to be put up.
Another rural district council takes a
very lenient view and allows almost any
kind of advertisement to be displayed
anywhere. If the views of those two
councils cut right across each other the
stringency of the one must be destroyed
by the leniency of the other. The areas.
of these two rural councils might adjoin
each other, and it might be very difficult
for those who put up these advertisements to know which authority to go to.
Of course, those who object to the course
I am suggesting might argue, "Well, the
Clause is only permissive. County
councils need not delegate their powers if
they do not want to." But I submit
that to give powers which you do not
wish should be exercised is absurd, and
the point is whether or not it is desirable
that these powers should be delegated by
the county councils.
Mr. LESLIE: I support the Amendment, partly by drawing attention to the
case of Larne Harbour, which would
seem to require special treatment if
power is taken away from the county
council and given to the rural district
councils, and partly by a desire to protect county councils from the invidious
duty of selecting which of their rural
185 Advertisements Regulation Bill- 1 JUNE 1937
Committee

relevant, as, in that case, the county
council can step in and make bylaws for
any area in which they are not being
made, but in which it thinks they should
be made. Normally county councils will
not need to delegate their responsibilities,
but in certain areas the rural districts are
best able to keep an eye on their own
amenities.
district councils they will entrust with
this power, and which they will not. It
is becoming more and more necessary
that county councils should work in
harmony with their rural district
councils, and I do not look forward with
pleasurable anticipation to the difficulties.
that might arise if rural district councils
were to apply for these powers and were
refused. We should be far safer if we
left these powers in the hands of the
county councils.
Mr. HILL-DICKSON: I also desire
to support this Amendment. It is not
necessary for me to repeat the arguments
that have been put forward by Senator
Sir Roland Nugent. In the case of the
Down County Council we have six rural
district councils, and if the county
council's powers under this Bill were
delegated to them we might have
different regulations existing in those six
rural districts. Therefore, I think the
Government should accept this Amendment.
Captain PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
also wish to support the Amendment. It
would be
difficult for
very
a county
council to say to one rural district
council, "We do not think you are the
right people to whom to delegate this
power", and then to tell another rural
district council that it was a suitable
body to administer the Act. In any case,
a county council has no means of discovering which of its rural district
councils are the most suitable or unsuitable to possess this power. In addition,
the membership of these rural district
councils changes from time to time, and
although the powers, once delegated,
might be exercised by the existing rural
council, their successors might be unwilling to continue to carry them out.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I cannot
accept this Amendment. The Government is definitely of the opinion that
rural district councils should, if they so
desire, be local authorities for the purpose
of making bylaws relating to advertisements, as, in many cases, they may be
expected to have more interest in preserving the amenities of a locality than the
county council. The fact that one rural
district council may make bylaws while.
another does not hardly seems to be
Mr. LESLIE: But the noble Viscount
has not told us how the rural district
councils can enforce the bylaw. What
means have they of getting this advice?
Road surveyors are always at hand to
advise their county councils.
Viscount
CHARLEMONT: Would
you repeat that point? I did not
catch it.
Mr. LESLIE: The point, as I think it
was made by the mover of the Amendment, was that the county councils have
under their command a body of road surveyors and assistant road surveyors and
people of that kind who are constantly on
the roads, and, therefore, in a position to
advise the county councils as to what is
happening or about to happen.
rural district councils, as far as I know,
have nobody answering the same description, and, therefore, it would seem that
you are giving them power which they
could not exercise.
The
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I understand the rural district councils have
sanitary officers.
Mr.
HILL-DICKSON: They have

This is a duty for the county surveyor

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid

honestly believe that if the county

CLAUSES 5 (Removal of advertisements erected in contravention of
bylaws), and 6 (Prosecution of offences)

interested.
Mr. LESLIE: I ask leave to withdraw

is not passed the Advertisements Regulation Act of 1907 cannot be repealed.
Although that may not arise I thought

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to insert

This is a little Amendment to which I
do attach a great deal of importance. It
is not really advertisements that this Bill
is going to regulate; it is the exhibition
of advertisements, and the omission of
the words "of the exhibition" led to
rather unnecessary discussion in another
place. It went on the lines of the ethics
of the particular advertisement. It
seemed to be thought that the Bill was
directed to the ethical side of the question
rather than to the physical side. The
best way out of that difficulty was to put
in the words "exhibition of" which
occurred in the original Act of 1907
which we
are repealing. In England
they did not repeal the Act of 1907.
They grafted the Act of 1925 on top of
it, but we are making one Act do the
work of two. Accordingly, I think it is
very desirable that these words should
go into the Title, so that those who run
or rather stand and gaze at the advertisement may read.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I accept
the Amendment.

CLAUSE 2 (Bylaws as to advertisements on trees).
Subject to the provisions of this Act
and without prejudice to the operation

thereof, a local authority may make bylaws for the purpose of providing that
no advertisements shall be affixed to,
