Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, at the end of Clause 2, to insert,
"or, without the consent in writing of the
owner and occupier, upon any wall, fence
or building. This is the amended
version of the Amendment I moved on
the Committee Stage. The objection to
the original form of my Amendment was
that it might be made mandatory on the
county council in making any bylaw at
all, to include the list of things I had in
my Amendment, and that some county
councils might think that that was too
sweeping and pass no bylaws at all on the
subject. The altered form of my Amendment gives a county council more liberty
in that respect, so that it can make bylaws that no advertisements shall be
affixed to trees, and only with the written
consent of the owner or occupier allow
them to be put on walls, fences and buildings. Personally, I must say that I do
not like this Amendment as much as my
original proposal, but, at the same time,
I recognise the weight of the arguments
that were put forward against my first
Amendment.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of

world is ever completely convinced by
argument, so that I dare say the mover
of the amendment does think his original
idea is better than this Amendment, but
I accept this one. (Laughter.)

Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
8 JUNE 1937
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 8th June, 1937.
The Senate met at a quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Solicitors Bill [S.].
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LESLIE: My first duty this
afternoon in connection with the Bill,
the Second Reading of which I am about
to move, is to apologise to you, Mr.
Speaker, and the House for the absence
of my colleague in support of the Bill,
the hon. and gallant Senator from
County Down (Captain Perceval -
Maxwell). He had to be in England
to fulfil an engagement of long standing,
and in consequence he is not able to be
here this afternoon. I am sorry if only
because had he been here he would have
joined with me in expressing our joint
gratification in being asked to be the
sponsors in this House of a Bill of such
importance as the one before us.
It occurs to me that some Members of
the House may have some doubt as to
the propriety of a Bill of this class being
introduced by a private Member rather
than by a Member of the Government.
But the solution to that doubt is a
simple one. It is in accordance with
precedent that Bills relating to the
Societies of the legal profession should
be introduced by private Members.
That has been the general practice. I
do not say it has been the exclusive
practice, for there are several instances
to the contrary. The only Measure of
that class which we ourselves have produced in Northern Ireland is the Act
of 1922, and that, some hon. Members
will recollect, was introduced in the
Senate by the then Parliamentary Secretary, and it had previously been introduced in another place by the Minister
of Home Affairs. We were unable to
follow precedent on that occasion partly,
I think, because we were then somewhat
in the embryonic stage. We had
scarcely got far enough in Parliamentary
experience for private Members to introduce Bills of general application, and,
accordingly, it was decided on that
occasion that the work should be undertaken by the direct representatives of
the Government.
But although we are not following
that precedent to-day I am not without
hope that the Government of the day
will not, so to speak, hold that up
against me, but that they will be able,
through the mouthpiece of the present
Parliamentary Secretary, to express
benevolent approval of the purposes of
the Bill and to refrain from offering any
obstacles to our obtaining the necessary
facilities for debate.
I have just said all the precedents are
not in favour of the introduction of a
Bill of this class by a private Member,
but I want to take one case where a
Bill was introduced by a private Member
at Westminster. That is the case of the
Bill of 1898, which is so much referred
to in the Bill before us. That Bill was
introduced in the House of Commons
by Mr. Sergeant Hemphill, as he then
was, and it was introduced in the House
of Lords by Lord Macnaghten. There
are some Members of this House who
will understand how great a satisfaction
it is to me personally to follow
humbly, in this second Chamber of the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, in the
footsteps of so great a jurist as Lord
Macnaghten in the House of Lords.
He was probably the most prominent
lawyer Northern Ireland ever produced
He was a near neighbour of my own.
and a very kind and honoured friend.
But although I am following him in
introducing this Bill, I cannot follow
him in regard to the extreme brevity
with which he introduced the Measure
to which I have referred. He had this
advantage, that he was able to say that
the Bill had already passed the House
of Commons. That is not the case today, for we are introducing the Bill
ourselves.
I want to be as brief as I reasonably
can, and, therefore, I shall refer the
House to the very expressive but succinct
memorandum which appears on the face
of the Bill. The particular matters as
to which it is desired to effect improvement in Northern Ireland are education,
discipline, and elasticity, and certain
protection for clients. It will be noticed
that education comes first, and that is so
Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
entirely by design, because during the
fifteen years of its separate existence the
Incorporated Law Society of Northern
Ireland has always had education particularly in its mind. It is now thought that
the time has come when it must come
back to Parliament, to the Parliament
which created it, for further powers in
order not only to secure the ground
already obtained, but to prevent the
profession in Northern Ireland in any
way lagging behind in the march of
modern educational progress.
I do not
want it to be understood that I am
suggesting that interest in education in
the legal profession, or in any other
learned profession, did not begin until
1922. That would be entirely contrary
to the facts.
If the House will think for a moment
of what has happened in the profession
of therapeutics they will know that for
many years there has been a long course
of progressive legislation bringing detailed education to a higher pitch than
was the case before such legislation was
introduced. Many Members will remember perhaps the Act which gave rise to
the "fraternity of judicious apothecaries," which was passed towards the
end of the eighteenth century, and which
gave place to the Medical Council Acts of
1860 and 1862. Later on Parliament
incorporated a society for those purposes,
and the Act expressed itself as being a
Measure to deal with "education, regulation and control." The House, will,
therefore, agree, I think, that the
position of the two learned professions,
the medical and the law, is very much
allied.
As regards the development of statutory provision for legal education, we
have to confess that the earliest record
we have is a comparatively modern one.
That is speaking of law in the sense of
that which is really extremely ancient.
The earliest record we have of a Statute
dealing with the legal profession is something less than six hundred years old. It
dates from 1402, in the time of His
Majesty King Henry IV, and it may
amuse the House to be told that the
Statute then passed in England was not
repealed for England until 1843, nor for
Ireland until 1872. That was the earliest
Statute I know of, but we must jump
over a few intervening centuries and
come to what has happened in our own
country.
A Charter was granted to the Attorneys
and Solicitors of Ireland in 1852, and it
was stated as one of the purposes for which
that Charter was granted that it was for
"the foundation of an institution for
facilitating the acquisition of legal knowledge." In the first Charter given to
solicitors in Ireland education was kept
in the forefront. That was followed by
the Act of 1866, which was obtained, I
understand, with considerable difficulty;
but as it has since been repealed I need
not trouble the House with it. Then
came a supplementary Charter in 1888,
which introduced the modern title of the
Incorporated Law Society of Ireland, so
if this House concurs with me and gives
this Bill a Second Reading, and if the
Bill is so fortunate as to obtain the
approval of our colleagues in another
place and comes into operation, as we hope
it will, on 1st January next year, it will,
I think, be a not unfitting milestone of
the jubilee of the Incorporated Law
Society of Ireland.
But it is with Northern Ireland we have
to deal more than with Ireland as a
whole, and the Northern Ireland Incorporated Law Society started in 1922. It
was found necessary at that time, owing
to the establishment of our own courts
in Northern Ireland, that a society
should be set up with the same authority
over Northern Ireland matters as
exercised by the older society over the
rest of Ireland. As a matter of fact,
there had been something of the sort before, for the Northern Law Society was
in existence. That was a society of
was
solicitors for the title of solicitor had
come into force long before-of Antrim,
Armagh, Down and Belfast, and that
society had become incorporated, though
only under the Companies Act, in 1911.
It was on the foundation of the Northern
Law Society that the Incorporated Law
Society of Northern Ireland was really
founded.
Now I think the House will have heard
perhaps enough about the ancient history
of the societies, and will want me to come
to the Bill itself. The Bill itself, in the
main, seeks to amend the Act of 1898. It
also amends to some extent the little Act
of 1922, but it brings in at least four
more recent statutes, and those statutes
have to be mentioned, since one of them
Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
8 JUNE 1937
is the English Act of 1932, which was a
Consolidating Measure. It was followed
by a similar Act for Scotland, with, of
course, the necessary changes required by
the difference between Scottish and
English law, and the Bill also deals with
the later English statutes of 1933 and
1934. There has been a subsequent
statute of 1936, which, although considered during the preparation of the Bill,
does not find any particular expression in
the Bill before us.
I could have wished that I should have
been able to come to the House with a
proposal for the complete consolidation of
the law relating to solicitors. It may be
somebody's good fortune some day to be
in that position, but the time is not yet.
It is not thought that, certainly until a
new Act such as the Bill before us has
been on the Statute Book for some few
years and experience has been gained in
that way, that it would be practicable to
come before Parliament and ask for a
completely consolidating Measure. I know
the House, and certainly people outside
the House, will feel some disappointment
at that statement, and I ask those people
to think of what I have just said. That
is, that a Consolidating Act was brought
in in England in 1932, and that, speaking now, in the middle of 1937, I am able
to point to at least three English Amending Acts, which would seem to show that
consolidation undertaken with undue
rapidity does not always lead to complete
success. Moreover, we have to deal with
the question of amending existing law,
and as we have been told before, amendment and consolidation are horses which
do not run well in double harness.
The main objects of the Bill are improvement in education, the enforcement
of discipline in the profession, the protection of clients' monetary interest, and,
further, to introduce a general elasticity
and to increase the discretion of the Incorporated Law Society of Northern Ireland,
who are the controlling authority over
all matters relating to their profession.
I think the Bill will bear me out that
it is intended that all these things should
be done strictly under the control of the
King's Courts, in exactly the same way
as things are managed to-day.
I am not going to go through
the whole Bill. There are sixtytwo Clauses and two Schedules, and,
although it might perhaps assist one
or two people if the Bill was read
through Clause by Clause, I do not think
it is necessary this afternoon, for there
will be a Committee stage, and I have
other reasons for refraining from going
too minutely through the details
of the various Clauses. First of all, I
do not want to weary the House, and
also there is really no occasion why I
should, for the Bill is, as hon. Members
will see, so divided into parts following
those main lines of thought I have
mentioned, and also is so punctuated
with references to the repeals of the existing Statutes which are to be effected that
there is very little difficulty in following
Clause by Clause the differences in the
existing provisions of the law which it
is desired to introduce.
Again, there is one other thing to be
remembered in this connection, and one
which I would like to impress upon the
House. To some extent those of us who
are concerned in the presentation of this
Bill to Parliament are acting for our
clients. We have no authority to pledge
our clients in any way as to the manner
in which they will exercise the discretion
we hope Parliament is about to confide
to them. We cannot bind our clients in
any matter of administration. The intention is to widen the discretion of the
Incorporated Law Society, and it would
be most injurious, and, if I may say so,
quite improper for anything to be said
in this House by people promoting the
Bill, which would render itself liable to
be quoted hereafter as undertaking that
this or that would be done. Therefore, I
shall refrain from dealing in too much
detail with the Clauses.
But I must draw attention to one or
two Clauses in the Bill. First of all I
want the House to look at Clause 1, and
at the very beginning of the Clauseparagraph (b) of sub-clause (1) at the
top of page 2. It will be observed that
power is proposed to be taken there for
making regulations to enable the progress
made during apprenticeship in acquiring
knowledge of the theory and practice of
the law to be tested. Those words are
almost exactly as they come from the
Charter, and, although the intermediate
examination, under that term, no longer
finds a place in the Bill, there is ample
possibility provided here for encouraging
the honest apprentice and discouraging the
idle apprentice. Exactly what method the
Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
It
[Mr. Leslie]
society intend to adopt for the discouragement of the idle apprentice I do not
know, and, if I did, in accordance with
what I have previously stated. I should
keep the information to myself.
may be that it might be thought desirable
to institute some course of lectures on
the works of Hogarth, the artist, and to
take apprentices to see the works of
Hogarth, but if that course were decided
upon there is ample power in the Bill for
the society to follow that course.
I pass on rapidly to Clause 14, which
is a Clause about which I ought to give
the House some little warning. That
simply deals with the position of people
who, having been called to the Bar of
Northern Ireland, and for some reason or
other decided to seek refuge in another
fold of the same profession, have a desire.
to give up the Bar and become solicitors.
It is required that a person who has been
called to the Bar in Northern Ireland and
wishes to become a solicitor, must obtain
from the Attorney-General a certificate
to the effect that he is a fit and proper
person to practice as a solicitor. He must
obtain his certificate from the Attorney-
General. I have left that provision in
the Bill because it reproduces the existing
law which was laid down in the Act of
1922. At that time, the House will
remember, we had no Inn of Court of
Northern Ireland, and, therefore, the Inn
of Court which did not exist had no
Benchers. It was, therefore, necessary at
that time to depart from the older practice which was laid down in the Act of
1898, that such a person should obtain
his certificate from two of the Benchers
of the King's Inn, and it may be possible
that the House in Committee will be
asked to revert to the ancient practice of
making the certificate obtainable from
two Benchers of the Inn of Court of
Northern Ireland instead of from the
Attorney-General.
I think that is all I have to say to the
House as regards the Clauses in Part I,
and I pass to Clause 20, which is in Part
II. I want to draw a little attention to
Clause 20, because it exemplifies two
points I desire to make. The first point I
want to take is that it exemplifies the
anxiety to introduce elasticity into the
proceeding. The purpose of the Clause
is to give the Incorporated Law Society,
through its Disciplinary Committee,
power to deal with an offending solicitor
themselves in respect of a misdemeanour
of such a class that it is not necessary to
take him before the actual court with a
view to his suspension from practice, or
striking him from the Roll. Similar
powers exist across the water, and it is
thought very desirable that they should
be here. Accordingly, this Clause has
been inserted which empowers the Incorporated Law Society, acting through its
Disciplinary Committee, to deal with an
offending solicitor with reasonable severity
but without undue hardship.
This Clause and the next one also illustrate what I said about the strict maintenance of the control of the Court, because, as the House will see, a report has
to be made to the Lord Chief Justice on
any such case. An appeal lies from the
decision of the Committee to the Lord
Chief Justice. That would seem to afford
complete protection for the person
charged, very much in the same way as
the ordinary person can appeal from a
magistrate to a higher authority.
Clause 25 deals with one or two matters
of detail, which may perhaps give some
little trouble. I may as well confess at
once that it will be necessary to get rid
of the italics. I do not know how they
crept in, but I suspect the printer. It is
not now the practice to use italics in
presenting a Bill to Parliament, except
in relation to a Money Resolution. More
important than that is an arrangement
as to documents produced or required to
be produced before a Committee of
Inquiry. I am not certain that the
Government, in spite of the benevolence
of their disposition towards the Bill for
which I appealed, will be prepared to go
quite so far as the words that are in-
Icluded in this Clause. I am hoping that
one may get an almost clear lead from
the Government before we finally part
with the Bill, and any representation
made on that subject will certainly
receive the respect it deserves, and the
House will be asked to act upon it.
In Clause 29 again we have the matter
of privilege, which may give some little
difficulty. The provisions of the Bill do
not accord exactly with those in the
English or Scottish Measures. Indeed,
the English Measures are remarkably
silent on the point, and the House may
be advised to make some change there
in a matter of detail, but it will not in
Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
8 JUNE 1937
any way affect the main principles of
the Measure.
I now pass to Part III of the Bill,
which in Northern Ireland is entirely
new. Part III relates to regulations as
to the keeping of accounts and professional conduct. So far as the keeping of
accounts is concerned, it is based entirely
on the later English Act of 1933, and
that later Act of 1933 was, I find from
the Parliamentary Debates, asked for, or
something of that nature was asked for,
when the earlier Consolidating Act of
1932 was before the House of Commons
at Westminster. Hon. Members who
read the provisions of this Part for themselves will, I think, be bound to agree
with me that what it amounts to is giving
protection to the client, and that it almost
goes so far as to make the bank in which
the money is deposited a trustee for him.
There is more power for the Society
to ensure the safe deposit of money for
clients of solicitors, the lack of which
has previously caused some few of them
to mourn, although cases of matters of
that kind going wrong in Northern Ireland have been extremely rare.
There is one exception-paragraph (c).
It deals with the professional practice,
conduct, and discipline of solicitors, and
is distinct from the main purpose of the
Clause, which is the matter of financing.
Those words do not occur in the English
Act of 1933, but I think the House will
agree that they are very desirable. There
is nothing there which can put any impediment in the path of the honest
solicitor, but there is much there which,
if it is properly administered, as it will
be, should make the path of the transgressor considerably harder than it is at
present. I think that is all I need say
to the House on Part III. The hon.
and learned Member on my left (Mr.
McAllister) calls my attention to Clause
38. That Clause deals really with a
person who is not qualified to act as a
solicitor, but does so act, and it is, of
course, necessary that he should be dealt
Iwith in the Bill before us. The corresponding passage in the English Act of
1898 is Section 53.
I now turn to Part IV, Clause 49.
Some slight explanation is due to the
House on that Clause. I shall not go
into it deeply at the moment, but it is
sufficient to say that the necessity for
the insertion of this Clause is due to a
case decided in England in 1934, which
was subsequently dealt with by an Act
of Parliament of that year. Leaving
that comparatively trivial matter, and
coming to Clause 51, that is a provision
for the acceleration of the remedy of a
solicitor in the case of a client obviously
about to default. An exactly similar
Clause will be found in Section 65 of
the English Act of 1932.
Going to the next Clause, Clause 52,
dealing with the authority for making
General Orders under the Solicitors'
Remuneration Act of 1881, the Clause
in the Bill simply reproduces as nearly
as may be the original tribunal set up
by the Act of 1881. In 1881, speaking
as regards Ireland, the authorities were
the Lord Chancellor, the Lord Chief
Justice, the Master of the Rolls, and the
President of the Incorporated Law
Society, although he was not then known
by that name. We have not got either
a Lord Chancellor or a Master of the
Rolls, and therefore we have adopted the
procedure of giving the power to the
Lord Chief Justice and one other Judge
appointed by that Lord Chief Justice,
and the President of the Incorporated
Law Society, a course which, I hope,
will meet with the approval of the
House.
I pass now to the First Schedule,
which deals with the fees payable to the
Incorporated Law Society. I find that
these fees are, with one very trivial
exception, which I will mention in detail
because it of some importance, exactly
the same fees as those decided upon by
the appropriate authority in November,
1918. There is no increase. The only
change is in regard to item 5, which has
introduced the expression "prescribed
examination" in lieu of the expression
"intermediate examination," which formerly appeared.
I hope I have not trespassed unduly
on the indulgence of the House. I ask
the House to agree that the Bill, speaking
for itself, has made out its case, and that
the time has come when our own Incorporated Law Society should be equipped
for the better performance of its arduous
task in the future than it has been in
the past. I do not propose that the
Committee stage should be taken until
after the Summer Recess, in order that
the fullest time may be given for the
consideration of the various details.
Solicitors Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
There will be some details for consideration in Committee. The House will
recognise that in the case of a Bill where
so much collation has to be done between
that Act and this Statutory Order and
the Bill some amendment of details is
inevitable, but I ask the House to read
the Bill a Second Time in order that the
Incorporated Law Society may be able
to continue its beneficient work with renewed and increased vigour. I beg to
move, "that the Bill be now read a
Second Time."
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM:
Although this Bill contains a large number of Clauses, I am going to ask the
hon. and learned Senator who is responsible for its introduction to consider the
addition of another Clause. The Clause
I refer to deals with the furnishing of
accounts by solicitors. In my lifetime I
have come across several cases where lawyers' accounts were never furnished to
clients for years. In one particular case
I knew of the account was not rendered
for over twenty years, and the solicitors
held a lien on documents against that
account until it was settled. The Incorporated Law Society might introduce a
rule or regulation under which a solicitor would have to furnish his account
to his client within a reasonable time
after the case was completed. It might
not be convenient to accept payment, but
the account should be furnished within
three or six months, and unless that was
done the solicitor should not be allowed
to have a lien on any documents that
were in his hands.
It would be no hardship on the
attorney, because it would enable him
to get in his accounts, and to the general
public it would be a very great benefit.
A person would know that the account
for a particular case was not being mixed
up with an account for something else.
I would ask the hon. and learned Member to take into serious consideration the
insertion in the Bill of a Clause to this
effect. It would be of great benefit to
the public, and it would not be a disadvantage but rather an advantage to the
solicitor himself.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I hope Senator
Leslie will give serious consideration to
the suggestion made by Senator Samuel
Cunningham. It reminds me of a case I
came across not very long ago where a
client had actually paid to his solicitor
the costs of an agreement, but the
solicitor kept a lien on the document.
Then the client had the audacity to go
to another solicitor, and when the latter
wrote to the first solicitor who held the
agreement he refused to hand over the
document unless he received a certain
sum. I discussed the matter with the
second solicitor who had been employed,
and he said there was a custom in that
district whereby a client leaving
solicitor was punished by a fine. I do
not think any solicitor has any right to
do such a thing, and I think it is high
time the public were protected from such
a practice. Solicitors should not hold
documents unless they have a written request from their client to do so. Therefore I hope the suggestion made by
Senator Samuel Cunningham will be
incorporated in the Bill.

(Finance) Bill, and the Appropriation

New Industries (Development) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The Government in framing this Bill have had the advantage of
the experience gained in the administration of the New Industries (Development)
Act of 1932.
The purpose of the Bill is the same as
that underlying the 1932 Act. It is to
encourage industrialists to establish new
industries in Northern Ireland so that
additional employment may be provided
and the basis of the economic life of the
Province broadened.
Hon. Members are aware that the effects
of the economic crisis through which the
world was passing when the Act of 1932
was passed, were most oppressive to the
two industries of textile manufacture
and shipbuilding upon which those
engaged in industry and commerce in
Northern Ireland are largely dependent
and the desirability of increasing the
number and variety of our industries was
clearly demonstrated.
The Act of 1932, while it assisted in
bringing employment in new industries
to some 200 persons, was not so successful
as had been hoped. Experience has
shown that the offer of a free site and of
exemption of premises from rates for 20
years do not provide in themselves a
sufficient inducement to most industrialists who are considering the setting up of
a new factory to set it up in this area.
Furthermore, in framing the 1932 Act,
the Government thought it necessary, in
order to protect industries already established, to restrict the benefits of the Act
to persons proposing to produce goods of
a kind which were not being produced
in Northern Ireland when the Act came
into force. In the Bill now before this
House this limitation is not made, but
Clause 1 avoids the danger of subsidising
new undertakings to compete with old by
laying down that the Ministry of Commerce and its advisory committee shall
apply certain tests in determining
whether a new enterprise qualifies for
assistance. The tests are:-Is the enterprise likely to make a substantial addition
to the existing sum total of employment,
and is the additional employment likely
to be continuing.
As regards the inducements offered under
the Bill, it is proposed to offer, as was
done by the 1932 Act, a free grant in
respect of site value up to a maximum
period of twenty years, and in cases
where premises are rented, to substitute
for a grant in respect of the site, a free
grant not exceeding the fair rent of the
premises. This latter grant will be made
by way of annual payments over a
maximum period of five years.
The facilities I have described are those
available to successful applicants who are
able to find existing premises to suit
their needs. Applicants who have to
adapt premises to their requirements
may in addition be given a loan free of
New Industries Bill
Unemployment Assistance Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
interest for a period of five years of the
sum required to carry out the necessary
works of adaptation. At the end of the
interest-free period they would be required to repay the loan, but not the
grant in respect of the premises in instalments together with interest counting
from the end of the first five years.
Finally, successful applicants who have
either to purchase existing or to erect
new premises may obtain in addition to
the twenty years site grant, a loan free
of interest for five years, of a sum equal
to the purchase price or to the cost of
building the premises. In such cases.
also, applicants would be required to
repay the loan, but not the grant, together with interest accruing after the
expiration of the free-of-interest period.
The aggregate total amount which the
Ministry of Finance is authorised to lend
under the Bill is £200,000. As in the
1932 Act, local authorities would be
empowered to exempt from the payment
of rates any undertaking in respect of
which a grant or loan had been made
under the Act-the period of exemption
not to exceed the period of the currency
of the grant, or, where a loan is made,
the period between the date on which
the loan is made and the date fixed for
the repayment of the final instalment.
These are the main features of the Bill.
There are two other provisions, however, to
which I should like to draw the attention
of hon. Members. The first is that the
Ministry of Commerce is empowered to
take into consideration in deciding to
what extent an applicant should be
assisted; whether there are any circumstances which may make it desirable that
his undertaking shall be established in
some particular district rather than in
another. The second is that the Ministry is empowered to advertise the
facilities offered by the Act.
I believe that the Bill will commend
itself to hon. Members as an attempt,
which I think deserves to succeed, to
bring new industries to Northern Ireland.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, motion agreed to.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now committed."
Mr. LESLIE: Although I propose to
give my Friend, the Parliamentary
Secretary, the usual back-handed support he expects on these occasions, I cannot refrain from reminding the House
that when the former Bill of 1932 was
before us we did put it through the
Committee stage. I do not think myself
there is the least object in going through
that formality this afternoon, because I
do not think there is anything in the
Bill anybody would want to amend.
On Question, Motion negatived.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Mr. Long.)

"That this Bill do now

Unemployment Assistance (Finance)
(No. 2) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: As this is a financial
Measure and contains nothing new in
principle, I shall make my explanations
as brief as possible.
The Unemployment Assistance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1934, provided that
as from the second Appointed Day, when
the relief of a considerable number of
persons would under the Act be taken
over by the Unemployment Assistance
Board from the Boards of Guardians,
with consequent relief to ratepayers,
contributions, on a scale laid down for
three years in the Act, should be paid
by local authorities towards the expenses
of Unemployed Assistance.
As hon. Members are aware, the second
Appointed Day was postponed from 1st
April, 1934, to 1st April, 1937. During
that period the unemployment situation
has improved, and the Government has
accordingly reduced the amount of the
contributions as laid down in the 1934
Act by approximately one quarter and,
as a result of representations made by the
Belfast Authority, has made a special
reduction in the amount to be contributed by them.
Suggestions made. by the county
councils in regard to the methods to be
Roads Bill-
Second Reading
29 JUNE 1937
adopted for the collection and appropriation of contributions have been incorporated in the Bill. It is for this reason
that contributions from urban districts
will be paid direct instead of through
the county councils, and the moneys
collected will be appropriated in aid of
the relevant Vote, instead of paid into
the Exchequer. I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed.”—(Mr. Long.)

Roads Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): The main purpose of this
Bill is to facilitate the Ministry of Home
Affairs in the provision of new roads.
The Ministry's existing powers to build
new roads are derived from the development and Road Improvement Funds Act,
1919, and are difficult to apply, as the
machinery of the Act is cumbrous and
out of date, particularly in regard to
the compulsory acquisition of land, which
can only be effected under the complicated Land Clauses Acts.
The Bill will provide new machinery
to enable the Ministry to construct any
new roads which are needed to facilitate
traffic and which by their position and
function should properly be made by the
Government rather than by local authorities. Such roads will become, on completion, the property of the county,
county borough, or urban district in
which they are situated, and their maintenance will be the responsibility of the
councils with assistance from the Road
Fund. It is provided, however, that no
entrances to the roads may be made without the approval of the Ministry. This
condition is necessary in order that the
through traffic for which roads are mainly
necessary shall not be unduly impeded.
In regard to the acquisition of land,
the relevant provisions of the Local
Government Act
Act (Northern Ireland),
1934, have been adopted with the alterations necessary to enable them to be used
by the Ministry. It is proposed that all
expenses shall be defrayed out of the
Road Fund, either directly or out of
money borrowed on the security of the
fund.
The matters dealt with in Clauses 4 and
5 of the Bill are not concerned with the
construction of new roads. When a road
is opened by a public utility undertaking
for the purpose of repairing or introducing wires, pipes or the like, or for the purpose of inspecting its property, it is normally obliged to repair the road. This
is not so, however, in the case when the
road has been opened by a sanitary
authority.
Clause 4 is designed to remove this
anomaly by obliging sanitary authorities.
who open county council roads to repair
them to the satisfaction of the authority.
Clause 5 assigns to courts of summary
jurisdiction the duty of assessing the
damage done by a county surveyor or by
a contractor when obtaining materials for
the repair of roads if the amount cannot
be agreed upon by the parties. The existing procedure, which has proved to be too
slow for present-day conditions, is that
the matter is decided by three substantial
householders, selected in the manner laid
down in the Grand Jury Act of 1836. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not know whether
other Members of the House who read the
Bill found it a difficult Bill to follow. I
must confess that I myself found it, and
still find it, a somewhat difficult Bill, and
if it is difficult for us I think we must
remember that it must have been rather
a difficult Bill to arrange for and to draft.
It is not going to be a very easy Bill to
discuss, and it certainly will not, in my
judgment, prove to be an easy Bill to
administer. The fact is really-the noble
Viscount hinted at it, though he did
not say so that this Bill is an attempt to
put new wine into an old bottle, and it
happens that the old bottle is the Act of
1909, to which the noble Viscount referred.
Roads Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
That Act was one of a series of Acts
passed in a period of Parliamentary history which, perhaps, did not appeal to
many of us, and I do not think and I
have said so before in this House-that
that Act of 1909 was at all a model to be
followed. However, the proposal now is to
some extent to depart from it, and I think
we can best see what departures there are,
and what the new proposals are, by going
through the Bill. I think, perhaps, the
House will pardon me if I go rather into
detail, because I understand that it is
desired to get this Bill, or so much of it
as this House may leave intact, through
before Wednesday of next week. I understand it is desired to take the Committee
Stage of the Bill on Thursday and the
Report and Third Reading stages on
Friday or perhaps Monday. Consequently
time is rather short.
me
I will, therefore, ask the House to do
the honour of accompanying me
through the Bill itself. To begin with
the object of the Bill is not so much to
create new means of communication between point A and point B; it is rather
to facilitate traffic between these two
points, though probably in a roundabout
direction. That is, the Bill is more
directed to securing the construction of
by-pass roads than it is to the construction of what I may describe as new means
of communication altogether. I hope I
have made myself clear. The point is,
I think, the Bill is directed rather to the
provision of by-pass roads than to the
provision of roads for any other purposes.
Also I think the Bill is probably intended to be limited to that purpose, and
that it is not intended out of the powers
to be given by this Bill to make any
special arrangement for the promotion.
of road racing by motor-cars in Northern
Ireland. Anything of that sort, I think,
will be outside the provisions of this
particular Bill. That is the main object
of the Bill-the facilitation of through
traffic.
That at once, I think, brings us to the
consideration of the first great change
the Bill is to make. Under the Act of
1909, as the House will remember, power
was given to the Road Board, as it then
was, with the consent of the Development
Commissioners. In England, since then,
the Road Board has become the Ministry of Transport, and in Northern Ireland the Ministry of Home Affairs. The
Development Commissioners in Northern
Ireland and the Treasury, which came
into the Act of 1909, are both represented by the Ministry of Finance. But
the original power was to the Road
Board to construct a road and to keep
it; that is to say, it did not pass over
to the county councils. There was
similar power given the highway
authority. The power of the highway
authority to construct a new road is
entirely unaffected by the Bill before us.
The Bill is to give the Ministry power
to make a new road and then pass it
over to the county council for maintenance. There is nothing to prevent
the county council from making a road.
under existing powers for itself. Consequently you may have constructed by the
Ministry under this Bill what I may call
a composite road; that is, a road passing
through the areas of two or three
authorities. Whether that will bring
its own difficulties in its train or not I do
not know. I am inclined to think it
may. On the other hand, I imagine no
new road will be constructed in any area
without the consent of the councils
through whose area the road is to pass.
I imagine also the Roads Advisory
Council is likely to have something to
say on the matter. I do not suppose
there is any intention to make new roads
and cram them down the throats
into the pockets, which is more to the
point, of reluctant county councils.
or
So much for the first two sub-clauses
of the first Clause. Now, we come to
the proviso to the first sub-clause, and
this is really, to my mind, one of the
most difficult things in the Bill. The
House will, perhaps, remember that a
somewhat similar proviso in the Act of
1909 was affirmatively expressed. What
it said, if I can trust my memory, was
that communications between the road
and other roads, paths, lands, premises,
and so forth should be made in manner
approved by the Ministry. The Bill
in this case uses the negative form
and says these communications shall not
be made except with the approval of the
Ministry. The effect of that is, that as the
Bill stands, it would be perfectly possible
for the road to be constructed from
which the frontagers would be entirely
cut off. The question whether the new
frontage was, or was not to have access
Roads Bill-
Second Reading
29 JUNE 1937
to the new road, seems to rest entirely
on the whim of the Ministry. I do not
believe that is the intention of the Bill,
but I do suggest that that is what it
says. I quite agree with what the noble
Viscount says as regards the necessity of
protecting the new by-pass road from
negligent ingress from the frontages. If
you are really to have a by-pass road you
cannot have it interrupted every five
yards or so by roads coming into it.
There must be control. Then we come
to the question of whose control-duality
of control or not. The responsibility of
deciding is in the Bill cast on the
shoulders of the Ministry. The road
itself, that is, the new road, has passed
away from the Ministry. It has gone
to the county councils.
Again we have the necessity of maintaining control as to ingress in this new
road. We ought to provide some sort of
penalty for the transgressor. There is
no penalty for breaking into the road.
We have the penalty in the previous
paragraph of existing road enactments.
The only enactment I can think of is
that paragraph in the Summary Jurisdiction Act of 1851, which provides the
extreme penalty of 20s on a person who
breaks a hole in a hedge without the
permission of the county surveyor.
Accordingly I propose to put something
on the Paper for the Committee stage
really comprehensive, which will serve as
a subject for discussion, that the Ministry, before it declares that the road has
been completed, shall provide suitable
means of communication between the
land and premises and the new road, and
after that has been done nobody shall
make a new form of entry without the
consent of the Ministry and the county
council in whom the road is vested.
Further, I propose to suggest that we
should provide a suitable penalty for the
infraction of the new enactment, and
that penalty would be to my mind as a
maximum not 20s but £20, and also there
should be the cost of compensation which
would naturally be the closing of the unlawfully made entry and generally to
make matters straight. We shall see in
a minute how wide this question of entry
may be.
We come now to the second Clause,
which again presents some difficulties to
my mind. The first Sub-Clause provides
that when the Ministry of Finance,
taking the place of the old Commissioners, have approved of a proposal to
construct a road the Ministry may
acquire land that is necessary for the
purpose. But they go on to say they
may in addition acquire land on either
side of the proposed road within 220
yards from the middle of the proposed
road. I think we must look at that a
little in detail, because it will excite a
good deal of comment outside. First of
all let me remind the House that 220
yards is the maximum quantity, and the
Ministry are not bound to take the full
220 yards. Further, if they do take the
220 yards or whatever quantity they do
take they are to measure it from the
middle of the road and not from the
extreme verge. Now I think we have to
inquire for what purpose they want this
220 yards. It was put into the Act of
1909, an Act to which I have already
referred in terms not wholly of approval.
I do not know why it was put in. It is
nearly 30 years ago. But when speaking
on the subject of that Bill, Mr. Lloyd
George, then Chancellor of the Exchequer, spoke of the possibility of making
a new road for motors on which there
would be no speed limit. But I am not
at all certain that he had not in the
back of his mind something in the nature
of his land tax proposals, that he did not
want to collar the frontage value for the
benefit of the State. He said there should
be a power to acquire up to 220 yards.
If we are repeating that I want to know
what we are going to do with it.
Hon. Members will see if they turn to
the interpretation Clause that the expression "road" in the Bill before us includes
this extra land, which I will call the 220
yards, in the road, and, therefore, this
220 yards on the completion of the road
is to be transferred by the Ministry to the
county council. That would seem to be
the intention of the Bill. Now, what can
the Ministry want this land for? It cannot be for making a supplementary motor
road, say a road for swift traffic, as
opposed to the old one, because they can
do that under the ordinary power of taking land for making a new road.
seems to me it must be intended to be
used as protection against building right
up to the edge of the new road, and so to
enable the Ministry to construct in Northern Ireland roads similar to those very
It
Roads Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
fine roads which are beginning to appear
in England.
The North Road will probably occur to
most of us. I was on part of the North
Road a few days ago, and there must be
very nearly 220 yards on either side of the
middle of that road before you come to
the houses, and that 220 yards is laid out
in the fashion that used to be called
parterre; in fact, you have a very fine
road with a boulevard effect, because they
are planting trees there, though they have
not got very far yet. That may be the intention. That land, according to the Bill
as I read it, will be vested in the county
council, not in the Ministry. And, again,
we are not told in the Bill if this land is
to be acquired, when it is to be acquired.
Therefore, I propose to ask the House
in Committee if it is decided to maintain
the 220 yards application to insert words
to make sure that this extra land shall
only be acquired with the consent of the
Ministry of Finance in the same way as
the land for the road, and that if it is to
be acquired at all it shall be acquired.
before the completion of the new road is
certified. It seems to me that that last
condition is necessary as otherwise you
would take your new road and have your
frontages up to 220 yards with the possibility of compulsory acquisition hanging
over the heads of the people until the end
of time.
We
A further point arises on that.
will deal later on with the Clause in the
Bill which relates to repeals very much
in the same way as Section 11 of the Act
of 1909. That Section contained a proviso, which is not repeated in the Bill
before us, and the effect of that proviso
was to exempt from the compulsory operation of the Act certain classes of land
which were set out, I am speaking from
memory, in Section 5 of Part I. Those
classes of land were demesne land, gardens, land required for the amenity of a
dwelling-house, land occupied by a public
utility company, although it was described
in rather more lengthy terms and the site
of an ancient monument. None of that
is before us and the question is whether
we should not reinsert it, and if we do
whether we ought to insert it as regards
urban land or rural land, or both.
Curiously enough, I am inclined to think
that if we continue protection for rural
land we should do all that is necessary,
because I think the mere difficulties of the
situation and the expense would be
sufficient protection for the urban districts. I do not believe there are any
rural districts in Northern Ireland in
which you can make a new road and
acquire 220 yards on either side. However, that is a matter which ought to be
discussed with
some fulness in Committee.
The House will remember that I have
laid stress on the fact that this 220 yards
is to be handed over to the county
councils, and I want to draw attention for
a moment to Sub-clause (4) of the
Clause now before us. That is a
perfectly ordinary, normal Clause
which gives the Ministry power to
sell, lease, and manage any land
acquired under this Bill. I do not suppose that that is intended to apply to
this 220 yards. I imagine it is rather
intended to apply to a case in which the
Ministry in acquiring necessary land for
the road have had to take a bit more,
which so often happens. A man says,
"If you take so much of my land you
must take a bit more,'
" and the Ministry
is left with a little land on its hands
which it does not want, and therefore it
must do something about it. I believe
that is what sub-clause (4) is intended to
deal with.
We have to know what is to be done
with this 220 yards of road, where it is
acquired before the road is completed,
and not so much who is to pay for it,
but we must know what is to be done
about the entrances across it. That was
the matter to which I was referring a
minute or two ago. Take the North
Road in England. The houses are on
either side and garden territory is laid
out between the houses and the road
and there
All
are paths across it.
that has to be considered. I think the
House should be told something more
about it. We ought to consider what
protection we are going to give against
the compulsory acquisition of land which
is not intended for the road itself, but
as an addition to the amenities of the
road.
That is all I have to say as regards
Clauses 1 and 2, and I pass on to
Clause 4, the new Clause restraining the
sanitary authority in the matter of
breaking up the county road. I understand that representations have been
Second Reading
Roads Bill-

29 JUNE 1937
made to the Government by the county
councils on this subject already, and
that the Government intends to make
some proposals to the House in Committee to deal with the difficulty created
by the absence of definite requirements
in the Bill before us, that the road
authority should have notice of intention to break up the road. As that is
so, I need not to-day say anything more
about it except that we cannot pass the
Clause without sharing in the general
wonder that such a Clause should now be
necessary, that is, that the sanitary
authority should have escaped the policeman for so many years. It is really
rather ridiculous.
Now we come to Clause 5, which deals.
with compensation for stones, and so on,
when they are taken compulsorily by the
proper authority, and the compensation
for which is assessed in the manner mentioned by the noble Viscount. He did
not go into much detail. The three
householders are selected in this manner.
The man whose stones are taken appoints
one, the contractor or the county surveyor, as the case may be, appoints the
second, and a neighbouring justice of
the peace appoints the third. That was
the condition that obtained until 1935
when, in what I might perhaps venture
to call a non-lucid interval, this Parliament took away powers from the justices
of the peace, and so far as this particular
question was concerned made no provision.
Therefore I presume that at the
moment the third member of this
tribunal is to be appointed by the
resident magistrate. I think that must
be so. Under the old grand jury powers
this was the power and duty of the justice
of the peace.
These three men were to
go and inspect the land before the stone
was taken; again, they were to inspect
the land after the stone was taken, and
then they were to announce the result
of their deliberations. That is now to
be swept away, and the decision of the
resident magistrate is to be substituted.
Speaking not perhaps entirely without
experience of these matters, I do not
believe that anybody will be a penny
the worse for the alteration, but there
may be people who think they will be
put in a worse position, and therefore I
cannot agree to these words at the conclusion of the second sub-clause, that the
decision of the resident magistrate "shall
be final and conclusive." The difference,
if there is still a difference, between the
owner of the stone and the county council
or contractor is surely a difference of a
civil nature, and where a court of summary jurisdiction has to decide a case of
a civil nature from the old days of 1851,
and even through the tragedy of 1935,
the decision of that court of summary
jurisdiction is open to appeal. It cannot
become effective without an order, and
every order is open to appeal. Accordingly, I shall ask the House in Committee
to reject a line and a half of the text,
and that. I think, will have the effect
that we desire.
I pass now to my final observations.
It is rather a little curiosity. If the
House will be so kind as to turn to
Clause 7 they will see that it is proposed
to repeal sub-sections 1, 5 and 6 of
Section 11, and part of sub-section 4.
Sub-section 5, as it seems to me, ought
to be put into the same category as subsection 4, that is to say, we should only
repeal so much of it as relates to purchases of land by the Ministry. The
reason for that is that under the Act of
1909 the highway authority-for practical
purposes the county councils-have
authority as regards compulsory acquisition, and so on. If we leave the text of
the Bill as it comes before us, it seems
to me that we shall be depriving our
county councils of that power. I think
that if we are going to do that we ought
at least to be told why it is going to be
done. All that is required, it seems to
me, is a slight verbal alteration, and I
think the House will very readily make
it, and make it the more readily, I cannot but believe, when they remember
that the highway authority, which was
originally the county councils, and so on,
has by a later Act of our own Parliament
-the Drainage Act of 1933, I thinkbeen made for some purposes to include
the Ministry of Home Affairs themselves.
Whether it is really the intention of the
Bill that the Ministry should lose the
power which they took as recently as
1933 is a matter which they can, if they
desire, explain. I think that the House
will consider that the powers left with
the highway authority under that subsection should be preserved for them,
and therefore will make the necessary
alteration.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
should like to say a word or two in support of this Bill. I feel that in this
Measure the Government are obtaining
powers for the construction of roads that
will be for the benefit of the people, particularly those round the city of Belfast.
At the present time the city has been held
up considerably owing to roads passing
through county council property and
Corporation property. For instance, we
have a Circular Road that was to be constructed round the whole of the city of
Belfast. That undertaking has been held.
up at one particular place because of the
fact that the Antrim County Council owns
part of the road and the Corporation the
other part. If the Government obtain
these powers for constructing roads they
will be able to assist the Corporation in
creating a fine circular road, and will
enable them to develop and open up
land for the benefit of the citizens.
There is one thing I think the Government should be very careful about, that
is, they should look well ahead, and not
content themselves with making roads
suitable only for present-day traffic. They
should see that roads are constructed to
provide for the increase in traffic that may
take place in the future. I suppose this
Bill gives the Government power for the
construction of this road that has been
spoken of, linking up Belfast with the
County Down. That is certainly a very
good idea the building of a road leading
out of Belfast so as to relieve the congestion about Holywood Arches and the Newtownards Road. But unless the Government build a bridge as a direct entrance
to that new road it will not serve the purpose in the way that is necessary. The
Queen's Bridge at the present time is terribly congested; it is a very dangerous
Roads Bill

spot, and the same observation applies to
Short Strand and the Newtownards
Road.
If they build a bridge directly opposite
High Street leading on to this new road
it will be one of the greatest benefits the
Government could confer on the citizens
of Belfast. For traffic leading in and
out of the city it is an absolute necessity.
There may be considerable difficulty in
being able to persuade the Harbour
authorities to allow a bridge to be built
there, but the Government is the Government, and it should see that it does
what is really necessary for the greatest
number. I believe the greatest number
would be in favour of this bridge being

Colonel Thomas Sinclair took the Oath.
Question to Minister.
MILK HAULIERS IN CUSHENDALL
DISTRICT.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether it is a fact
that notice has been given to milk hauliers
in the Cushendall district that their businesses are being taken over on Friday,
and that if they carry milk after that
date they will be liable to forfeit their
compensation; and, if so, whether the
Government will make representations to
the Transport Board to assure that
adequate measures are taken to carry the
milk of the farmers in that district after
that date.
With reference to the question I put
to the House, I may say great inconvenience will be caused to farmers by this
proposed change. Private motor owners
call at different farms on the by-pass
roads as well as on the main roads. It
is reported that when the Transport
Board take over this service they will
merely collect milk on the main roads,
and the farmers will require to cart the
milk a distance of half a mile, or more
in some cases, to the main road. The
farmers have not as yet been notified as
to the scale of charges to be made by the
Transport Board in the event of their
taking over this service. In the district
to which I refer some of the motor owners
have been notified that they must cease
collecting milk to-morrow, Friday, otherwise their claim for compensation will not
be entertained. That is one reason why
I wish this House to take some action
and appeal to the proper authority to
stay their hands in this matter. Further,
that they should stay their hands in view
of the recent statement by the Prime
Minister that he would consider any complaints or grievances in the Province in
regard to motor hauliers and the Transport Board charges. On those grounds I
appeal to the House to take some action
to stay the hands of the proper authority
until something is done to relieve the
great inconvenience which the farmers and
milk producers will be put to.

CLAUSE 1 (Powers of the Ministry of
Home Affairs as to construction of new
roads)-
*
*
*
(2) A road constructed by the Ministry under the provisions of this Act
shall be a public road and shall, to
the extent to which it is situated in
any county, county borough, or urban
district, be vested in and maintainable
by the council thereof as from the date
of the completion of the road as certified by the Ministry, and the enactments relating to public roads shall
apply accordingly:
Provided that communications between any roads, paths, lands or premises and a road constructed by the
Ministry shall not be made except with
the approval of the Ministry.
*
*
*-
*
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie:
Page 2, line 3, leave out from "that"
to the end of line 6, and insert-
"the Ministry, before certifying the
completion of the road, shall make or
open such communications between the
road and adjacent roads, paths, lands
or premises, as may be reasonably
necessary, and that, after the completion of the road has been so certified,
it shall not be lawful to make or open
any new communications as aforesaid
without the consent of the Ministry
and the council in which the road is
vested.
(2) If any person unlawfully makes
or opens any such new communication
as aforesaid, such person shall be liable,
on summary conviction, to a fine not
exceeding twenty pounds, and, in
addition, to pay such sum by way of
compensation as the court hearing the
case may consider just."
Mr. LESLIE: I mentioned this as a
matter of some considerable difficulty,
when speaking in the House on the Second
Reading of the Bill. The object of the
text is, of course, to prevent unnecessary
and unreasonable access to a road made
for facilitating road traffic, commonly
called a by-pass road. As far as that
goes it will, I think, commend itself to
the sympathy of us all. There is no use
having a by-pass road to facilitate traffic
if you are going to allow it to be interrupted by entry every few yards, as is
done in some cases with the main roads
Roads Bill-
Committee
1 JULY 1937
to-day. But there are limits to the concessions the public should be asked to
make for the use of roads for motor
traffic. The Committee may remember
that the somewhat similar proviso that
occurred in the Act of 1909 read
follows:
as
The communication between a road
or path, and a road constructed by the
Road Board, shall be made in manner
to be approved by the Road Board.
That, as it seems to me, plainly meant
that the Road Board, which is succeeded
by our Ministry of Home Affairs, expected means of communication to be
made; in fact, it was not an unreasonable
expectation. But in the Bill before us it
has been thought advisable for various
reasons into which I need not enter, because they are for the Parliamentary
Secretary, rather than for me, to alter
that wording and adopt the negative
phrase instead of the affirmative. As it
stands, the suggestion in the Bill plainly
is that communications are not to be
made except with approval. They can
be approved, but the indication there is
that the fewer there are of them the
better, with which I am rather inclined
to agree.
There are four points to be considered.
Let us see what is going to happen. As
a convenient illustration I am going to
take the table of this House, going in
that direction, and I will ask the Committee to imagine a man has land behind
the table and in front of the table, that
a new road is made, represented by the
table, and that on each side of it land
is taken under the 220 yards power un
to this first bench. Behind that there
is land again. I have suggested that the
man's land should be behind and before
the table, and that the road should be
the table going that way, and verges, of
course, that
and that way.
way
The
man will be cut off from one part of his
land. The road has gone straight
through his farm, or wherever it may be.
He has got to have access from one side
to the other, but he need not have it
every five yards.
It might very well be that part of that
220 yards might be used up in making a
side service road with cross-roads here
and there connecting with the new road,
because across that new road by some
means or other that man has got to get.
For such means of communication there
is no provision whatever in the Bill, and
the first object of my Amendment is to
ensure that it shall be the duty of the
Ministry which is making this new road
to provide such means of communication
from one side to the other as may
reasonably be necessary.
The Committee will remember, of
course, that there is one very essential
difference between what we are proposing
to do and the existing powers under the
Act of 1909. Under the Act of 1909
the Road Board, whose place has been
taken by the Ministry, had power to
make a road, and it had to keep and
maintain that road. In the Bill before
us a road, when made, will be handed
over to the County Council. The
essential difference is that under the 1909
scheme the action was taken or not taken
by a Minister responsible to Parliament.
Under the Bill before us we shall no
longer have a Minister responsible to
Parliament for the future management
of the road, but we shall have a county
council, which is not responsible for it.
I think we are entitled to insist that
before a man gives up land for a road he
shall have means of access provided from
one side to the other, but we are also
entitled to insist from the very
necessities of the case, that those means
of access shall not be unreasonable; that
there shall not be means of entry to this
new road every five yards. Although
five yards is an extreme case, in the
country you do find farm gates opening
on to the road every hundred vards, and
very often there is a little lane going
from the farm which results in those very
dangerous corners we do not want to
reproduce. It is imperative that this
means of access should be provided to
start with, although it may be restricted.
Then, if a new communication is opened
contrary to the provisions of the Bill we
require some penalty which will operate
to restrain a person from doing so. Under
the present law the only penalty I can
discover is the possibility of fining a man
twenty shillings, and that seems to be
inefficient. But we are entitled to insist
that before the Ministry declares a road
completed and vested in the county
council the new road shall be provided
with all reasonable means of access for
the service of the frontagers on both sides,
Roads Bill-

I may want to build a terrace of houses,

whether the people, through whose land

CLAUSE 2 (Acquisition of land).
(1) Where the Ministry of Finance has
approved of a proposal by the Ministry
to construct a new road under this Act
the Ministry may acquire land for the
purpose, and may, in addition, acquire
land on either side of the proposed
road within two hundred and twenty
yards from the middle of the proposed
road.
(2) For the purpose of the purchase
of land by agreement under this
section the Land Clauses Acts shall be
incorporated with this Act, except the
provisions of those Acts with respect
to the purchase and taking of land
otherwise than by agreement.
(3) Where the Ministry is unable to
purchase by agreement on reasonable
terms any land which it considers
necessary, the Ministry shall have
power to acquire the said land compulsorily by means of an order (in this
Act referred to as a "vesting order")
made by the Ministry in accordance
with the provisions contained in Part I
of the Schedule to this Act; and the
provisions of the Local Government
Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, which
are specified in Part II of the Schedule
to this Act shall have effect in relation
to every vesting order, subject to the
modifications and adaptations set out in
the said Part II.

Mr. LONG: The object of this Amendment would appear to be to make provision requiring the consent of the Ministry of Finance to the acquisition of any
land that may be taken by the Ministry
of Home Affairs on either side of a road,
and to provide further that such land can
only be taken by the Ministry before the
road is completed. Such provision is
unnecessary, as the Clause as it stands
already adequately covers the matter.
Any land that the Ministry purchases
under the Bill will have to be paid for
out of the Road Fund and no payment
of this character can be made by the
Ministry of Home Affairs out of the Road
Fund without the authority of the Ministry of Finance.
The Clause in the Bill enables the
Ministry of Home Affairs to acquire land
on either side of a proposed road; it does
not afford authority to acquire land on
either side of a completed or existing
road, and there is no intention on the
part of the Ministry of attempting to
do so.
I might add that there is not and never
will be any attempt by the Government
to acquire land after the road is completed.

The first thing I have to point out
is that there is a misprint on the Paper.
The Road Improvement Funds Act is
referred to as of 1929. It ought to be
1909. That is a small matter. The real
point is that such provision as I suggest
did occur in the Development Act of
1909, and it does not occur in the Bill
before us. I want to know why. Now
the actual provisions in the Act of 1909
are as follow:
Provided that the provisions of Part I
of this Act, prohibiting the compulsory
acquisition of the classes of land
mentioned in sub-section (2) of Section 5
of this Act shall apply to the acquisition
by the Road Board of land on either side
of a road proposed to be constructed by

as
my
They did not differentiate,
amendment
proposes to differentiate,
between land in an urban district and

what these classes of land were the
classes of land referred to in sub-section
(2) of Section 5 of the Development Act.
The classes were:-
"No land shall be authorised by an
order under this Section to be acquired
compulsorily which, at the date of the
order, forms part of any park, garden,
or pleasure ground, or forms part of the
home farm attached to and usually
occupied with a mansion house, or 18
otherwise required for the amenity or
convenience of any dwelling-house, or
which at that date is the property of any
local authority, or has been acquired by
any corporation or company for the purposes of a railway, dock, canal, water, or
other public undertaking, or is the site
of an ancient monument or other object
or archæological interest."
These classes of land could not be compulsorily acquired under the Act of 1909,
but these classes of land will be acquired
or can be acquired when the Bill of 1937
becomes an Act. I have drawn the
amendment so as to limit myself to reproducing the restrictions in the case of
rural districts. I have not troubled about
the urban districts, because I think the
interests of the owner will be sufficiently
protected by the extraordinary cost which
would be incurred if you acquired land
in an urban district. I have also had
regard to the fact that you are much
more likely to require compulsory powers
in the urban district in the different conditions there from the conditions in the
country. In the urban district it is
almost bound to happen that the premises
are owned by one person and occupied by
another. In the rural districts the
occupier and the owner will in 99 cases
out of 100 be the same.

the arbitrator, in fixing compensation,

CLAUSE 4 (Restoration of public
roads broken up by sanitary authority).
Where a sanitary authority, acting
in the exercise of any power conferred
upon them by the public Health
(Ireland) Acts, 1878 to 1918, break up,
for the purpose of constructing or
maintaining a sewer, a public road
maintainable by a county council, the
sanitary authority shall repair the
road to the satisfaction of the county
council by whom it is maintainable;
and, if the road is not repaired to the
satisfaction of the county council, that
council may cause the necessary repairs to be done and may charge the
costs against the sanitary authority,
and those costs shall be a debt due
from the authority to the council.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move in Clause
4, after "council" ("of the county
council that") to
to insert the words
"within a reasonable time."
While this Amendment appears first in
the Bill it must logically be considered
after the Amendment adding two paragraphs at the end of the Clause, and it is
desirable, therefore, to deal with both
these Amendments at the same time.
Clause 4 deals with the restoration of
roads under the care of the county council
Roads Bill—
Committee
1 JULY 1937
which may be required to be broken up
by the sanitary authority, that is to say,
the rural district council, and the present
position is that while there is adequate.
provision in the matter where the opening of the road is required for gas or water
pipes there is no adequate provision in the
case of sewer pipes. Representations
were made to the Ministry a considerable
time ago on this subject, and it was to
meet these that Clause 4, in the form that
it now appears in the Bill, was designed.
Since the introduction of the Bill, however, further representations have been.
made to the Ministry that owing to certain
arrangements that have been made for
bringing of water from the Silent Valley
reservoir to Belfast there is a possibility
of very numerous sewerage schemes being
undertaken in many rural districts, and
it is, therefore, of most urgent importance
that some further provision should be
made to secure, as far as possible, that
roads will not be opened and left in such
condition as to impede traffic for any
longer time than is absolutely necessary.
On consideration of these representations, the Ministry came to the conclusion
that it would be desirable to provide certain safeguards, and these now appear
in the form of the Amendments to this
Clause. Their effect is to extend the provisions of the Clause so that before a sanitary authority proceed to break up a road
the county surveyor shall be given an
opportunity of advising them as to any
special circumstances that may be affected
by such work, and, secondly, to ensure
that when a road has been opened it shall
be restored to a satisfactory condition as
soon as possible. It is obviously impracticable to lay down any definite period
for the restoration of the road, and,
accordingly, the Clause would now provide
that the sanitary authority must restore
the road within a reasonable time and the
county council may appeal to the Ministry
of Home Affairs to determine what time
is reasonable in the particular circumstances of the case.
Mr. LESLIE: Speaking on both of the
Amendments together, I may point out
that one reason which is given for the proposed change is that the rural surveyor is
very probably quite unacquainted with
what exists under the surface of the
county roads, that is to say, he has no
knowledge of the pipes for purposes other
than drains which are not within the
control of his own council-such things
as water pipes, gas pipes, electricity
works, and so on. I think the Committee
ought to be acquainted with the fact that
there is a real difficulty in regard to some
of the county roads; more so, I believe,
in the county roads near Belfast.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I am very glad
that steps are being taken to deal with.
this matter. In my experience a great
deal of friction has arisen in the past on
this question, particularly between the
officials of the local authorities concerned.
The Armagh Council have for long regarded this as a very serious grievance.
The rural council, which is the sanitary
authority, have opened roads without
notice, and great trouble has been experienced in getting them to repair the
roads. This has always been a source of
great trouble to the county council.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move in Clause 4,
to add at the end the following new subsections-
"(2) Before a sanitary authority
acting as aforesaid proceed to break up
any such road, the authority shall, except in a case of emergency, give to
the county surveyor seven days' notice
in writing of their intention so to do,
together with a plan showing particulars of the proposed work so far as
it affects such road, and the sanitary
authority shall, before proceeding with
the work, consider any representations
which may be made to them by the
county surveyor.
(3) In any case where any difference
arises between a sanitary authority and
a county council as to what is a reasonable time for the purposes of sub-section
(1) of this section, the matter shall be
referred to the Ministry, whose determination thereon shall be final and
conclusive."
Amendment agreed to.

(1) Where any damage is done in

section one hundred and sixty-two of

(Adaptation of Irish Enactments)

Mr. LONG: I beg to move, in Clause
5, sub-clause 1, to leave out the words
"twenty-one days" ("within twenty-one
davs') and to insert instead thereof
"three months."
The object of this Clause is to provide
that where compensation is claimed in
respect of any damage done to land by a
road authority this damage shall, in
future, be assessed by a Court of Summary Jurisdiction instead of by three
substantial householders as was the law
hitherto. As originally drafted, the
Clause allowed a period of 21 days within.
which an appeal for the assessment of
compensation might be made. Since the
introduction of the Bill it has been represented to the Ministry by the Law
Society that this period is too short, on
the grounds that it might well be that
the owner of the property would happen
to be absent from it over a period of
more than 21 days since the damage was
done, and the Law Society have suggested that a period of three months
should be inserted instead of 21 days.
This recommendation
appears to the
Ministry to be open to no objection at
all and to offer certain possible advantages, and it therefore is in favour of
the acceptance of an Amendment to such
effect.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
5, to leave out the words, "The decision
of the said court shall be final and conclusive."
This Amendment raises a very serious
question. A dispute between a contractor
or the county council on the one hand
and the man whose quarry or field has
been damaged on the other can only
arise in default of agreement. If they
cannot agree the matter will, under this
Clause, be decided by a resident magistrate sitting alone, instead of by three
householders as provided under the Grand
Jury Act of 1836, that is to say, one
appointed by the man himself, one by
the county surveyor or contractor, and
the third a justice of the peace.
third householder-the magistrate-has
disappeared. The man had some sort of
jury, and he is losing that. The resident
magistrate has to do the thing instead.
I think it is extremely probable, in view
of the trivial nature of the question, that
nothing more than the decision of the
resident magistrate is likely to be required; but the fact remains that a
dispute which arises is a dispute
The
an
of a civil character. In that sort
of case it has always been the practice
that there should be the right of appeal.
The Summary Jurisdiction Act of 1851
-Chapter 92-provided for such
appeal, and when the Summary Jurisdiction Act was passed by this Parliament within recent years the right of
appeal was retained. You have then
here a case of a civil nature to be settled
by a resident magistrate. An appeal
ought to lie if either party really wants
it. Whether a party wants it or not is
not a question for Parliament. I have
always objected to Parliament trying a
case it has not heard. Therefore, I see
no reason for separating such a dispute
as this, paltry though it may be, from
the ordinary law which would provide the
man with the right of appeal. I think
we can get that right of appeal by
leaving out the words on the Paper.
Mr. LONG: The object of this Amendment appears to be to provide an appeal
from the finding of the court of summary
jurisdiction. While this is no doubt sound
as a general principle it is entirely un-

courts of summary jurisdiction in Ireland.
