That is ample justification for giving the
right of appeal in these cases. The county
court would be the most satisfactory
tribunal for dealing with these matters.
The county court is not congested and the
judge has time to hear each case properly.
I suggest strongly that the right of appeal

2 JULY 1937
THE SENATE.

Friday, 2nd July, 1937.
The Senate met at Quarter after Three
of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the
Chair.
Business of the House.
Resolved, "That Standing Order No.
XXV be suspended for this day's sitting
for the purpose of taking the remaining
stages of the Roads Bill."-(Mr. Long.)
Roads Bill.
Order for receiving Report of Amendments read.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That
this report be now received."
Hon. Senators will remember that
when this Bill was in Committee yesterday I promised that a certain matter in
connection with Clause 2 would be reconsidered by the Government before the
Bill came before them again to-day.
There was considerable discussion
on this Clause, but the various
points were, I think, satisfactorily explained to the House with the exception
of just this one, which the Government
has in the interval most carefully considered. This was the question of
whether the 220 yards which the Ministry
of Home Affairs is authorised, subject to
the approval of the Ministry of Finance,
to take on either side of the road is no
more than might reasonably be required.
I would, in the first instance, repeat
what I said yesterday, namely, that it is
definitely not the intention to acquire
wide strips of this character on either
side of any of these roads, and, while the
Bill provides that strips may be taken on
either side of the proposed road, in all
probability, even in an extreme case
where it was necessary to acquire a wide
strip beside a road, it would only be
taken on one side of it. It is by no
means necessary, nor is it expected, that
it will always be taken on both sides of
the road. The distance of 220 yards is
that provided for in the 1909 Act, and
it does admittedly seem to afford a
generous margin to meet all possible requirements, but I think I may say that
the House was prepared to admit that
had advantage been taken of this provision in certain instances in connection
with our Coast Road very great benefit
would have accrued. It was suggested,
I think, however, that the Coast Road
might be regarded as altogether an
exceptional case.
After most careful consideration the
Government has reached the conclusion
that to adopt a smaller margin than 220
yards would risk inhibiting the whole usefulness of the provision. It is not
difficult to envisage all manner of cases
where a small strip running back from
one side of the road as far as 220 yards
might be most necessary in order that the
public might gain the fullest possible
benefit from the road. For example, it
is of the utmost importance that in connection with a road of the kind a proper
line of vision should be protected at
curves, and, while it is hardly likely that
the whole 220 yards would be required
for this purpose, it might well be that
such a large part of it would be required
that it would be more economical and
equitable to all parties concerned to
acquire an entire interest rather than
part of an interest. Moreover, as honourable Senators suggested in connection
with the previous Clause, the question
of the right of entry on to by-pass roads.
is not one that can be entirely swept
be most
aside, and this clearly can
suitably dealt with by means of service
roads, so that an unnecessary number of
entries on to the road can be avoided
without hardship. Where such service
roads have to be made it is plainly desirable that there should be a reasonable
separation of them from the by-pass
road. Generally speaking, this separation would not necessitate the acquisition
of any very material width, but it is
easy to imagine circumstances as, for
example, again, at curves, where
quite a considerable separation would be.
necessary and desirable.
In all the circumstances, therefore, the
Government cannot but feel that to depart from the provision that has been
law in connection with this matter since
1909 would be a very great mistake.
Roads Bill-
Third Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]

inquiries regarding a person in Cushendall who was reported to have received

inform me that they can trace no communication addressed to this gentleman
whatever.
Mr. MAGUIRE: With regard to the
hauliers in the Cushendall area-

there not some mistake? The question
before the House, as I understand it, is
that the Report of the Amendments to
the Road Bill be received. I cannot see
that the hauliers in Cushendall district
have anything to do with that. I may
be wrong.
Is that not right?
Mr. SPEAKER: I will put the

On Question, Motion agreed to.
Mr. MAGUIRE: In regard to the
hauliers in Cushendall area, I am very
glad-
Mr. SPEAKER: I think any remarks
to be made on the Bill had better be
made on the Third Reading.
Mr. LESLIE: If the question of the
hauliers in the Cushendall district has
anything whatever to do with the Roads
Bill it would seem to be a matter to come
up on the Third Reading; if not, it would
seem to relate to the Adjournment.
Mr. SPEAKER: I did not quite catch
what the hon. Member was speaking on.
If it has any relation to the case of the
Cushendall haulier it has nothing to do
with the Roads Bill. It is a matter that
can be brought up on the Adjournment.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read the Third time."
Mr. LESLIE: I have very little to
say as regards the Third Reading of the
Roads Bill which is now before us. Most
of what I have to say, I think, is to
thank the Parliamentary Secretary for
the observations he delivered to us when
he moved that the report of the Committee be now received. The Parliamentary Secretary dealt briefly with the
matters which engaged our attention
yesterday afternoon, and which gave rise
to some discussion. Partly he spoke of
the difficulties attending the question of
access to and across this new by-pass
road. I think that really we are all in
agreement with what he said this afternoon upon the subject. I should myself
have preferred, as the House knows, that
something more direct had been inserted
in the Bill dealing with that question,
rather, perhaps, because one cannot but
have the question of railway crossings in
one's mind.
After all, railways were constructed
to facilitate the movement of swift
traffic, and it is possible to provide
crossings there, but I do not believe
either the Parliamentary Secretary
or the Ministry of Home Affairs
has any real objection to crossings being
made at strictly suitable intervals and
under strictly controlled conditions over
these by-pass roads. The question of the
number of crossings which may be
admitted depends on the unknown factor,
that is the length of the particular road.
If your by-pass road is 100 yards long
you could probably do without any
crossing whatever. If, on the contrary,
it was ten miles long you would obviously
put in a great many. You are not likely
to have a by-pass road in Northern Ireland
ten miles in length. The question of
expense would be always before you, and
that would be a governing consideration.
I should, as I said, have preferred something a little more direct in the Bill,
because it would have been of assistance
to the wayfaring man outside, and the
man outside would have been left without
the necessity of reflecting that his rights
were protected not directly in the Act
but indirectly by the compensation he
would receive for any damage, and, still
further, in an even more roundabout
direction by the possibility of his having
to have recourse to the Court for a
decision that the proposals made to him
were not in fact reasonable. There exists
in the Bill, as I read it, ultimate protection for everybody against injustice.
and, therefore, I do not think there is any
real necessity for any alteration in the
terms of the Bill.
We must remember, as I tried to indicate yesterday and earlier on the Second
Reading, that it is imperative that the
Roads Bill-
Cushendall Milk Haulier
2 JULY 1937
by-pass road, if made, should serve the
purpose for which it was constructed.
Passing to the question of the 220 yards,
I may remind the House that I did not
myself attack the extent of that provision.
I did say, I think, that I had seen roads
in England where I believe the full 220
yards had in fact been taken, and so
far as I can form an opinion they were
in fact required. I do not believe for a
moment that anything approaching 220
yards on even one side of the road would
be required in Northern Ireland, but in
view of the fact, which the Parliamentary
Secretary drew our attention to this
afternoon, that the provision for 220
yards is in an Act passed by the Parliament of the United Kingdom as long ago
as 1909, I should hesitate a very long
time before I suggested we should alter it.
Again, the protection against any unreasonable action rests in the right to
compensation and the right to appeal in
the last resort to the courts on the
ground that the terms of compensation
offered are not reasonable, that is, in
effect, that the Ministry could have
obtained the land on reasonable terms if
they had offered reasonable terms. As
they have not done so they cannot resort
to compulsion. I do not think there is
any reason why we should further delay
the passage of the Bill. We know, of
course, that we have had to take the
Bill with undue rapidity, but that is a
matter about which I cannot make any
complaint, as we have always agreed that
the circumstances were wholly exceptional.
As a matter of fact, I drew attention to
the celerity with which we have had to
deal with this Bill. I mentioned inconvenience of this kind about two or three
years ago.
We had to approach the Committee
stage with the Amendments put down
on the Paper, which was only in the
hands of Members on the morning of the
day on which we were to consider them,
and without the printed report of the
Second Reading before us. One ventures
to hope that that will not happen again,
and we know that if the Leader of the
House and the Parliamentary Secretary
have anything to do with it it will not
happen except under the exceptional
circumstances which occurred this year.
I do not for a moment oppose the Third
Reading of the Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That this Bill do now pass."
-(Mr. Long.)

haulier and informed him that in the
meantime he could carry on the collection
of liquid milk, and also any merchandise,
in the ordinary way of business. I am
very glad that at least one private motor
haulier in the Province is satisfied.

the Transport Board having looked at the
files of correspondence and the name of
the owner of the vehicle instead of the
file addressed to the solicitor.

Motion before the House, I should
like it to be clearly understood that it
does not follow from my silence on this
occasion that the Government will offer
facilities for the passage of this Bill
through another place.

CLAUSE 5 (Candidates not to present
themselves for final examination until
expiration of apprenticeship).
(1) A person bound by indentures
of apprenticeship to serve as an
apprentice to a solicitor shall not,
without the consent of the Society,
present himself as a candidate at the
final examination until after the expiration of the period for which, under
the provisions of this Act or regulations made thereunder, he is required.
to serve as such apprentice before being
admitted a solicitor.
(2) Section twenty-eight of the
principal Act shall cease to have effect.
The following Amendment stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Leslie:-
:-
Clause 5, page 5, after line 7, insert:
"Provided that if a final examination is held at any time less than six
months before the expiration of the
said period it shall be lawful, subject
to the provisions of this Act, and notwithstanding anything contained in
this sub-section, for such person
as
aforesaid to present himself as a
candidate at such examination."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not propose to
move the Amendment on the Paper. If
you will be so kind as to put the Clause
separately I will endeavour to explain.
the matter to the Committee.
Amendment not moved.
Question put, "That the Clause stand
part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: This proved to be a
rather difficult problem. It will be
observed that in the Clause before us it
is proposed amongst other things to
repeal Section 28 of the Act of 1898; that
is the Solicitors' (Ireland) Act, which
has been so much in our minds during
the preparation and discussion of the
Bill. I had better tell the Committee
what Clause 28 provided. It provided
that a person bound by indentures should
not present himself as a candidate for
the final examination until he had completed his term, provided that whenever
any of the periods of five, four, and three
years' service expire in any vacation, any
person whose period of apprenticeship so
expires is at liberty to present himself at
the final examination, which shall be
held immediately preceding such vacation. That was a definite saving for a
particular occurrence. In those days
there were three or two final examinations held in the year. Now there will
probably be only two, and possibly only
one. The position is very much altered.
But after the Second Reading representations were made to me that an
apprentice might be seriously damaged in
the course of his career if he had to wait
until the next examination came along.
Accordingly I undertook to draft something in the way of a proviso which would.
perhaps prevent any grievance arising,
and the result was the Amendment on
the Paper. I did not myself like the
Amendment, and I do not think the
Committee would have liked it either,
because although we aimed to provide, as
was done in the Amendment not moved,
for the six months man, what is to
happen to the seven months man? You
cannot provide for every conceivable case
of hardship.
The only provision we can make has, I
think, been made in the text of the Bill.
If the Committee will look at the first
Solicitors Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
sub-clause they will see that a person
bound by indentures can, with the consent of the Society, present himself before
the proper time. The prohibition is,
"shall not without the consent of the
Society," and it seems to me that on the
whole we should leave the matter in that
way, as in the Bill, rather than interfere
to some extent with that wide discretion
to the Society for which I asked when
moving the Second Reading. Accordingly, although I put the Amendment on
the Paper, I have not moved it, and I
suggest it should stand over for Report
in order that if further representations
are made something better may be devised and presented to the Committee on
the Report stage. I support the Motion
that the Clause as it is stand part of the
Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Clause accordingly ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
14, lines 27 and 28, to leave out the words
in paragraph (c) "the Attorney-General
for," and insert, "two of the Benchers
of the Inn of Court of." It may be
recollected that I spoke of this small
matter on the Second Reading.
point is that under the original Act of
1898 the power of giving the certificate
required by a man who wished to cease to
be a barrister and become a solicitor was
vested in two Benchers of the King's Inns.
When in 1922 we established our own
Incorporated Law Society in Northern
Ireland we had not got an Inn
of Court, and consequently had not
got any Benchers. Now we have
both an Inn of Court and Benchers
for that Inn of Court, and I may
with
say it is
the consent of the
Attorney-General that I move we restore
the prerogative to the Inn's Benchers,
to whom it properly belongs. Hence the
Motion on the Paper.
Amendment agreed to.

part of the Bill.

Solicitors Bill-

THE SENATE
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (8) to leave out the words
"resident magistrate may report to the
Society any solicitor in a case where it
appears to such judge," and to insert,
"justice of the peace may report to the
Society any case where it appears to such
judge or justice." The point of this is
that the occurrence we desire to prevent,
that is, the misconduct of a solicitor
before whatever court he may be appearing, is just as likely to happen before an
ordinary justice, hearing such things as
he has to hear, such as an application to
commit for trial, as before any other
court. Therefore, it is desirable that
power should be vested in a magistrate
just as much as in anybody else.
hope it will not happen.
Amendment agreed to.

Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

CLAUSE 25 (Procedure before Committee).
(1) The Committee may administer
and take oaths and affirmations for the
purpose of any application made to
them under this Act, and the applicant
and the solicitor to whom the application relates shall respectively be entitled to require the evidence of
parties, witnesses and others interested,
and to call for such evidence and
documents and examine such witnesses
as to them may seem proper; but a
person shall not be compelled to produce any document which he could not
be compelled to produce on the trial of
an action, unless the Committee order
the production of any such document.
The applicant and the solicitor to
whom the application relates may sue
out writs of subpoena ad testificandum
and duces tecum to compel the attendance of witnesses before the Committee.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (1) to leave out "unless the Committee order the production of any such
document." This is, perhaps, a more
serious matter. On reconsideration the
conclusion was arrived at that it would
Solicitors Bill-
Committee
5 OCTOBER 1937
not be right to ask Parliament to give
the power contained in the words I am
suggesting should be left out. It would,
perhaps, be suggested that you were
giving the Committee, so to speak, power
greater than that possessed by the High
Court. I could not defend that before
this Committee. For the moment, therefore, I am suggesting that these words
should be left out.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

CLAUSE 28 (Discretion of registrar
to refuse certificate in special cases).
(1) In any case where a solicitor-
(a) applies for a registrar's certificate,
having neglected so to do for
twelve months after the expiration
of the last registrar's certificate
issued to him; or
(b) applies for a registrar's certificate
while he is an undischarged bankrupt; or
(c) having been suspended from practice or whose name has been struck
off the roll, first applies for a
registrar's certificate after the expiration of his suspension or his
re-admission to the roll, as the case
may be; or
(d) applies for a registrar's certificate
while any fine, penalty, costs or
expenses imposed upon or ordered
to be paid by him under this Act
or the principal Act remain unpaid;
such solicitor shall, unless the registrar
or the Lord Chief Justice otherwise
orders, give to the registrar, at least
three weeks before the application is
made, notice of his intention to make
the application, and the registrar may
in his discretion refuse the application,
subject, however, to an appeal to the
Lord Chief Justice, who may affirm the
decision of the registrar or may direct
him to issue a certificate to the applicant on such terms and conditions, if
any, as the Lord Chief Justice may
think fit.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to insert
after the first line: "(a) applies for a
first registrar's certificate after the expiration of five years from the passing of
his final examination."
This requirement appears in the corresponding Acts
in England, and it is thought it should
go in here. The effect is, as I am sure
the Committee will see, that in any case
where a solicitor applies for a first instance certificate after five years from the
passing of the examination, that is, if
he has waited five years after passing his
examination before he applies for a certificate then he should be treated in the
same way as other people referred to in
the Clause, that is, he must give three
weeks' notice and so on.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to leave
out the words "if any" in the last line
of sub-clause (1). It is not customary to
circumscribe the Lord Chief Justice in
any way, and these words are unnecessary.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 29 (Power of the Society to
publish orders and reports)--
(1) A copy of every order made by
the Lord Chief Justice under this Part
of this Act and a copy of every report
made by the Committee to the Lord
Chief Justice under this Part of this
Act shall be sent to and be filed by
the secretary of the Society, and the
Society shall be entitled to publish any
such order or report, or any part or
parts thereof, or a synopsis thereof,
in the annual report of the Society, or
by sending a copy thereof to every
solicitor in Northern Ireland.
(2) The making of the report to the
Lord Chief Justice or the setting down
of any report for hearing before the
Lord Chief Justice by the Committee,
or the publication of any order or
report of any part or parts thereof
or a synopsis thereof under the provisions of the last preceding sub-section
shall be deemed to be absolutely privileged.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (1) to leave out the words "under
this part of this Act," where they first
Solicitors Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
occur, and to insert "or by the Committee." We do not want to repeat the
expression "under this part of this Act,
and we do want to bring out the fact
that an order can be made by the Committee. Hence the suggested alteration.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (1) to leave out "or by sending a
copy thereof to every solicitor in Northern
Ireland," and to insert "and by sending
a copy thereof to every solicitor in Northern Ireland and in any other manner
approved by the Lord Chief Justice."
The whole question of publishing reports
and so on is rather difficult, and it is
thought better that we should make here
the requirement that if any other manner
than that mentioned in the Clause is resorted to it should be done with the
approval of the Lord Chief Justice. The
effect is things will be sent to every solicitor in Northern Ireland and may be
published. They cannot, of course, be
disseminated or broadcast recklessly.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Sub-
Clause (2) to leave out "publication"
and insert "making or publishing." It
is desired to bring in making as well as
publication. It is very simple.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.
CLAUSES 30 (Issue of warrant as to
costs). 31 (Rules as to proceedings under

society to make regulations as to certain

CLAUSE 34 (Consequences of failure
to comply with regulations).
(1) If any solicitor fails or neglects
to observe or comply with any of the
regulations made under the last preceding section, the society or any person
may make a complaint in respect of
that failure or neglect to the Committee.
(2) The provisions of this Part of
this Act shall apply in relation to complaints under this section as they apply
in relation to applications to the Committee under Part II of this Act.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Sub-
Clause (2) to leave out "this Part" and
insert "Part II." It will be seen that
there are two small Amendments to this
Clause, and they both arise from the same
cause, that is, that during the drafting
of the Bill Part II and Part III got temporarily mixed, or were not properly subdivided. Accordingly, we have to make
the expression "this Part" read "Part
II."

later stage. Therefore, it was thought
not desirable to bring it in in Clause 38.
At the same time the question arose

anything but anxious to press the Clause
on the Committee. Accordingly, I ask
to withdraw the Clause. If a better case
can be made at a later stage the matter
can be reconsidered.
Amendment agreed to.

(2) It is hereby declared that in

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to leave
out sub-clause (2) and to insert "(2)
For the removal of doubt it is hereby declared that in the principal Act and in
this Act references to persons shall,
where the context does not otherwise require, be so construed as to include
references to bodies corporate”.
This is really a very technical matter
dealing with the question whether references to persons include bodies
corporate. After very careful consideration we came to the conclusion that the
best way was to make it general rather
than particular as was done in drafting
the Bill, that is to say, in the Bill as it
received the Second Reading. It will be
observed that in the new sub-clause I
put in the words "for the removal of
doubt," a practice sometimes adopted to
make a thing quite clear when you do
not want to admit there is any doubt
about it.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

CLAUSE 51 (Action to recover solicitor's costs).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Samuel
Cunningham:
Clause 51, page 28, line 29, after Subsection (1) insert the following new Subsection:(2) A solicitor shall not have
a lien on any document belonging to a
client for costs unless within six months
from the completion of the transaction
to which the costs relate he shall have
furnished an account of such costs to the
client."
I
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM:
move the insertion of this new Sub-clause
Solicitors Bill-
Committee
5 OCTOBER 1937
in the interests of solicitors' clients, and
because in the first Sub-section of this
Clause solicitors are taking power to be
at liberty under certain circumstances to
commence proceedings to recover their
costs within a limited and shorter period.
Therefore, I think it is only reasonable to
provide that if a solicitor does not furnish
to a client an account of his costs within
six months he should forfeit the right to
any further lien he may have on documents belonging to that client. I need
not recite cases I know of, and others I
have heard of, where the action of a
solicitor in retaining his lien on documents has proved a hardship to the client.
I would point out to the Committee that
a solicitor stands in a very different position to his client from that of any other
professional or business man. He is his
client's confidential adviser, and the client
is expected to and does take his advice.
Therefore, a solicitor who fails to advise
his client within a reasonable time of the
amount of his debt should no longer be
entitled to a lien on his client's securities.
I do not think the insertion of this
Sub-section would cause any hardship to a
solicitor, because all he has got to do is to
furnish his account, and if he chooses not
to do so as a good many solicitors do and
to allow payment of his costs to remain
over for an indefinite time he will not
forfeit his right to recover those costs, but
he will not have a lien on his client's
securities. One of the objects of this
Measure is to strengthen the position as
between solicitor and client, and in my
opinion the insertion of this new Subsection would have that effect. Accordingly, I beg to move.
Mr. LESLIE: I am very sorry, but I
am quite unable to recommend the Committee to accept this Amendment. I do
not think the insertion of the words suggested would really facilitate anybody.
We must remember that at present a
client who feels himself defrauded or mishandled in any way by his solicitor has
recourse to the courts. He can, in the
first instance, obtain the services and
advice of some other solicitor who will
very promptly advise him of his rights and
remedies. He can also bring any possible
default before the notice of the Law
Society. Again, this lien of the solicitor
on the deeds of his client is based, and
rather deeply rooted, in the common law.
It has received statutory recognition from
time to time, but its basis is a common
law basis, and the common law in
Northern Ireland is the same as that in
England.
In England, although they have
revised the legal position of solicitors, in
so far as they can do it by Act of Parliament, several times since 1932, they have
not seen fit to introduce a change of this
description, and if we were to introduce
it here we should be left in a different
position from England, and I may add,
so far as I know, from Scotland and the
Irish Free State. I need scarcely point
out to the Committee that the possibility
of a solicitor or his client being engaged
in an operation covering the whole three
countries is anything but fantastic. It
may very easily happen. Indeed we have
in Northern Ireland solicitors who also
practise in the Free State, and it is
scarcely conceivable that we should introduce here a change which is not
In any case,
accepted elsewhere.
ought to wait for a lead from England.
we
Again, my right hon. Friend proposes
that costs should be rendered within six
months from the completion of the transaction. It will be rather difficult to find
out when the transaction is completed.
The solicitor would tell you that the transaction was not completed until he had
actually got his costs. But if there are
any grievances the courts are open, and
let us not introduce here a change which
has not been introduced across the water.
I cannot recommend the Committee to
accept the Amendment.

certainly hesitate to agree to any Amendment without investigating the whole

CLAUSE 54 (Dissent from regulations).
Copies of all regulations made under
this Act or the principal Act shall as
soon as may be after they are made be
transmitted to all the judges of the
Supreme Court for the time being and
if within twenty-eight days after a copy
of any regulation has been so transmitted a majority of the said judges
(the Lord Chief Justice being one)
signify by writing under their hands
addressed to the president or secretary
of the society their dissent from such
regulation or any part thereof, the same
shall be of no force and effect; and if
after any such regulation or any part
thereof has come into force a majority
of the said judges (the Lord Chief
Justice being one) signify in the
manner aforesaid their dissent from
such regulation or any part thereof the
same shall on the expiration of two
months thereafter cease to be of any
force or effect.

Lavery, Mr. T. R.

Viscount
Charlemont's Resignation.
Mr. LONG: I should like to take this,
the first, opportunity of expressing the
deep regret with which I personally, and
I am sure every other Member, learnt
of the resignation, enforced by illness,
of Lord Charlemont from his position as
Leader of this House, of which he has
been so long so loyal a member.
I think it will be agreed that this
House Owes much to his unassuming
manner, unfailing courtesy and tact, and
to his sense of proportion, which, in the
guise of humour, has so often instructed
and enlivened our deliberations.
I know I voice the feelings of every
Member of this House when I wish him
speedy recovery. (Hon Members:
Hear, hear.)
a
Mr. McHUGH: I desire to associate
myself with everything that has just been
said by the Deputy-Leader of the House
with reference to Lord Charlemont. I
have been associated with him as a
Member of this House for upwards of
eight years, and I never found him anything but courteous and kind, especially
to those who differed from him politically.
I am very sorry, indeed, that ill-health
has compelled him to resign his position
in the Government. I believe it would
be impossible to find his equal, and in
endorsing everything that has been said
by the Parliamentary Secretary I hope
and trust that Lord Charlemont will
soon be restored to his usual health.
Mr. LESLIE: Like other speakers, I
deeply regret the event which has taken
place. I feel, as indeed we all feel, that
we have lost not only a leader but a
friend. Lord
Charlemont's personal
geniality endeared him to us all, and I
think I may add that the faculty of selfeffacement which he possessed did nothing
to detract from our estimation of his
character. But I do not think we ought
to let the personal quality blind us to the
other gifts which our former Leader possessed. He did show on more than one
occasion in this House evidence of a
distinct gift of statesmanship, and he
evinced that gift partly as a stalwart
guardian and protector of the privileges
and status of this House, and still more,
perhaps, in the way he held, and showed
that he held, that constitutional sense of
loyalty to his Cabinet colleagues which is
so desirable an element in the conduct of
any civilised State. We are poorer for
the loss of the Leadership of Lord Charlemont, and we shall regret him for very
many days.
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY
(Sir James Wilton): The only apology I
have to offer-if any apology is necessary
--for attempting to add anything to what
the previous speakers have said is that I
have had the opportunity of being
associated with Lord Charlemont in his
capacity as Minister of Education for
quite a long time, and in a rather intimate way.
In the dual capacity of

without

fear of contradiction that during his term

Question to Minister.
HOUSEBREAKING IN CREGAGH
AREA.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Dr. Gibson-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state how many cases
of housebreaking have occurred in the
Cregagh area since the removal of the
police barracks from that district; how
many cases have occurred within the
past month; and what steps the
Government propose to take to protect
the homes and property of the inhabitants.
Dr. GIBSON: I desire to bring before
the House the question of which I have
given notice. During the time the
Cregagh area enjoyed a police barracks
it was practically immune from the crime
of housebreaking, but since the removal
of the police barracks a great many cases
have occurred in that district. Recently
the house of one of our resident magistrates was broken into, and I regret to
say there were some uncomplimentary
comments made with regard to him and
some of the pictures in his room.
A permanent official of the High Court also
had his house broken into. My professional work takes me into a large number
of houses in this district, and I find that
the utmost apprehension, almost dread,
exists in regard to houses being broken
into. There is a widespread feeling that
the police arrangements at the present
time are not adequate to protect the
homes and property of the people in that
area.
Mr. LONG: The police barracks at
Cregagh were closed on the 8th September 1930, and the townland comprising
Contributory Pensions Bill 308
Oral Answers
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
the old Cregagh police district formed
part of Carryduff district, where a new
barrack had been built. Subsequently,
in November, 1935, that portion of the
old Cregagh district nearest to the city
of Belfast was transferred for policing
to the Willowfield district, and another
small portion was attached to Ballynafeigh district.
From the 8th September, 1930, to the
15th October, 1937, that is, in a period
of just over seven years, 57 'cases of
housebreaking were reported to the police.
During the month ended the 15th
October, 1937, four cases of housebreaking were reported to the police, but these
four all occurred on the same night, and
were, presumably, carried out by the
same person.
From an examination which has been
made of the figures of housebreaking in
the Cregagh area since 1923, it is clear
that there has been a considerable increase
in this type of case in recent years, but
I am glad to be able to inform the hon.
Senator that the number of detections
made by the Royal Ulster Constabulary
has increased in almost the identical ratio
to the number of cases of housebreaking.
The records show also that the increase
in this class of crime-which has on many
occasions been referred to by the presiding Judges at the City Commissions in
Belfast is not peculiar to the Cregagh
area, but extends generally throughout
the city.
I can assure the hon. Senator that all
necessary steps are being taken to protect
the homes and property of the inhabitants, and, in fact the percentage of
detections made by the police in recent
years has been extremely satisfactory. I
must remind the hon. Senator of a criticism which has been made very often
by His Majesty's Judges, and which is
only too true, that the owners and
occupiers of houses are themselves very
often to blame by reason of the fact that
they do not properly secure their houses
before going on holidays or on temporary
absences from home.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Widows',
Orphans', and Old Age Contributory
Pensions (Voluntary Contributors) Bill,
the County Court Judges (Salaries) Bill,
and the Finance Bill, to which they
desire the concurrence of this House.
Widows', Orphans', and Old Age
Contributory Pensions (Voluntary
Contributors) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
County Court Judges (Salaries) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Finance Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Widows', Orphans', and Old Age
Contributory Pensions (Voluntary
Contributors) Bill.
Mr. LONG: It is always a pleasant
task to recommend to this House a Bill
which provides for some extension of our
social services, and by this Measure it is
proposed to extend one of the most
essential of our schemes of social
insurance.
I do not wish to trouble hon. Members
with statistics, but I would like to remind
them that well over 400,000 persons are
at present insured under the existing
contributory pensions scheme, and if the
dependants of these persons are taken
into account, the greater part of the
population of the Province is covered by
it. The existing scheme is, however, restricted to persons employed under a
contract of service in what are called
"insurable employments," and to a small
group of "voluntary contributors," whose
title to continue insurance voluntarily
depends upon their first having had at
least two years' compulsory insurance.
There remain outside the scope of the
scheme a large number of people whose
incomes are comparable to the incomes of
those who are insured, and who are in
equal need of old-age pensions and provision for their widows and children.
This Bill is designed to enable them to
procure on a voluntary basis the same
pensions benefits as those who are already
309 Contributory Pensions Bill 19 OCTOBER 1937

First Reading
insured. It is estimated that there are
some thirty thousand married men, and
a large number of single persons, who
would be able to take advantage of the
proposed scheme.
The proposed scheme is, briefly, this:
Any man between the ages of twenty-one
and forty, who is resident in Northern
Ireland at the date of his application
and who has been resident in the
United Kingdom during the ten years
preceding that date can apply to join,
provided his total income does not exceed
£400 a year, and can, by paying contributions graduated according to the age
at which he enters, obtain the advantages
enjoyed by those who are at present insured, that is to say, a widow's pension
of 10s a week with allowance of 5s a week
for the eldest or only child and 3s a week
for each other child, an orphan's pension
of 7s 6d a week for each orphan or an
old-age pension of 10s a week at 65 for
himself, with a similar pension for his
wife when she reaches that age. He may,
if he prefers, insure for the widows' and
orphans' pensions only at a lower premium. A woman between the same ages
whose total income does not exceed £250,
can insure for a similar pension at 65,
and will have cover for orphans' pensions
as well. All pensions at 65 and widows'
pensions are continued after 70 under the
non-contributory pensions scheme without
any test of means. Orphans' pensions
and children's allowances are payable up
to the age of 14 or, if a child remains at
school, until the 31st July next following
his sixteenth birthday.

By a special concession, applications
for entry into insurance will be accepted
during the first year of the scheme from
anyone who satisfies the other conditions,
whose age does not exceed 55 on the 3rd
January, 1938. These "initial entrants,'
as they are to be known, will also as a
further concession be entitled to contribute, not on a graduated scale, but at the
rate appropriate to a person under the
age of 21, namely, 1s 3d a week for a
man insured for all the benefits, and 6d a
week for a woman.
I have stated the limits which the total
incomes of entrants must not exceed, and
I must add that not more than £200 of
a man's and £125 of a woman's income
may be unearned. These limits apply
only at the time of entry into insurance.
It is considered that persons having
greater means than these at the time when
they would enter insurance should be able
to provide for themselves without State
assistance. There are certain persons
either already insured or already provided
by Statute or other recognised means,
with security for their old-age and for
their dependants equal to that to be given
under the proposed measure. It would
be unreasonable that such people should
be entitled to a second pension under a
State-aided scheme and these classes are,
therefore, excluded from the scheme by
the First Schedule to the Bill. Male
teachers and other classes who have
superannuation rights may insure under
the scheme for widows' and orphans'
pensions, but not for old-age pensions.
An important aspect of the scheme is
that no statement of health and no
medical examination are to be required.
It is necessary, therefore, in order to
keep within reasonable bounds the
liability which the State may have to
assume, to lay down conditions to ensure
the proper working of the scheme as a
scheme of insurance. Accordingly, for
a widow's pension the scheme will require
a period of 104 weeks' insurance and the
payment of 104 contributions, and for an
old-age pension 10 years' continuous insurance and the payment of 260 contributions. Moreover, as the scheme is
entirely voluntary and there is no compulsion whatever to pay contributions, it
has been necessary to provide that in
order to qualify for a full pension a
contributor must maintain an average of
50 contributions a year throughout insurance. If the average falls below 50,
pensions will be payable at reduced rates.
which are stated in the Third Schedule
to the Bill. If the number falls below
26 in any year, the contributor will have
an opportunity to make up the 26 in the
following year, paying in addition, of
course, at least 26 contributions for that
year. If this opportunity is not taken,
insurance ceases at the end of that second
year.
A contributor who ceases to be insured
will not necessarily lose the whole of the
premiums he has paid; if he has paid a
sufficient number he will be granted a
"paid-up" policy, the terms of which are
set out in the Fourth Schedule of the
Bill. They provide that men other than
those initial entrants who are insured
for widows' and orphans' pensions only,
311 Contributory Pensions Bill--

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Long]
shall have five years' full cover for
widows' and orphans' pensions from the
last year in which not less than 26 contributions were paid, and also, that men
insured for all purposes and entering insurance after the first year of the scheme
will be entitled, if their contribution
average is sufficient to warrant it, to a
deferred annuity payable to them when
they reach the age of 65, and to their
wives (or their widows, if they themselves
die) when they have attained that age.
Women will receive a similar deferred
annuity at the age of 65.
Existing
voluntary contributors
are at present insured both for health
and for pensions, and have all the benefits
of both schemes (except medical benefit
if their income exceeds £250 a year).
They will now be able to select whatever
they consider the most suitable insurance
for their needs. They may, if they wish,
remain as they are or they may come
under the new pension scheme. If they
choose the latter course, they may still,
if they wish, continue their health insurance, and in that case the contributions of ls 5d or 1s 8d, as the case may
be, will be split into two contributions,
11d for pensions, and 6d or 9d for health
insurance. Instead of having to pay at
least 45 contributions a year in order to
continue their insurance, they will only
have to pay 26, but if their average
number of contributions a year drops
below 50 they will be subject to the
averaging which I have already described.
Another change in the existing position
is that married women will now be
entitled to be insured for pensions
purposes.
The advantages of this Measure, of
which I have tried to outline the main
structure, will, I think, commend themselves to hon. Members. Persons with
small incomes who are outside the present
compulsory scheme, and who in many
cases are facing the prospect of old age
with anxiety because of their limited
means will, I am sure, welcome the
scheme. I beg to move, "That this Bill
be now read a Second time."
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time.”
Mr. LESLIE: The Bill before us is
certainly a Bill which will be of great
interest to a very deserving class of
people. How large the number of those
people may be I am afraid I have no
definite information. I do not observe
from the White Paper circulated with the
Bill that the Government is in possession
of any very definite or accurate information to place before us. However, I do
not think that matters, because after all
if you cannot do good to everybody that
is no reason why you should not do good
to as many as you can. The Bill itself,
although I do not think the Parliamentary Secretary said so in so many
words, follows practically word for word
the Measure which has recently become
law in England. The English Measure,
I think, became law on the 1st of July
last, but it will not, of course, come into
operation until, I think, the 3rd of
January next year-at all events assuming that the 3rd of January is the first
Monday-for the first Monday is the date
named in the Bill. Therefore, we shall
not be in any way behind our friends
across the water in adopting the Measure
here.
The Bill itself is rather hard to follow
in places, and that, of course, is because
of the amount of fencing and qualification which is necessarily inserted in order
to prevent anything in the Bill running
counter to anything in any of the other
social schemes not expressly altered for
this purpose. I think as good an example
as I can find of the temporary difficulty
one feels is to be found in the fourth subclause of Clause 1. I read it three times
before I came to the conclusion it was
ment to catch the particular case of the
man who attained the age of 55 years
during the period of twelve months'
grace. I think the Bill is already
sufficiently plain, but it does not jump
to the eyes at once. And again, we are
not told specially in the Bill anything
about the method of administration, but
I presume I may take it that contributions will be payable by means of stamps
affixed to cards, and so on, because that
method is adopted in the other schemes.
I presume that is so, and no doubt we
shall be told at a later stage.
The Bill will be popular certainly with
those who become beneficiaries under it,
partly, perhaps, because it possesses a
certain elasticity which is absent from
some schemes. There will, at the same
time, be left some cases of hardship. I
may draw attention to one or two of these
313 Contributory Pensions Bill-- 19 OCTOBER 1937

Second Reading
here.
It is obvious we cannot endeavour
to cope with them, because we would be
adding an additional liability on the
public, and that is outside the powers of
this House. The orphan in this Bill will
be the same orphan as under the
principal Act. An orphan is a child who
has lost both his parents. There is, it
seems to me, a rather possible hardship
where the person insured under this Act
is a woman who leaves a husband and
child or children, and the husband is,
perhaps, bed-ridden and unable to support himself. If the expression "widow"
in this Act included "widower" it would
meet the case, but it does not. Although
we have an Act of Parliament which says
that words masculine include the
feminine, we have not got the corresponding provision on the other side.
I think that is rather a hardship, but
it may be that across the water, if not
here, some day that may be looked into.
That is the case of the wife who is the
wage-earner departing this life and leaving a helpless husband with helpless
children. As I make out, the Bill,
although it will come into force
January next, will not be immediately
applicable, because, if I understand the
Bill correctly, you have to wait, as a
in
minimum, ten years for an old age
pension. If you go in at the age of 55
you will not get the old age pension until
65, nor the widows' and orphans'
pension for apparently two years.
That brings me to a point on which
the Bill before us is not clear, and it is
a point I will take steps to clear up at
a later stage. The wording of the Bill
to which I propose to direct attention
follows exactly the wording of the English Measure, but the point I want to
make arises out of the word "insurance."
The White Paper uses the word, and the
Parliamentary Secretary used it again,
but I am going to suggest to the House
that we are not told in this Bill what is
meant, for this purpose, by the word
"insurance." The House will remember
that under the existing law in
Widows' and Orphans' Act of 1936, as it
stands to-day we have, although not a
definition of "insurance," an indication
of how we can get that definition.
It
may be that at a later stage the Deputy-
Leader of the House may be able to convince us that the point I am trying to
our
make is really of no importance and is
effectually covered, but at the moment I
cannot see it.
The point arises in that very important
matter of the number of contributions
that has to be paid before you can get
your pension of any sort. The passage
is Clause 3, paragraphs (a) and (b) on
page 5. We say that so many contributions have to be paid since his entry
into insurance. It seems to me that,
notwithstanding the fact that nothing
more occurs in the English Act, we do
want to add to that the words, "as a
special voluntary contributor." It seems
to me that without a further definition
of that sort we are left in some doubt
as to what is really required, and the
point is of very considerable importance.
I am quite sure the Ministry has no
doubt in its own mind as to the answer,
but we ought to express the Bill in such
terms that nobody can fail to understand
it, but as the text of the Measure stands
it does not seem to me to be beyond dispute that that is the intention.
There is nothing else worth detaining.
the House with this afternoon that I am
likely to call attention to, because I
think the Bill is really, if you take it
slowly, quite comprehensible, and it is
a Bill which will be of benefit to a great
many people. Happily the burden on
the Exchequer will not be very heavy.
I make out from the White Paper that
it is not likely to be an excessive sum,
although there is a heavy book debt as
regards the men who join early and are
of mature age, but I do not think the
public generally will object to that. I
think they will take the good with the
bad and welcome the Bill, because it is
intended to help a class which is singularly unable to help itself. I support the
Second Reading of the Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the

Measure is to fix the salaries of county

appointed in Northern

Finance Bill-

THE SENATE

will fix the salaries of other county court

increase the remuneration of any county

to be superseded have formed, since the
war, only part of their remuneration,
the remainder taking the form of a
cost-of-living bonus. The proposed rates
are slightly lower than the previous rates.
including cost-of-living bonus. I should

Finance Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The purpose of this Bill
is normally to give effect to changes in
taxation announced in Budget statements. This year, as hon. Members will
have noticed, it is proposed, in addition,
to make some small changes effecting a
decrease in rates of motor licence duty
and removing anomalies in motor taxation, which were not announced in the
Budget statement. Furthermore,
reference is made in the Bill to the reduction from 10s to 5s of the fee for a
driving licence which was announced in
that statement. The reason for the
no
omission is that as it is not an Excise
matter the Title of the Bill would not
Cover it. The reduction of motor
drivers' licence fees will, therefore, be
effected by a special one-clause Bill.
Briefly the effect of Clause 1 of the
Bill is to reduce, as from 1st January,
1938, the rates of taxation on private
motor-cars and motor-cycles, and on
motor vehicles used for the transport of
agricultural goods to the level obtaining
in Great Britain, and the duty on goods
vehicles and hackney carriages by onehalf of the difference between the existing rates in Great Britain and the existing rates in Northern Ireland.
These changes are set out in the First
Schedule to the Bill, which, for the sake
of clarity, and of administrative convenience, includes also a statement of
certain rates of duty which remain unaltered. Some minor concessions designed
to remove anomalies and to bring the
incidence of motor taxation more into
line with practice in Great Britain are
also made in the Schedule.
Clause 2 exempts invalid carriages
from tax. Clause 3 exempts from
taxation vehicles specially designed or
adapted for road construction purposes.
Clause 4 enables licensing authorities to
exempt from taxation farm vehicles
which use the public roads to a less extent than six miles per week. Under the
existing law a farmer whose land is
divided by a public road has to pay duty
on motor vehicles used by him for the
sole purpose of transporting goods from
one part of his farm to another.
Clause 5 enables licences to be taken
out for a minimum period of one week
for certain hackney carriages and omnibuses used only during holiday periods.
This is a concession which is not available
in Great Britain. Clause 6 enables the
police and licensing authorities to
demand information regarding
identity of persons driving unlicensed
vehicles. Similar powers are provided
under the law in Great Britain.
the
Clause 7 makes a concession in entertainments duty. Hon Members will remember that under the Finance Act of
1935 a lower rate became chargeable for
certain entertainments given by living
19 OCTOBER 1937
Finance Billis
performers. Due to the wording of the
Act, however, the higher rate of duty
was payable when such an entertainment
was combined with a performance of a
non-dutiable kind. The anomaly
removed by this Clause, which also makes
it clear that displays by any of His
Majesty's Forces or by the Royal Ulster
Constabulary come within the scope of
the concession. It is proposed that the
Clause shall have effect as from the date
of the Budget statement. I beg to move,
"That this Bill be now read a Second
time."
Mr. LESLIE: I should like to welcome the greater part of this Bill, and
particularly Clauses 2, 3, and 4, and,
I may add, Clause 5. Clause 5, I think
the Parliamentary Secretary told us, has
no counterpart in Great Britain, but the
other three Clauses, as the House will
recognise, appear in the Finance Act of
1936. Having said so much in favour
of those Clauses I come to Clause 6, and
at a later stage I shall atack that Clause
with all the vigour at my command. I
am sorry to say that that Clause is also
found in the Imperial Finance Act of
1936, where it appears as Section 13perhaps a somewhat unlucky number,
the more so as it amends Section 13 of
the Roads Act. Although the Clause
was pretty heavily attacked at Westminster it was not attacked altogether
on the grounds on which I propose to
attack it.
When speaking last year about the
Finance Bill I said that it was necessary,
perhaps, that a Bill imposing taxation
should have attached to its body something in the way of frills and flounces,
to which this House did not attach any
great importance, but still, in this case
it is not a case of frills and flounces, it
is a complete Court train. The Clause
really does have a serious effect on the
law of evidence and practice generally,
and I am afraid the fact that it slipped
through in England is not sufficient to
justify us in passing it here. I will not
deal further with it here because it is
a Committee point, but I hope later on
to get that particular Clause taken out
of this Finance Bill to which, in my
humble judgment, it does not properly
belong. It is not a finance question at
all. It belongs to the Road Act or the
motor law, but I do not think it should
Second Reading

properly appear on the Statute Book at
all. There is no object, of course, in
detaining the rest of the Bill on account
of one objectionable Clause that can be
removed without difficutly at
stage.

Question to Minister.
SCHOOL TESTS FOR DEFECTIVE
HEARING.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Samuel
Cunningham:---
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if school children
are being tested for any defects in hearing, and, if not, whether it would be
practicable to test children for their
hearing when they are being tested for
their sight.
In
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM:
asking this question I should like to say
that I know school children are very
carefully examined as regards their eyesight, but I do not know whether their
hearing is as carefully attended to. Some
of us who are getting older have trouble
with our hearing, and I think it is very
important that careful attention should
be paid to the hearing of school children.
There is another point I would like to
draw the attention of the House to. It
has struck me that some children do not
really catch sounds as other children do.
We know that as regards eyesight some
children see red, while a minority of
children see another colour. That is
commonly called colour blindness. Would
F
Message from the Commons
[Mr. Samuel Cunningham]
THE SENATE
it be possible that, similarly, some
children may never hear the right sound
as other children do. When we have
such a good staff of teachers, I think
they might be asked to consider this
question. I am sure the medical profession are looking into it, but it might
be useful to draw the attention of
teachers to the matter in case there is
such a fault in hearing as there is in
seeing.
Mr. LONG: I am glad to inform the
right hon. Senator that the testing of
school children for defects in hearing at
present comes under the Schemes for
Medical Inspection and Treatment of
School Children, and reports of school
medical officers show that they are paying
no little attention to this aspect of their
duties. I am sorry that at the moment
I am not able to give the right hon.
Gentleman a reply to the latter part of
his question.

contributions would be paid by stamps on

suggest the Government should consider

Mr. LESLIE: Perhaps I should explain that I deliberately did not ask the
Committee to expunge the words to which
my hon. Friend takes exception, and the
reason I did not suggest, and do not now
suggest, that the words "final and conclusive" should be taken out, is that

of this code and are necessary. We have
to remember that the case heard by the
umpire does not partake in any way of
the nature or character of a civil suit.
Its decision is how far the benevolence of
Parliament goes, and it is really limited
to questions of fact. There may be
some slight question of law involved.
am not suggesting that there should be
an appeal in the ordinary sense. All I
intended to do was to save the issue of a
prerogative writ. It is surely the right
of a man to come to court to see whether
the umpire has gone beyond his jurisdiction, in other words, whether his jurisdiction has been judicially exercised.
That is an entirely different proposition.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to support

think the House will agree that the public
have entire confidence in the administration of the law. The fact has been
suggested that these courts are held more
or less behind closed doors. The point
of view that a great many of the public
are bound to take is this, that in this
court the umpire who is in supreme control is the nominee of the Ministry of
Labour whose views the worker is fighting against. I think it is very desirable.
in the interests of justice, and in the
interest of the people's confidence in the
administration of this law that there
should be an appeal to the county court
judge. I do not for a moment question
the integrity of the umpires, but it is
always a satisfaction to feel there is a
court of appeal when one thinks there is
an injustice. So far as I know the
umpire under these Acts is in the position
of a judge. I know I have heard it
ruled in another place that his decisions
cannot be criticised in Parliament. He
is in the position of a judge, but without
the advantage to the public of holding
his court in public. I strongly support
the suggestion made by Senator
McLaughlin that there should be a
right of appeal to the county court judge.

CLAUSE 6 (Provisions to facilitate
prosecution of persons using vehicles without excise licences).
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to leave
out the Clause. When speaking on the
Second Reading of the Bill I intimated
my strong disapproval of this highly controversial Clause. I trust that I was not
unduly vehement, but I was very anxious
that all hon. Members should be led to
consider before we met again to-day both.
the wording and the implications of the
Clause. I was particularly anxious that
hon. Members should ponder over the
word "alleged," which comes so very
much, as I think, into the picture.
wanted to look into the word myself a
little, and I have done so without in any
way departing from my original views. I
will return to that question.
I
I
The
I told the House that the Clause was
a reproduction of Section 13 of the
Imperial Finance Act of 1936, and that
it did not pass through the Committee of
the Imperial House of Commons without
rather serious objection being taken.
did not remind the House of Sub-section
(3) of Section 113 of the Road Traffic Act
of 1930, although that particular statute
was referred to in the debate in the
Imperial Commons. I only mention it
now because it was mentioned as I say in
the Imperial debate. That Sub-section
I have quoted was limited to allegations
against the driver of a vehicle and was
of general application to offences. It
did not relate to matters of excise.
Title of the United Kingdom Act runs
rather differently from the Title of our
own Bill, but I do not think I need detain
the Committee by going into that because
having regard to such precedents as the
Imperial Statute affords and also, I may
say, the acceptance of this Clause in
another place, I do not propose to submit
that the Clause is not within the title of
the Bill. But I should like to say that
it is, so to speak, rather hard to get it in,
because it provides not so much for an
Amendment or even an extension of the
existing law as for the creation of entirely
novel responsibilities in respect to matters
which are foreign to the main purpose of
the Bill.
I
In short, I cannot regard the Clause
as appropriate to a financial measure.
think it is the introduction of one of
those things which if not unlawful are
at all events inexpedient. If such a
Finance Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
Clause is to be introduced at all I think
it might more conveniently be introduced
by way of amendment to some more or
less analogous Clause of some of our
Traffic Acts than as at present. That is
more a matter of personal opinion than
of any great importance. Before I pass
to attack the proposed Clause in detail
I should like to draw the attention of
the Committee to two important matters
wherein our existing law as regards the
motor code differs from that in force
across the water. First, let me remind
the Committee that it is required here
that every driving licence shall have
affixed to it a photograph, representing
the person to whom the licence has been
issued. Secondly, in addition to those
powers which we obtained under the Act
of 1926 as regards arrest of a drunken
or dangerous driver in circumstances detailed in that Act which include, the
Committee will remember, amongst other
things absence of identification marks on
his vehicle we have power under our Act
of 1934, which may be remembered
amongst other things as the Act which
introduces the agency clause, which presumes that the person so acting is the
agent of the owner of the vehicle in his
actions of negligence. Under that Act
we have unrestrained power of summary
arrest of any person committing certain
offences, which does not exist in the
United Kingdom. But hitherto we have
not given such power of arrest in the case
of the offence, reprehensible enough but
not felonious, of driving an unlicensed
The Committee might well be of
the opinion that an extension of this
power of arrest might be a better way of
dealing with the trouble to which this
Clause relates if legislation is required.
at all. As to that I do not know.
car.
Let me come to the Clause itself and
endeavour to exhibit or illustrate what I
regard as its more objectionable features.
Before I do that I want to lay down a
general proposition, with which I am sure
the Committee will agree, and that is, if
an unlicensed vehicle is, in fact, used on
the road it must be so used either with the
consent and knowledge of the owner or
without it. It must be one of these two
things. If the vehicle is used without the
consent of the owner I cannot imagine
that there will be any difficulty in securing the fullest assistance from that owner
in tracing the persons concerned. That is
where there is no knowledge.
But in
practice we are, of course, intending to
Ideal with the use of an unlicensed vehicle
with the knowledge and connivance of the
owner. Where that occurs I suggest that
the owner and driver of the vehicle are,
in fact, accomplices. I ask what is the
value of evidence given on statements
made by one accomplice against the other,
especially if the other is not present at the
time they are made.
Passing from that to this word
"alleged," of which I spoke, what is
meant by alleged? Who is going to do
the alleging? Under what circumstances
is it going to be done? In court or out
of court, or both? I am not satisfied
myself, I may say, that the word "alleged"
is limited to an allegation brought before
a court. Indeed the course of the debates
both here and in another place seem to
indicate the contrary. Alleged would
seem to have its normal meaning of a suggestion or supposition brought forward.
Surely in such cases, that is, the case of
the use of an unlicensed vehicle with the
consent of the owner it is the owner who
is the responsible person, the person you
want to get at. You must, I think,
remember that the owner like any other
offender cannot be asked to incriminate
himself in any way, and he is entitled to
legal assistance. That is the person we
ought to get at and the way to get at the
owner is to stop the vehicle on the road
and examine the driver's licence. After
that has been done it would be insulting
the intelligence of the constabulary to
suggest that the rest would be found
difficult. The vehicle has actually been
seen on the road; therefore, the question
of allegation disappears entirely. It has
given place to certainty. But unless the
vehicle has actually been on the roadnot is alleged to have been on the roadand unless the owner can be shown to have
been present at the time, how can he say
definitely and on oath who was, in fact,
driving, and, still less, who was in the
vehicle.
I am not myself in favour of any
Measure for the further promotion of
tittle-tattle, and still less for the
further promotion of perjury in
Northern Ireland. Further, let us suppose the owner plays the idiot child or
gives misleading information. What is
the penalty? There is no penalty pro-
Finance Bill-
Committee
21 OCTOBER 1937
vided for that. I will now leave paragraph (a) with a reminder of an ancient
proverb "First catch your hare, then
cook him," and "catch" means something
rather more tangible than mere supposition.
And now for paragraph (b) in which we
are conducted still higher up into the
clouds. First, we have the expression
"power to give." That would not go far
enough to enable a husband to give
evidence against his wife, and vice versa,
and the power can easily be nullified by
that simple non-possumus attitude which
not infrequently follows the appearance of
inquisitive persons, particularly if the
person happens to be a policeman. I do
not think that will help us very much.
Then we have the expression "may lead."
Is not that the very acme of nebulous
speculation? Does it not also suggest the
possibility of certain inquisitorial proceedings such as are anathema to the ordinary freedom-loving citizen? There is too
much indication of something that describes the first step towards third degree
methods, and those things are anything
but popular.
Again, in this paragraph (b) we are
definitely confronted with the presumption
that "allegation" is identical with fact.
The whole Clause seems to proceed on the
lines that because it is alleged so and so
has happened it has happened. I said a
few minutes ago that the use of an unlicensed vehicle is a reprehensible, and,
indeed, to some extent, a serious offence.
To avoid misunderstanding I am quite
prepared to go a little further, and to
enunciate that there is a moral duty
incumbent on us all to support the law
and to assist in bringing offenders against
the law to justice. That is all very well
as a moral precept, but there are some
methods of inculcating and trying to give
effect to that precept which not only fail
to achieve their object but positively militate against it. I think this Clause
would fall within that class.
Though the offence of using an unlicensed vehicle on the road, or causing it
to be so used, is a serious offence it really
is not high treason nor anything distantly
akin to treason. I suggest the offence is
distinctly less culpable than murder, rape
or burglary. Yet in the case of those
three felonies there is no provision in our
law for compelling the giving of information, or, except in court by order of the
court, the giving of evidence. I suggest
that one of the reasons for the absence of
such a provision is the unreliable and
worthless nature of any evidence compulsorily acquired.
I submit that, apart from the fact that
the Clause would prove unworkable in
practice we should be losing all sense of
proportion if we were to pass it, because
we would be giving special powers in the
case of comparatively minor offences which
we refused to give in the case of a serious
felony. As a matter of fact, we have not
been told anything of the extent of the
evil it is desired to circumvent, but whatever it
be I
may
that this Clause offers
say
no effective remedy. To my mind, it is
nothing but a quack nostrum fraught with
the germs of future trouble, and I invite
the Committee to reject it.
Mr. LONG: I am unable to accept the
hon. and learned Member's Amendment.
The Clause is quite relevant to the present
Bill, which, so far as this matter is concerned, amends the Roads Act of 1920,
which itself amended the Finance Act of
1920, the basis of the present system of
taxing mechanically-propelled vehicles.
As regards the hon. and learned Member's argument that the Clause conflicts
with the law of evidence, I would like to
point out that the principle embodied in
the Clause is not new, either to Northern
Ireland or to Great Britain.
The Motor Vehicles and Road Traffic
Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, in Section
10 (2), which relates to accidents, and
Section 113 (3) of the Imperial Parliament Road Traffic Act, 1930, which deals
with public service vehicles, contains
practically similar provisions. It might
also be pointed out that Section 13 (3) of
the Roads Act, 1920, goes further, and
places the burden of proof on the defendant in certain cases arising out of the
use of motor-cars; while, as the hon. and
learned Member is no doubt aware, Section
16 (2) of the Finance Act, 1934, passed
by the Imperial Parliament to deal with
the smuggling of cattle into Northern Ireland from the Irish Free State, obliges
any person in whose possession or control
goods are found to furnish to any officer
of Customs and Excise proof that the
goods have not been imported from the
Free State, or that if they have, the
Customs duty has been paid thereon.
Finance Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
The Clause is necessary to prevent loss
of revenue to the Road Fund, and to prevent the use of unlicensed mechanicallypropelled vehicles on public roads. Road
Fund licences are issued for either the
calendar year or for any of the four
quarters of that year, and each licence expires either on the last day of the year or
on the last day of the quarter in which
it was granted. Vehicle owners are given
fourteen days from the expiration of a
licence in which to renew the licence, and
frequently motorists use their vehicles unlicensed during those fourteen days
although it is an offence to do so.
To prevent this practice a quarterly
check is regularly carried out by the police
and the extent to which vehicles with
time-expired licences are used can be estimated from the fact that as a result of
these checks the police last year reported
to licensing authorities nearly 8,500 such
cases. Any person who uses an unlicensed
vehicle is liable to an excise penalty. It
is the user of the vehicle, not the owner
who is liable, and where the user of the
vehicle is not the owner difficulty frequently arises when a licensing authority
is endeavouring to ascertain who should
be prosecuted.
The Clause merely requires owners to
disclose the identity of drivers, and it is
no more unreasonable to ask them to do
so than in the other cases mentioned.
"Alleged," of course, means alleged by
a police officer. The hon. and learned
Member will appreciate that the Clause
will materially assist licensing authorities
in dealing with breaches of the law, and I
am sure he entirely agrees with its object.
