Poor Relief (Amendment) Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That the
Bill be now read a Second time."
As the law relating to poor relief now
stands, boards of guardians may give
relief in two alternative ways-by receiving destitute persons into the workhouse,
or by giving relief outside the workhouse.
Their powers in regard to able-bodied
destitute persons are, however, limited in
that only relief in the workhouse may be
given. Before the reorganisation of unemployment benefits and the establishment of the Unemployment Assistance
Poor Relief Bill-
Second Reading
26 OCTOBER 1937
Board to take over those who normally
engage in insurable occupation but who
have exhausted their unemployment insurance benefit, boards of guardians were
empowered under the Poor Relief (Exceptional Distress) Act (N.I.), 1928, to give
outdoor relief to able-bodied destitute
persons, provided an order declaring their
area one in which exceptional distress
existed had been made by the Ministry
of Home Affairs.
Exceptional distress orders were naturally not made in respect of every union,
so that in many unions no strictly legal
means were open to the guardians to help
deserving cases except by receiving them
into the workhouse. In these circumstances the guardians were often obliged
to give outdoor relief of very doubtful
legality in cases not suitable for indoor
relief.
The primary object of the present Bill,
achieved by the first Clause, is to empower guardians at their discretion to
give outdoor relief to able-bodied destitute
persons provided the recipients conform
to three specified requirements. The first
of these is that they must be residents
of the United Kingdom of seven years'
standing. This is a parallel to the corresponding requirement in the Representation of the People Acts. The second
requirement is that the recipient must
have registered his name at an employment exchange as available for employment. This has no connection with registration for insurance, and is open to
any person prepared to accept employment within his or her capacity at a suitable place. The third requirement is that
the recipient should be unable, at the time
of receiving relief, to support himself or
his dependants.
It should be noted that the Bill does
not throw responsibility on the guardians
for any additional persons; all those to
whom Clause 1 applies would already be
eligible for indoor relief, and would not
be eligible for assistance by the Unemployment Assistance Board or for unemployment insurance benefit. The effect of
the Clause is merely to give the guardians
a discretion in regard to the nature of
relief to be afforded.
The second Sub-Clause of Clause 1
merely protects the validity of the earlier
statutes mentioned therein and does not
affect any principle.
Although the prime necessity for exceptional distress orders is removed by Clause
1, emergency conditions may arise which
would make it desirable to give outdoor
relief to persons who, occupying small
holdings of land, are declared ineligible
for such relief by the Section of the 1862
Act mentioned in Sub-Section 2 (a) of
Clause 1. Power is accordingly preserved
in Clause 2 to make appropriate exceptional distress orders in such circumstances, and as in such an emergency the
normal funds of board of guardians might
not be sufficient to meet the demands on
them, provision is made in Sub-Clause 2
of Clause 2 for the financing of the emergency by temporary borrowing.
Clause 3, Sub-clause (1), regularises any
action of questionable legality taken by
guardians since the passing of the Unemployment Act (Northern Ireland), 1934,
in the way of giving outdoor relief to
persons made eligible therefor by the
present Bill. Sub-clause 2 of the same
Clause provides for the financing by temporary borrowing of any additional expenditure incurred by guardians during
the current financial year,
since,
obviously, such expenditure could not
have been budgeted for this year.
The only other provision to which I
should like to refer is that which substitutes a new section for Section 2 of the
Poor Relief Act, 1862. As the law at
present stands, persons in occupation of
land of a greater annual value than £3
are not entitled to receive outdoor relief.
Boards of guardians have found that this
limitation sometimes debars them from
granting outdoor relief in deserving cases,
and the extension of the limit from £3
to £4, which is provided for in the Bill,
gives effect to a recommendation put forward by representatives of boards of
guardians at a conference with the
Ministry of Home Affairs.
The Bill was circulated to boards of
guardians for their observations, and
Members will, no doubt, have observed
that resolutions objecting to it were passed
by a number of boards. As the objections
appeared to be based on misunderstanding
of the purpose and effect of the Bill, the
boards of guardians were invited to send
representatives to a conference with the
Ministry of Home Affairs, at which its
provisions were explained to them; and I
am happy to say that after a full discussion of the matter they agreed to accept
Poor Relief Bill-
Marketing of Eggs Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
the principle of the Bill, subject to a few
slight Amendments which were introduced
in another place, and are now embodied in
the Bill, as it comes before us.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: There is really very
little to be said about the Bill except that
throughout it proceeds on the line of
materially widening the discretion at present vested in boards of guardians. The
first Clause, I understand, had a somewhat troubled passage in another place,
and was ultimately amended to some extent. I can only say that I myself prefer
the first edition of the Clause to that which
comes before us. There is not the smallest
difference in principle, and I think I can
say there never was. There was a general
demand in the country for such alteration as the Bill seeks to give effect to, that
is, allowing land up to a valuation of £4
to come in. That, as the Parliamentary
Secretary has said, has been done. I shall
myself have a small Amendment to one of

be one to detain us for more than a few

of the Report and Third Reading stages
together. I support the Bill.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I would like to
suggest to the Government that they
should consider the advisability of extending the powers of boards of guardians to
enable them to use a wider discretion in
dealing with cases of small farmers seeking outdoor relief. Under this Bill, as
it stands, a board of guardians may grant
outdoor relief to persons occupying land
the valuation of which does not exceed
£4, but there are a large number of
holdings on land occupied by farmers
where there is no dwelling-house attached.
and to deal with cases such as those, I
think boards of guardians should be
given discretionary powers and not have.
their hands tied, as they will be under
this Bill. Cases might arise from time
to time where there might be illness in
the house of a small farmer. There
might be cases of infectious disease,
where the patients would have to be
sent to hospital, and I think the valuation of £4 should be increased so as to
cover such cases.
The members of a local board of
guardians generally know the standing
and the financial circumstances of a.
particular farmer, and although he might
own twenty acres of land he might still
be in a state of destitution from the
point of view that he had no help on the
farm and no stock. I suggest that the
Government should seriously consider at
least doubling the original valuation of
£3 that was in the Bill and making it
£6. That would give the guardians discretion to relieve farmer who
destitute, but whose valuation exceeded
the £4 laid down in the Bill. If such
a man was forced to enter the workhouse
in order to obtain relief it would be a
great hardship.

Marketing of Eggs Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The object of this Bill is
to effect a small amendment in the
Marketing of Eggs Act, 1936. As hon.
Members will recall, that Act provided
for the establishment of an Egg Marketing Committee to advise the Ministry of
Agriculture with regard to the fixing of
the prices at which eggs could be purchased by wholesale dealers from producers. Last August the Minister of
Agriculture received a unanimous report
from the Egg Marketing Committee,
which consists of representatives of producers and wholesalers and certain independent members, under the chairmanship of Sir Roland Nugent, regarding
the methods to be adopted in fixing the
price of eggs sold by producers to wholesalers. That system has recently come
345 Marketing of Eggs Bill-

26 OCTOBER 1937
Milk Bill
into operation, thanks to the valuable
services of the Egg Marketing Committee.
Under the Act of 1936 the Ministry
was given power by Section 7 to extend
the prices fixed for the purchase of eggs
by wholesalers to the purchase of eggs by
retailers. Producers are, however,
accustomed to receiving somewhat higher
prices from retailers than from wholesalers, and although it is desirable that
retailers should be brought within the
same system of price-fixing as applies to
the wholesale trade, it is considered that
they should be enabled to pay a somewhat higher price than applies in the
case of wholesalers since their expenses
are less, and producers who are in a
position to market their eggs direct to
retailers should receive any advantage in
price which the retailer is able to give
them.
The Bill which is before the House consequently provides that the Ministry may
fix prices to be paid by retailers not at the
same rate as those paid by wholesalers, as
could only be done under the Act of 1936,
but by reference to the price to be paid
by wholesalers. In other words, this
Clause will enable the Ministry to fix the
price to be paid by retailers at a certain
figure above the price paid by wholesalers.
Representations have been received from
producers and wholesalers and also from
retailers regarding the price to be paid by
retailers, and there is unanimous agreement among the interests concerned that
retailers should be allowed to pay a price
up to 1d a pound above the standard price
paid by wholesale merchants.
We are privileged to have with us today the Minister of Agriculture, who has
kindly attended to answer any questions
that may be raised. He has also kindly
offered to attend this House for the Committee Stage of this and the succeeding
Bill. I beg to move, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Milk and Milk Products Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: I am confident that this
Measure will command the entire
sympathy of all Members of this House.
It is designed to enable the Ministry of
Agriculture to increase the rates of equalisation payment and bonus payment to
creamery suppliers. Since the original
Milk and Milk Products Act of 1934 was
passed providing a guaranteed average
price of 5d and 6d per gallon in summer
and winter for all milk delivered to
creameries in Northern Ireland, plus a
bonus of 1d per gallon to producers of
Grade C milk who had to supply their
milk to creameries, there has been a very
substantial increase in farming costs. In
particular, prices of feeding stuffs have
advanced, and the cost of milk production
on this account is estimated by the Department of my right hon. Friend at between 3d and 1d per gallon above costs
two to three years ago. Labour costs
have also risen, and these circumstances
have been reflected in
a tendency for
supplies of milk to creameries to be reduced.
During the three years following the
introduction of the Milk and Milk Products Act of 1934 the supply of milk to
creameries increased by over one-third,
but during last summer there was a reduction in supplies compared with the
summer of 1936. Everyone interested in
the future of agriculture in Northern Ireland must have viewed this reduction with
misgiving. The creamery movement is so
intimately connected with almost every
phase of our livestock and poultry industries that any reduction in the supply of
milk to creameries must be a cause for
concern.
When the supply of milk to creameries
goes down it means, not alone that less
butter and cream are produced at our
creameries, but also that fewer calves are
being reared, fewer pigs fattened, fewer
poultry kept for egg production, because
the separated milk which is returned from
the creamery is the basis of calf production throughout a great part of the
country, and is also extensively used for
feeding to pigs and poultry.
Hon. Members will recall that as a
result of the increased costs of milk production the Joint Milk Council, in fixing
prices for the year commencing this
month, awarded an increase in price to
producers of about 21d per gallon above
the price originally fixed a year ago.
feel everyone will agree that it is only
equitable that creamery suppliers also
should receive some increase in their
price. Their milk is manufactured into
I
Milk Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
butter and cream-commodities which,
especially in the case of butter are specially subject to competition from imported
supplies largely from our own Dominions.
It has been impossible for the Imperial
Government to afford butter manufacturers in this country adequate protection
against imported supplies, and for this
reason the creamery movement has a
special call for financial assistance from
the State.
The present Measure does two things.
In the first place it increases the equalisation payment during the winter months
by d per gallon. This will apply to all
producers, and will, it is hoped, prove
an encouragement to winter milk production. In the second place, the bonus on
Grade C milk delivered to a creamery is
increased from 1d to 2d per gallon all
the year round. Undoubtedly one of the
biggest helps to the creamery movement
would be an improvement in the quality
of the milk supplied. Already nearly 40
per cent. of the milk supplied to our
creameries is of Grade C standard, and
it is clear that it is this improvement in
the quality of the milk which has resulted
in the very considerable improvement in
the standard and quality of Northern
Ireland butter and cream during the past
few years.
During the first nine months of 1937
we shipped some 467,000 gallons of cream
to England and Wales, and never before
has the reputation of our cream been
higher with the wholesale trade in that
country. In order to place the creamery
movement on an economic basis it is important that everything possible should be
done to improve the quality of the milk
supply, and it is for that reason that
it has been provided that the main assistance afforded by this Bill should be given
to the producer of Grade C milk.
Hon. Members will observe that the
opportunity of the present Bill has been
taken to make a number of administrative
amendments to the Act of 1934. I do
not think, however, that at this stage
I need trouble the House with regard to
the other provisions of the Bill, which
are of a purely administrative character.
I beg to move, "That the Bill be now
read a Second time."
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I

the price of farm produce has to be raised
so much to the consumer. Something
should be done to endeavour to reduce
the cost of feeding stuffs or to prevent
the price rising to anything like the extent
it has been doing. This increase has been
abnormal during the past few years,
especially since the Pig Marketing Board
came into existence. I understand that
the farmers lost the benefit of the prices
fixed by that board owing to the increased
cost of feeding stuffs.
Surely the Ministry of Agriculture can
devise some means of counteracting the
tendency for feeding stuffs to rise in price.
to the extent they have been doing
recently. Speaking the other day in the

price for their produce, and his reply was,
"I think the farmer is being favoured far

and too little to the consumer in the city."

consumers.

Sir BASIL BROOKE: I should like,
if I may, to thank Senator Leslie for his
kind remarks, and to assure other hon.
Senators that I appreciate their anxiety
with regard to the price to the consumer.
As Senator Leslie has said, this Bill will
in no way affect the price to the consumer,
because hon. Senators are probably aware
that when the original Bill was brought
in in 1936 a grant of £200,000 odd was
made by the English Treasury, out of
which the equalisation payment has been
made. The increase which this Bill seeks,
namely, d for all milk supplied during
the winter, plus 1d for milk that reaches
C standard, will be met through an adjustment of that grant which is being made
from the Treasury.
As I have said already, the Bill does
not seek to increase the price to the consumer. I am as anxious as hon. Senators
are with regard to that question. Although
I am Minister of Agriculture and I might
perhaps be accused of a desire to accomplish my ends through the consumers'

price in the Belfast market.

Sir BASIL BROOKE: We have a
little trouble sometimes. One hon. Member
made the remark that he feared that the
increase in cost would result in the consumption being seriously reduced. I was
asked a question last week in another
place with regard to the increase or decrease in the consumption of liquid milk
in this country since the Milk Act came
into force, and I was able to show that
some 500,000 more gallons of liquid milk
were consumed than were consumed prior
to the Act coming into force.
If I may
I will turn to the difference
between creamery milk and liquid milk.
This is a point which, I think, very often.
puzzles people who are not acquainted
with the two sides of that industry. The
producer of liquid milk is obliged to keep
his cattle in a certain state of health, that
is to say, if he is producing Grade A
milk and they react to the tuberculin test
they have to be destroyed, and if he is
producing Grade B milk he is not allowed
to supply milk from cattle showing clinical
signs of tuberculosis. In addition to that,
he has probably contracted to supply milk
to a dairyman for the whole year round.
He may at times find that the cows had
not calved at the right time, or he may
have calving trouble with his cattle, and
he has probably to buy cows to make up
the quantity, but so far as the buyers of
milk for manufacturing purposes are concerned he has no such obligations.
One of the objects of this Bill is that we
are trying to induce greater cleanliness
on the part of producers of milk for
creameries, but as things stand at present
in regard to 60 per cent. of the milk
brought to creameries for the manufacture
of butter and cream there is no obligation
for the byres to be kept in a certain
state of cleanliness. They are not
obliged to produce that milk at
any particular season of the
year and
they really supply milk when there is a
surplus. They will not put on the cartage
of milk, because from some of the outside
creameries to Belfast that would mean a
sum nearly equal to the cost of Grade C
milk at the present time. If that manufactured milk was allowed to come into
Belfast it is obvious that it would tend
to knock out the man who is a legitimate
milk supplier.
May I turn now to two points raised
by Senator Leslie ? One was in Section
3 (3) (a), "Where a creamery has failed
to remain open," etc. The object of
that is that one might possibly find that
a creamery manager did not wish to have
a creamery open on a certain day. The
result would be that he could not receive
Grade C milk. As practically all these
creameries there are only three exceptions are co-operative societies, it is
thought that if there are members of a
co-operative creamery who are not able
to get their milk into the creamery because the manager will not open the doors
on the proper day, suitable action will
be taken by the members and he will be
forced to do so. I hope that is clear.
Mr. LESLIE: Yes, I see the point.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: The other point
was the prohibition of producers from
selling skim-milk. The House will remember that a short time ago the Sale
of Ice Cream Bill was brought into this
House. Just after the Bill was passed it
was found that it was the habit of icecream vendors to buy skim-milk and mix
it with cream. By so doing they evaded

Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to ask
the Minister of Agriculture a question.
During his reply to the debate he made
reference to the testing of cattle,
especially cattle producing Grade A
milk, and he said that if one were found
to be suffering from tuberculosis it ought
to be disposed of. I would like to know
what steps the Ministry are going to take
to prevent that cow if she is sold by a
Grade A producer coming into the hands
of a Grade C producer? That cow could
be sold to a Grade C producer and the
milk could be distributed raw to the
consumers in Belfast or elsewhere. I
think that steps should be taken to ensure that when a cow is suffering from

so that the milk would not reach the
community.
Sir BASIL BROOKE: There are two
different types of these cows.
There may
be a cow which would react to a test and
her milk be perfectly all right. There
might be a gland in the neck affected and
the milk would be all right, but if you
got an animal which had tuberculosis of
the udder that animal would be destroyed
and the owner paid compensation. Any
animal producing milk which is tainted
is destroyed. In the case of an animal
which might react through quite a small
infection of one of the glands there is no
danger from the milk, but a Grade A
producer could not keep that cow.

Exchequer and Financial Provisions
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: This is a finance Measure,
and I will not detain the House with
lengthy explanations of its provisions. As
hon. Members are aware, it is necessary
from time to time to increase the limit
of money which can be lent by the Government Loans Fund, as the original
Government Loans Act gives control to
Parliament over the gross amount lent
by the fund without taking repayments
to the fund into account. The effect of
Clause 1 of the Bill is to authorise issues
to the fund up to £10,000,000. Although
the limit of £8,500,000, established in
1934, has now been exceeded, taking repayments into account, the amount in
fact outstanding is about £6,000,000.
Clause 2 relates to borrowings for the
Bann drainage scheme. The cost of the
scheme was originally estimated
£643,000, but 18 now estimated at
at
Financial Provisions Bill-
£858,000.
26 OCTOBER 1937
The reason for the increase
is that it has been found that the material
to be dredged from the bed of the river
is more closely packed and difficult to
remove in many places than the preliminary borings had indicated.
Clause 3 empowers the Ministry of
Finance to prolong the life of Ulster
Savings Certificates beyond the period at
the expiration of which they would otherwise require to be redeemed. The Clause
does not, of course, prejudice the right of
holders to demand repayment with
interest on the date of maturity of their
certificates. I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."

Labourers Cottages (Loans) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: This is a very simple
Measure which I am sure will appeal to
every Member of the House. During the
past year building costs have risen considerably, with the result that Rural
District Councils have been faced with
greatly increased expenditure when
formulating and carrying into effect
schemes for the erection of labourers'
cottages. The Government, recognising
that this increased cost of building would
either place a substantial additional
burden on the rates, or cause Rural
District Councils to hesitate in providing
necessary cottages, have decided to increase their financial liability in this connection, and this Bill is designed to implement this decision.
At present the Government makes a
grant of 40 per cent. of the loan charges
incurred by rural district councils in
carrying out building schemes, up to a
limit of expenditure of £350 per cottage.
Labourers Cottages Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
It is now proposed that the amount of
expenditure per cottage in respect of
which the 40 per cent. grant may be paid
be increased from £350 to £450. Hon.
Members will be glad to learn that since
the passing of the Act of 1935, which
renewed facilities for the erection of
labourers' cottages, very considerable
progress has been made by local authorities in the provision of labourers' cottages
throughout the country. No fewer than
24 schemes are now in
of comprocess
pletion, providing for the erection of
approximately 3,600 cottages, and I am
confident that the additional facilities
provided under the Bill will furnish a
further incentive to local authorities to
deal with the problem of accommodation
for labourers in a comprehensive way. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."

CLAUSE 14 (Claims and appeals).
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to move,
to leave out the words "and the decision
of such umpire shall be final and
conclusive." I think I am not going
too far when I say that it is practically admitted or has been agreed in
this House that in a case where an application is turned down by a referee or
umpire behind closed doors the applicant
should have the option of appealing to a
higher court, say, a county court judge.
It is very aggravating at times for oldage pensions committees and blind persons pensions committees to find cases
which they have recommended as very
deserving turned down by a referee or
umpire behind closed doors, and that the
applicant has no redress. I respectfully
suggest that it is at least disturbing to
the public mind at the present time to
find that in a great many of these Acts
the last word is left with the referee or
the umpire, and that the applicant has no
redress. Everybody knows that a referee
Report of Amendments

I
or umpire has not to give any explanation
as to why he arrives at his verdict.
think under those circumstances, and to
assure the people that they are getting
fair play, that the Government should
accept this Amendment and allow an
applicant, whose case has been turned
down behind closed doors, an opportunity
of appealing and placing his case in public
before a county court judge.
Question proposed, "That those words
be left out.'
""
Mr. LONG: Mr. McLaughlin's Amendment is to leave out the words "and the
decision of such umpire shall be final and
conclusive." The Amendment would not
be in the interests of the insured persons. At the commencement of the social
insurance schemes the question of the procedure for determining the claims of insured persons to benefits was considered.
It would have been possible to have allowed
disputes under the schemes to have
been dealt with in the courts. This would,
however, have involved expense to the insured persons and delay in dealing with
appeals, and so far as the inferior courts
of law were concerned it might have been
difficult to establish uniformity of practice
between different areas. Parliament
accordingly decided in the National Insurance Act, 1911, to establish special
tribunals for dealing with disputes on
claims. The decision of the umpire
under the unemployment insurance scheme
and of the referee under the health
insurance scheme was made final and
conclusive. The system has worked
satisfactorily for 25 years and has given
insured persons a readily accessible, expeditious, inexpensive, and expert court
of appeal from decisions of the Department on their claims. As was pointed
out by Senator Leslie in the Committee
stage, the words "final and conclusive"
are necessary in order to maintain the
system.
On the introduction of the Contributory
Pensions Scheme in 1925, it was decided
to extend the system to appeals under
that scheme also, and Clause 14 of the
Bill re-enacts the existing provision with
an Amendment to give the umpire jurisdiction, not only over claims, but also
over applications for admission to
voluntary insurance, and over all questions arising in connection with contribu-
363 Contributory Pensions Bill-
Report of Amendments 364
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
tions by voluntary contributors. The
provisos to the Clause reserve the existing right of appeal to the Ministry from
a decision of an approved society, and
to the High Court from a decision of the
Ministry on a question of insurability.
During the Committee stage, Mr.
McAllister criticised the umpire on the
ground that he is the nominee of the
Minister and is not, therefore, fully independent. As, however, hon. Members
will see from the Clause itself, the umpire
is appointed by the Governor and is an
independent statutory authority, and it
is not within the power of the Ministry
to question his decision. A further
criticism was that applicants suffer because of the absence of ordinary judicial
form, and because there is not, in every
case, a hearing in open court. The
umpire has an oral hearing in every case
in which he considers it necessary to get
the evidence of the parties, and he has
power to take evidence on oath.
One of the essentials in dealing with
appeals for pensions is that the system
adopted should be expeditious and inexpensive. The Amendment would, however, give not only the applicant, but
the Ministry, the right of appeal to the
courts from the decision of the umpire
and would lead to expense and delay.
In the interests of insured persons it cannot, therefore, be accepted.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In view of what
the Parliamentary Secretary has just said
I would respectfully ask what is the necessity for the local pensions committee? If
they make a recommendation to the
Ministry the referee or umpire can turn
it down, and there is no further appeal.
The local committee know the circumstances of the applicant better than any
referee or umpire does, and in spite of
that and in spite of the recommendation
of the local committee on pensions for
blind persons the umpire or referee can
turn the case down, and there is no
further appeal. I say that should not be.
Mr. LESLIE: Have we not got to look
at the question rather from this point of
view, that if we adopt my hon. Friend's
suggestion what is the use of an umpire
at all? Why should not the appeal go
straight to whatever authority is provided
whether it is the county court judge or
the archbishop, or anybody you like?
these words are taken out we are not told
to whom the appeal is to lie. The amendment I moved, and which was accepted,
went on an entirely different line; that is,
where any question of law arose in respect
of the jurisdiction of the umpire.
If
Again, I think I have myself frequently
objected to the Ministry holding inquiries
behind closed doors, but that is not the
same thing as an umpire. The umpire
is not an officer of the Ministry. He is
not appointed by the Ministry. He is
appointed by the Governor, acting on the
advice of the whole Cabinet conveyed to
him by the Prime Minister. The two
things are not in the least parallel-the
decision left to the umpire and the decision made by the Ministry. I do not
think it would serve any good object, and
it would be positively mischievous if this
amendment were carried.
Question (That the said words be left
out) put.

Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That the
promoters of Gilfillan's Divorce Bill and
M'Geagh's Divorce Bill, which have
been introduced into this House in the
present Session of Parliament, shall have
leave to introduce the same in the next
Session of Parliament, provided that
notice of their intention to do so be
lodged in the Library not later than
three o'clock on Tuesday, November
23rd, and that all fees due thereon, up
to that period, be paid.
"That the Bills shall be deposited in
the Library not later than three o'clock
on or before the third day on which the
House shall sit after the next meeting
of Parliament, with a declaration
annexed thereto, signed by the agent,
stating that the Bills are the same in
every respect as the Bills at the last
stage of the proceedings thereon in this
House in the present Session.
"That the proceedings on the Bills
shall be pro forma only in regard to the
stage through which the same have
passed in the present Session; and that
no new fees be charged in regard to this
stage."
Question proposed, "That the said
Motion be agreed to."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not, of course,
oppose the Motion, which is strictly in
accordance with precedent, but I cannot
refrain from pointing out that it is only
an
illustration of the unsatisfactory
character of the present arrangement in
dealing with matters of divorce. As we
have just seen, the granting of divorce
is to some extent dependent on the incidence of the sittings of Parliament.

Poor Relief (Amendment) Bill.
Order for the House to be put into
Committee read.
Resolved, "That this House do now
resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill." (Mr. Long.)
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Amendment of s. 1 of
10 and 11 Vic., cap. 31, as to outdoor
relief), ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 2 (Amendment of s. 2 of
the Act of 1862 as to outdoor relief).
The following section shall be substituted for section two of the Poor
Relief (Ireland) Act, 1862:—
Any person who is in occupation of
any land of a net annual value greater
than four pounds as shown in the
valuation lists for the time being in
force under the Valuation Acts (Northern Ireland), 1852 to 1932 (whether
any person is liable to pay rates in
respect of such land or not), and who
is considered by the board of guardians
to require relief, shall be relieved by
them in the workhouse and not otherwise.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to move
in the second paragraph to leave out
"four" and insert "ten.'
Before proceeding I should like to
protest against the way in which Bills
are rushed through the House. To-day
we have three Bills in Committee, and
no fewer than five Bills for Third
Reading. On Tuesday we had no fewer
than nine.
There is one question I should like to
ask with reference to this particular
Clause, and that is, why farmers have
Poor Relief Bill-
Committee
28 OCTOBER 1937
Surely
been singled out in this Bill.
if they are destitute they are entitled
to relief without being obliged to go
into the workhouse. The average farmer
with a valuation of £4 has what you
might call about a four-acre farm of
arable land. I think no one would suggest that a farmer with four acres of
arable land is able to maintain himself
and his wife adequately on that farm. I
think it should be agreed that if distress
overtakes this house and family they
should be entitled to outdoor relief without going into the workhouse.
I would respectfully suggest that the
average board of guardians, practically
the rural councils outside the city are
composed of farmers, and it is taken out
of their hands to relieve their fellow
labourers. I would go further and say
that there is no one who understands
the circumstances of the small farmer
better than his neighbour. If his neighbour happens to be on the board of guardians surely he should be entitled to
recommend that farmer for relief if he
is entitled to relief without going into
the workhouse. I think everyone will
agree that it would not be any harm if
the Government struck out this section
altogether, because as I said at the start
I do not see why the farmers should be
singled out any more than the man with
a dwelling-house or a business should be
singled out. I hope the Government
will agree to the suggestion of my amendment, that is, to make the valuation £10
instead of £4. By doing that they would
be doing justice to the farmer and giving
him a chance to live.
Mr. LONG: The Government cannot
accept this amendment. Prior to 1934,
as the hon. Senator is aware, a person
in occupation of more than acre of
land was debarred from obtaining outdoor relief. Based on the finding of the
Local Government Commission that "to
fix any area of land as a limitation is a
bad principle owing to the great divergence in the value of land," Parliament
in the Local Government Act, 1934,
introduced the principle of limitation by
valuation instead of limitation by size.
The limit fixed by that Act was a valuation of £3, although the Local Government Commission recommended that a
lesser valuation would in their opinion be
equitable.
The figure of £4, fixed under the
Clause at present under consideration,
was suggested by a representative deputation of boards of guardians-composed,
I may say, of the neighbours the hon.
Senator has spoken of-and when it is
pointed out, as was done in another
place, that a valuation of £4 brings in
farms of up to 30 acres in Northern Ireland, I am sure that the hon. Senator and
other right hon. and hon. Members of this
House will agree that considering everything the limitation now proposed is an
equitable one.
It would only be in cases of exceptional
distress, such as the wholesale failure of
crops, that it would be necessary to grant
outdoor relief to people with larger farms,
and the next Clause makes ample provision for granting outdoor relief to all
farmers without restriction should such a
calamity occur.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: With all due
respect to the Deputy-Leader of the
House I suggest that there are very few
farmers in this part of the country with
30 acres valued at £4 unless it is waste
land. I beg leave to withdraw the
Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the
Bill.

Mr. LONG: Mr. Leslie asked on the
Second Reading of this Bill if repayments made to the Government Loans
Fund could be loaned again so that the
gross amount issuing from the Fund
would be in excess of the limit laid down
by Parliament. The answer is, no. The
limit laid down by Parliament is in
respect of the total amount of loans
from the Fund since its inception and
takes no account of repayments.
Mr. Leslie also asked if the Ministry
of Finance had, in fact, issued from the
Fund loans in excess of the limit laid
down by Parliament in the Exchequer
and Financial Provisions Act of 1934;
and, if so, if they had been empowered
to do so under any other legislation. The
answers to these questions are that the
373 Financial Provisions Bill-
28 OCTOBER 1937
limit was passed in September last, and
that the excess has not yet received
Parliamentary sanction. The delay
which occurred between the passing of
the limit and the introduction of this
Bill cannot, I think, be considered
serious, and I am sure the hon. and
learned Member will agree that the
functioning of the Fund should not be
held up during periods when Parliament
is not sitting. I beg to move, "That
this Bill be now read the Third time."

is obvious that if such a contingency is

Expiring Laws Continuance Bill, which

do not think the House will wish to delay
the passage of the Bill, But I think it
was right to call attention to the matter.

Widows', Orphans' and Old Age Contributory Pensions (Voluntary
Contributors) Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Mr. LONG: Mr. Leslie has asked for
information about the meaning of the
expression "entry into insurance.'
occurs as soon
""
Entry into insurance is a question of
fact. So far as the combined scheme of
compulsory health and pensions insurance is concerned, entry into insurance
as the
person becomes
employed within the meaning of the
National Health Insurance Act. The
statutory provisions are contained in
Section 1 of the National Health Insurance Act, 1936, and Section 2 (1) of the
Widows', Orphans' and Old Age Contributory Pensions Act (Northern Ireland),
1936.
The latter provision will be replaced as from 3rd January, 1938, by
the definition of "insured" contained on
pages 30 and 31 of this Bill, and by the
provision in Clause 8 (I) (ii) (b).
So far as special voluntary contributors
are concerned, the date of entry into
insurance is the date on which a person
who fulfils the statutory conditions gives
notice in the prescribed manner in
accordance with the provisions of Sub-
Clause 1 of Clause 1 of this Bill. Sub-
Clause 4 of that Clause deals with the
special case of the person who does not
give notice until after his 55th birthday,
and who is to be deemed to have entered
into insurance on his birthday.
As I explained during the Committee
stage there is power to make regulations
for the transition of persons from employed insurance to special voluntary
insurance. In such circumstances the
insurance would be continuous and the
date of entry into insurance would be
the date on which insurable employment
commenced.
Mr. LESLIE: I am much obliged to
the Parliamentary Secretary for the
statement which he has given to us. I
think the explanation will be of value to
the public because owing, perhaps, to the
necessarily complicated nature of the Bill
the particular point could not be described
as very clear in the Bill as it comes before
Finance Bill
Third Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
us. As the Bill is word for word in this
matter the same as the United Kingdom
Measure, and as the regulations to be
made in due course will also presumably
follow the same example I do not imagine
any difficulty will arise. But it is interesting to note that the result of the information given us would seem to be that a man
entering into insurance under this new
arrangement will not necessarily have to
wait ten years for his old-age pension, nor
will his widow and orphans necessarily
have to wait two years for their pension.
That is a fact that really ought to be
brought out, and I think the Parliamentary Secretary concurs with that interpretation.
Mr. LONG: Yes.

Mr. LESLIE: As you are aware, I am
not moving my Amendment exactly in the
form in which it appears on the paper.
The reason is that it was pointed out to
me that the precedent I had followed in
the drafting of the Amendment was that
laid down by Clause 3, whereas Clause 4
is more appropriate owing to the slight
difference in the incidence of the cost of
maintenance of this or that form of track.
The exact Amendment I am moving is,
Clause 6, Sub-clause 1, to leave out in the
first line the words "It is alleged that a"
and insert "a vehicle has been used on a
road maintainable at the cost of any
county or county district and it is alleged
that such." The object of the Amendment is to make the provision, which the
Committee adopted the other day,
although by a somewhat narrow majority,
a little more comprehensible and a little
more workable, and, further, if I may
say so, to bring the quotations given by
the Parliamentary Secretary a little more
in accordance with the requirements of
the case.
I venture to think that this
Amendment will clarify the Bill, and will
prevent some of the difficulties to which I
alluded in Committee.
Mr. LONG: The Government accept
this Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
6, to leave out from the word "vehicle"
(using the vehicle) in line 22 of Sub-clause
1, paragraph (a), to the end of paragraph
(b). This Motion is to leave out paragraph (b), which I venture to think is not
of any great service to us. Now that we
have altered the first paragraph I do not
think that it would serve any useful purpose to leave paragraph (b) in the Bill.
The effect will be that the local authority
or the constabulary, as the case may be,
will now have a new power to require
information from the owner of a vehicle,
and to require, presumably, the production of books and papers, and stop there.
They will not be able to embark on a
general catechism which, although I do
not think was the intention, was I think,
the effect of paragraph (b).
Mr. LONG: The Government are prepared to accept this Amendment.

Question to Minister.
30-ACRE FARMS OF £4 VALUATION.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House if he can state how
many farms in Northern Ireland of an
extent of thirty acres have a valuation
of £4, and, if so, whether they are composed of arable land?
In putting this question I wish to protest against the Government repeating
statements made in another House which
cannot be verified. I protest against the
statement made here last Thursday that
there are farms of 30 acres and upwards
in Northern Ireland valued at £4. With
all due respect I wish to say that that
statement cannot be verified, certainly as
far as arable land is concerned. I have
made inquiries in County Armagh, and
I have gone so far as to look up the rate
books, and, with the exception of
mountain land, waste, or bog, there are
no farms of 30 acres valued at £4. It is
the other way about.
The average
valuation in County
Armagh is practically about £1 per acre.
In many cases it is £2 to £3. Therefore,
I do say, with all due respect, that if
the Government persist in the statement
that there are a number of farms valued
at this figure they should give at least the
percentage of such farms of 30 acres and
upwards valued at £4. If possible they
should give us numbers and tell us
whether this land is arable land or not.
Mr. LONG: It is regretted that there
are no existing statistics from which the
figures asked for could be extracted, and
the labour and expense involved in
carrying out a special investigation, to
enable the information to be obtained,
would hardly appear to be justified in the
circumstances.

Milk Bill
Adjournment
2 NOVEMBER 1937
Milk and Milk Products Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read the Third time."
Question proposed, That this Bill be
now read the Third time.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I just wish to get
some little information with regard to
the Grade C supply. In my district of
the country motor lorries only call three
days in the week, and very often the milk
is three days old before it goes to the
creameries. The test is made then, and
I do not think that is a very fair test if
the milk has been three days lying. Also
with the small farmers very often you
have four or five different milkings in one
churn. I feel that this does not give the
producer a fair chance of passing the
necessary test for that milk, and a
number of Grade C producers have been
turned down when applying for licences.
I feel that this is the cause of a great
many being turned down. I would like
the Ministry to look into this question.
and give the producers a fair deal in the
test made for the supply of Grade C milk.
Mr. LONG: I regret that this matter
was not mentioned in the House at an
earlier period, when I would have endeavoured to procure the information for
Senator Maguire. I regret that I personally am not able to inform him
as to the circumstances, but I will
make representations to the Ministry
with regard to the points raised, and if
the hon. Senator likes to put down a
question for a future date I will endeavour to reply to him in that case.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That this Bill do now pass."
-(Mr. Long.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.

Prorogation of Parliament.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command from his Grace the Governor to
read forthwith in this Chamber to the
Senators and Commons of Northern
Ireland therein assembled his Grace's
Proclamation proroguing this present
session.
Moved: "That the Gentleman Usher
of the Black Rod be commanded to inform the Commons that the Proclamation
proroguing the present Session of Parliament is to
to be read forthwith, and
inviting the Commons to attend in order
to hear the same."-(Mr. Long.)
On Question, Motion agreed to.
was
The Commons, being at the Bar, with
their Speaker, the Proclamation
read to both Houses of Parliament by
the Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of his Grace's Command), as
followeth :
.-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION.
For Proroguing the Present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance
of certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern
Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue, and dissolve the
Parliament of Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the
twenty-third day of November, one
thousand nine hundred and thirty-seven.
9 NOVEMBER 1937
Prorogation

Prorogation
for the despatch of divers urgent and
important affairs, and the Senators and
Members of the House of Commons of
Northern Ireland are hereby required to
give their attendance accordingly.
Now I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
Duke of Abercorn, Knight of the Most
Noble Order of the Garter, Knight of
the Most Illustrious Order of Saint
Patrick, Governor of Northern Ireland,
by this Proclamation do publish and
declare His Majesty's Pleasure that the
said Parliament be, as from the reading
of this Proclamation in the Parliament
of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday, the twenty-third day of November,
one thousand nine hundred and thirtyseven.
AND I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to
be that the said Parliament shall on
the said twenty-third day of November,
one thousand nine hundred and thirtyseven assemble and be holden at Stormont
GIVEN this ninth day of November,
one thousand nine hundred and
thirty-seven.
(Signed by his own hand)

Tuesday, 23rd November, 1937.
The Fifth Session of the Fourth Parliament of Northern Ireland, appointed to
meet 23rd November, 1987, in the First
year of the Reign of King George VI,
was opened by the Governor (His Grace
the Duke of Abercorn, K.G., K.P.) in
person.
The House met at Twelve noon of the
Clock, Mr. SPEAKER (The Right Hon.
Viscount Bangor, O.B.E., D.L.) in the
Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod to let the
Commons know It is his Grace's
pleasure that they attend him immediately in this House.'
""
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following speech from the Throne:-
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The present year has been a memorable one in the history of our Empire.
The celebrations solemnising the
Coronation of his Majesty King George
the Sixth will never be forgotten by
those who were privileged to witness
them, and demonstrated the depth of
affection in which their Majesties the
King and Queen are held by their
subjects throughout the Empire. Northern Ireland was graciously honoured by
the visit of their Majesties; and the
enthusiasm and spontaneous rejoicings
of our people on that occasion gave
abundant testimony of their love,
devotion and loyalty to the King and
Queen.
same

Although the improvement in trade
and employment that has been
marked in Great Britain has not been
experienced to the
extent in
Northern Ireland, I am pleased to learn
that there has been an increase since
the beginning of the year in the volume
of employment. The estimated number
of persons in employment in all industries, including agriculture, insured
under the Unemployment Insurance
Acts had risen in October to over two
hundred and fifty-seven thousand.
During the same period the Unemployment Register had fallen by over seven
thousand, notwithstanding the fact
that the inclusion of agricultural
workers in unemployment insurance
and the enlargement of the scope of
the Unemployment Assistance Scheme
have led to a much more complete
registration of unemployed persons than
at any previous time. During the past
twelve months some twenty-eight
thousand workers have been placed in
employment through the Exchange
service.
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE
It is gratifying that local authorities
and public bodies are availing themselves of the improved facilities for
undertaking works of public utility that
have resulted from the increases in the
rates of grant which came into effect
last February. Since the increased
rates became operative, works involving
a total expenditure of four hundred
and twenty-six thousand pounds have
been approved.
I am pleased to observe that the present trend of external trade indicates a
continuance of the improvement which
has manifested itself in recent years. 1
am gratified to learn that the value of
exports of linen goods to
Overseas
I
markets this year has so far been higher
than for the corresponding period of
any year since 1929, and that, in consequence, activity in the industry has
been at a higher level than has been
experienced for some time past.
trust that certain disturbing factors
affecting the industry that have recently
arisen in international markets will
prove to be only of a temporary nature,
and that very soon normal trading
relations will be resumed.
It is equally a source of considerable
gratification to me that a high degree
of activity has been maintained in our
shipbuilding industry where, in consequence, the rate of unemployment,
though still unfortunately appreciable,
has fallen steadily throughout the year.
It was impracticable last Session to
proceed with Measures for the amendment and consolidation of the law
regulating conditions of employment in

amendment of the Acts relating to
Blind Persons and the provision of

who

I am glad to learn that operations
under the Labourers' Acts are being
pressed forward, and that already
twenty-four rural district councils are
proceeding with the preparation of
schemes involving the erection of nearly
four thousand cottages.
The provision of suitable houses for
our working classes in Urban areas is
receiving the earnest consideration of
my Government and a measure dealing
with the matter will be submitted to
you.
I am advised that the Committees
which have been investigating the
question of the protection of the young
and the welfare of young offenders;
the law relating to the regulation of
shops in Northern Ireland; the conditions under which the organisation of
maternity services in Northern Ireland
is carried on; and the enlargement of
facilities for instruction of unemployed
young persons between the time they
leave school and the age of eighteen
years, will shortly conclude
labours, and I am confident that the
valuable information which has been
obtained as a result of the exhaustive
examination of these questions will
prove of the greatest assistance to my
Government in dealing with these
very important matters.
their

are no longer connected with recognised educational establishments, is a
matter of paramount importance at
will be

invited to consider proposals with this

Members of the Senate and of the

I am gratified to know that as a

and that our oldest and greatest industry, which affords employment to

to

insure themselves voluntarily for

Address in Reply to His Most Gracious
Majesty's Speech.
Mr. SPEAKER having read the King's
Speech,
Sir JAMES WILTON (in morning
dress): I beg to move:
"That the following Humble Address
be presented to his Grace the Governor
of Northern Ireland, to offer the humble
thanks of this House for the Speech which
his Grace has addressed to both Houses
of Parliament:-
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
"Your Grace,
We, his Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of the Parliament of Northern Ireland, beg leave to
offer our humble thanks to your Grace
for the Speech which your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament."
Whilst fully recognising my inability
to do full justice to the Speech which is
before the Senate to-day, I wish to express my deep appreciation of the honour
conferred upon me by asking me to move
the Address in reply to His Majesty's
gracious Speech. As hon. Senators will
have noticed, the Speech covers practically
the whole ground of the social activities
of Northern Ireland.
Whilst it is regrettable that the revival
in trade in Northern Ireland has not been
commensurate with that in other parts of
the United Kingdom, yet it is a matter
for congratulation that notwithstanding
the addition of new industries to the scope
of the Unemployment Insurance Act the
register of unemployed persons has, to
some extent, decreased. It is also a
matter for congratulation that our staple
industries of linen and shipbuilding have
shown improvement.
It is unnecessary to remind the House
of the very many remedial Measures
which the Government introduced last
session, and which we have assisted to
pass; but I would like to refer just
casually, perhaps, to the new Measures
which are indicated in his Majesty's
Speech. The question of housing is one
which is occupying the attention of the
entire United Kingdom, and I am quite
sure that we in Northern Ireland look
forward with considerable interest and
appreciation to the new Measures dealing
with that subject which are foreshadowed.
""
It will be readily conceded that the
provision of suitable housing accommodation in rural areas has been-to use a
hackneyed phrase "a long-felt want,'
but it is a matter of great gratification
that the Government propose also to introduce legislation with regard to housing
in urban areas. It is unnecessary to
remind hon. Senators who are acquainted
with conditions both in agricultural and
urban areas that this is a matter which is
acute at the moment, and I am sure we
will all look forward with interest to the
provisions of the proposed legislation.
It is also extremely gratifying to know
that in the near future legislation will be
submitted dealing with the conditions of
employment in factories and workshops,
the provision of assistance for blind persons, the recognition of young persons
under 16 years of age as being eligible
for medical benefits, and the welfare of
the young generally; the amendment of
the existing Shop Hours Act, and the
provision of facilities for young persons
between the ages of 16 and 18, for such
instruction as will assist them in finding
suitable employment. Most of these
matters are at present, as you are aware,
being considered by committees set up
some time ago. Speaking personally
as a member of some of those committees,
I can assure the House that the different
matters submitted to them have received
the utmost consideration, and every
source of information has been tapped in
order that Parliament may be in a
position to deal adequately with them.
when the time arrives.
Not the least important of all these
matters is the question of physical training, and the building up of a healthy
and vigorous race. We all welcome the
indication that a measure will be put on
the Statute Book dealing effectively with
this important subject.
It is only as it should be that the
agricultural industry should at all times
have a preferential claim on the attention
of Parliament, and it must be a source of
considerable gratification to His Majesty's
Ministers to know that their efforts in the
past to assist that industry have met
with, and continue to meet with, much
success.
Mr. LESLIE (in the uniform of a
Deputy Lieutenant): My first duty in
approaching the debate on the Address
is to apologise for the fact that I am
rising to second it. As the majority of
the House is aware, the duty of seconding the Address this afternoon was to
be discharged by our co-Senator, Sir
Roland Nugent. Unhappily he has been
taken ill, and consequently I have to try
to fill his place. I am sure we
all regret the absence of the hon.
Senator, if only because he has
rather a special knowledge of industrial questions, and I think that he
would have been able to address words
of counsel and instruction to the House
ST
t
a

[
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
23 NOVEMBER 1937
and to the Government.
The fact that
I am appearing as a substitute makes me
more than ever desirous of receiving from
the House that indulgence which for the
past sixteen years has never failed.
Passing to the Gracious Speech in reply
to which the Address is being moved, the
Speech opens, appropriately enoughindeed it could scarcely under existing
circumstances, have begun in any other
way-with an allusion to the visit of
their Majesties to Northern Ireland in
July of this year.
It records with satisfaction the loyalty and devotion of their
subjects and the heartiness of the welcome
extended to their Majesties. I venture
to hope that the recollection of that
welcome will induce their Majesties to
repeat their visit, not once, but many
times (Hon. Members: Hear, hear)-
and that we may look forward to the
privilege and pleasure of seeing them
again at not too late a date in the ensuing
year.
My hon. Friend who moved the Address
dealt pretty fully with what I might call
the miscellaneous details of the Gracious
Speech, and, therefore, I need not detain
the House with any detailed reference to
the great majority of them. But I
should like for a moment to draw attention to one or two of what I might describe perhaps as outstanding items which
are alluded to in the Gracious Speech.
There is an intimation that a Bill will
be brought before us for making provision
for medical benefit for young persons
under the age of sixteen who enter insurable employment. I think that demands
more than a passing reference, because,
as we know, there is still some tendency
to regard any forward step taken in
matters relating to insurance as simply an
additional pace in what has been called the
rake's progress, that is because all those
steps necessarily entail expense. I think
in the case of the particular subject to
which I refer it will be recognised that
the proposal is simply a natural development of what has gone before. That is
to say, the age for entry into insurance
having been reduced, and medical benefit
having been extended to those insured
at a later age, it necessarily follows that
the two periods of life should be brought
into relation, so to speak. Therefore, it
is not surprising, considering that our
Government is proposing to follow their
usual practice in these matters of conforming to the legislation on the subject.
passed across the water that I should
regard the proposal as a natural development of that matter, and a natural
development on which I propose to speak
again in a few moments.
Again, we are referred to the Labourers
Acts and to what has been done by
twenty-four rural district councils as regards cottages for their rural labourers.
We are told, I think, that 4,000 cottages
are in course of erection. Coming from
that part of the country from which I do
come, I take pride in the fact that almost
one-tenth of those 4,000 cottages are
going to be erected in the Ballymoney
rural district. I venture to suggest that
perhaps that is an example to other
districts.
Passing from the rural districts to the
question of providing houses for the
workers in the urban districts, it is, if
I may say so, a far more difficult proposition. We have to remember that
what we are really considering is to provide suitable houses at rents which the
occupiers can afford to pay, and it is
forced upon us that that rent must be
an economic rent-economic from the
point of view of the council and the
individual.
That is the gulf that has to be bridged.
It can only be bridged by assistance from
the funds at the disposal of the State, the
funds directly voted by Parliament, or
from funds of the municipalities themselves. We know that various parts of
our area—I am afraid the City of Armagh
is as good or as bad an example as I can
take-suffer from inadequate housing
facilities. I believe that the ancient city
has difficulties of its own, difficulties of
area and others that I need not mention,
but I do believe there is a great want in
that particular city for houses.
There is one thing I should like to urge
on the attention of the Minister who will
be responsible for introducing legislation
on this subject, and that is the necessity
for avoiding undue interference with the
local authority directly responsible for
the erection of the cottages. The plans
of the local authority are usually drawn
as a result of experience, and particularly
of local knowledge. It is very regrettable
that there should be any delay in the
approval of their plans, or if there should
be-and I do not say there has been-any
attempt at dictating to the local authority
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
with a view to driving them all into the
same mould. I hope that anything of
that sort will be avoided.
We notice that the agricultural community is referred to, and the hope is
expressed that the improvement which it
is claimed has already been effected will
be increased and amplified. What I believe your farmer wants more than anything else is capital. He wants to be
able to raise money on reasonable terms
on the security of his land, subject, it
may well be, to his already current
annuity for land purchase. I believe that
is the crying need of the farmer to-daymore capital. It must be remembered
that, after all, if your farmer is an arable
farmer or chiefly a grazier he has got to
bear the expense of some eight or nine
months before any return can be expected,
and if he is going to embark on business
of any size whatever it does involve a
serious strain on nis capital resources.
Some provision should be made for
loans to farmers on perhaps rather more
convenient terms-I do not say less exacting terms, for the arrangements made for
borrowing money for the purchase of
cattle are not in any way extortionate,
but they have been found in practice
difficult of compliance, and I would like
something done in that direction.
Again, reference is made to work which
was done last Session. Indeed the whole
Speech, if I may so, refers rather to work
done not only in the last Session, but,
by necessary implication, in the Session
preceding. The particular instance taken
is the contributory pensions scheme which
we well remember passing at no very distant date. That scheme is, it seems to
me, another instance of development. If
you follow, not only the subject but the
various Acts and Statutes to which it
relates, and consider the periods during
which these were passed, I think it must
be admitted that the whole code or succession of codes points to a steady development by the Government of
scheme since it took over some sixteen
years ago. I think that is so.
I think the Government in its general
policy has stood the test of time. I do
not say there have not been mistakes here
and there, but I do ask this House and
the people of the Province to remember
that it is not fair to blame the Government for any mistake that they may
detect in a Statutory Measure. The
blame rests with Parliament, for Parliament passes the Bill, which is only introduced by the Government. However, I
think it will be found when, as I
imagine, at no very distant date the
Government goes to its great assize in the
constituencies, that the Government's
record will be recognised as good and that
the people will continue it in office with
a renewed vote of confidence. I have
great pleasure in seconding the humble
Address.
Question proposed, That the following
humble Address be presented to His
Grace the Governor of Northern Ireland,
to offer the humble thanks of this House

"Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of the Parliament of Northern Ireland, beg leave to
offer our humble thanks to your Grace
for the Speech which your Grace has
addressed to both Houses of Parliament."
Mr. LONG: It is with very great
pleasure that I congratulate the distinguished members who have moved and
seconded the motion now before the
House on their most interesting and able
speeches. The hon. and gallant Senator,
the Mayor of Londonderry, is one of our
most respected Members, not only because
of his position as first citizen of a great
and historic city, but also because
wherever he moves he brings with him
those qualities which have made him a
great sportsman and a gallant soldier.
From the seconder of the motion, Mr.
Leslie, this House has learnt to expect
informed and constructive contributions
to its debates, the fruit of an acute and
cultivated mind and of extraordinary
powers of application.
The hon. and learned Member has
spoken eloquently of the Coronation of
His Majesty King George VI and of the
rejoicings to which this event gave rise.
The magnificent loyalty to the Throne of
our people was never, I think, more
wonderfully demonstrated than on the
occasion of their Majesties' visit to Northern Ireland. The most pleasing aspect
of the celebrations was the spontaneous
joy and enthusiasm everywhere
evidence.

Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
23 NOVEMBER 1937
We are fortunate in that the Coronation year of His Majesty has been marked
by a continuation of the steady improvement in the economic position of the
Province which has been maintained
during the past five years. The Gracious
Speech reminds us that this improvement
has not been so noticeable here as in Great
Britain. The cause lies in the large
degree to which we are unfortunately
dependent for the prosperity of the two
great industries of linen manufacture and
shipbuilding, and of some of our smaller
industries, upon world economic conditions, which are improving but slowly,
and in the face of grave difficulties to
which the Gracious Speech made some
allusion. It is a matter for thankfulness
that in spite of these unfavourable
circumstances such substantial progress
has been made. I would ask the indulgence of the House to quote figures which
give some measure of this progress.
The total value of linen goods of United
Kingdom manufacture exported during
the nine months ended the 30th September last amounted to nearly six million
pounds, a higher value than has been
attained during the first nine months of
any year since 1929, and higher by nearly
£670,000 than the value of linen
exports in the period ended the 30th
September last year. The tonnage of
vessels launched this year from the Belfast
shipyards already amounts to 67,800 tons,
while the tonnage launched during the
whole of 1936 was 62,500 tons.
Mention was also made in the Gracious
Speech of the increase in employment and
consequent fall in unemployment, which
have occurred during the past year. The
average number of persons in insurable
employment for the ten months ended
30th October last was 213,000. This represents an increase of 5,000
over the
number of persons in similar employment
last year, while a decrease of 7,000 has
occurred in the number of unemployed.
These figures are not spectacular, but it
will be agreed, I think, that they are
encouraging. Although, as I have already
stated, the steady progress of industry
over the past few years has been to no
small extent dependent on world factors
over which we have no control, contributory factors have also been the steadiness
in difficult times of the British people and
the sustained efforts of Government in
Great Britain and in Northern Ireland.
The Government of Northern Ireland
recognise that the road of trade recovery
and of increasing employment is not easy
to find or, once found, to follow, and, as
is stated in the Gracious Speech, they intend to make further efforts to broaden
the industrial basis of our economy by
offering further facilities to industrialists
who establish new industries in the Province. Several reference were made in
the Gracious Speech to existing and to
proposed legislation having as its object
the social welfare of the people. Such
legislation is now recognised as one of
the most important functions of Government, and I am sure the proposed
measures relating to the housing of the
working classes in urban areas, to the
provision of facilities for physical training and recreation for our people, to the
provision of medical benefits for young
persons in insurable employment, and to
the amendment of the law regulating
conditions of employment in factories and
workshops, and of the law relating to
blind persons, will be welcomed by this
House.
I
I should like at this point to express my
appreciation of the remarks made by the
hon. Senator the Mayor of Londonderry
on the work of the several Committees
mentioned in the Gracious Speech.
should, perhaps, also refer here to the
proposal to allow teachers on retirement
to make provision for their dependents by
the surrender of part of their pension.
Similar legislation exists in Great Britain
both for teachers and civil servants, and
has recently been applied to Northern
Ireland civil servants.
Both the Mover and Seconder of the
Motion have made reference to the need
for suitable housing accommodation for
workers in rural areas. I would like to
say on this subject that the Government
are anxious that good housing should be
available to every citizen of Northern Ireland. Since the passing of the Labourers
Act (Northern Ireland), 1935, steps have
been taken by 25 rural district councils to
formulate new schemes involving the
erection of 3,656 cottages and Provisional
Orders have been made in respect of eight
schemes, and while building is at present
going forward in only one rural district
it is expected to commence shortly in
others.
I am
sure hon. Members were much
pleased with the allusion in the Gracious
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
Speech to the improvement in the position of our farming community. This
improvement is due, in no small degree,
to the careful planning of Government
both in Great Britain and Northern Ireland. Our output of pigs has increased
two and a-half fold; our supply of milk
to creameries has increased by more than
one-third; exports of eggs have increased
by a similar amount, and shipments of
fruit two and a-half fold. With regard
to forestry, the Ministry of Agriculture
has itself planted an average of 2,000,000
trees each year over the past five years,
and, in addition, has supplied farmers
with a yearly average of over 150,000
young trees for planting.
Agriculture has been accorded the consideration due to it as the most important
of our industries, and the Northern Ireland farmer will benefit, not only from
the facilities and encouragement offered
by the Government here, but also from
the facilities offered by the Government
at Westminster under the Agriculture
Act of 1937. Under the provisions of
this Act farmers may purchase lime at
half, and slag at three-quarters of its
existing price, and are secured against
loss through low prices for certain of their
crops. These facilities, the increased
prices guaranteed to the farmer for his
milk, the stabilising effect on what tend
to be fluctuating markets, of the various
marketing schemes, will ensure, it is
hoped, that the new measure of prosperity enjoyed by the farming community
will increase.
In conclusion I should like to say that
while, in a world where government in
many countries seems to be occupied more
with seeking such unsubstantial ends as
military glory, or even that vague thing,
prestige, the matters with which we have
been concerned may well seem tame and
uninteresting. They represent, however,
practical efforts to attain practical ends,
and the lot of Northern Ireland will be
happy if it is given peace and security
to pursue aims of the kind indicated in
the Gracious Speech.
Mr. MCALLISTER: It has never been
my lot to listen to a more hopeless speech
than the Speech on this occasion. It is
bereft of the slightest practical hope of
any redress for the grievances which the
people of this country labour under at
the present time. I would like to take
an early opportunity of congratulating
the hon. and gallant Member who proposed and the hon. and learned Senator
who seconded the Motion, and congratulating them particularly on their courage.
The hon. and gallant Member, who proposed the Resolution, is well known
throughout Northern Ireland for his
sporting qualities. To describe a man as
a good sport is one of the most comprehensive terms to commend him to his
colleagues wherever he may be, or to the
general public.
I have always had the greatest possible
respect for the Parliamentary resource
and skill, and the wonderful, untiring
industry of the hon. and learned Senator
who seconded the Motion. But it shows
what hope there is when even a Parliamentarian of the resource, experience and
skill of Senator Leslie himself had to
admit that practically the entire Speech.
referred to past legislation, and, therefore, the present Speech holds out no
hope of any practical legislation during
the future periods.
It is stated in the King's Speech that
trade and employment have improved in
this country, but not to the same extent
as in Great Britain. But there is nothing
in the Speech to indicate any legislation
which is going to improve that position
and to attempt to bring it up to the standard in Great Britain. I say without
fear that if this area were put to the
same test as several areas in Great Britain
it would undoubtedly be dealt with and
treated as a distressed area, and if the
amount of unemployment-much more
than in England comparatively speaking
-and the amount of distress in this area
are considered it is futile to say that it is
not a distressed area compared with those
areas in England.
I
I am glad to notice that some legislation is to be introduced to improve the
position of blind persons, but I would like
to draw the attention of the House to this
particular aspect of blind persons.
gather that the legislation proposed to be
introduced is to reduce the age of persons
qualifying for that pension. But there
is one great injustice in the present legislation dealing with blind persons, and
that is in the wording of the Section of
the Act of Parliament entitling them to
the pension. I quite agree that grave
injustices have been done to persons by
