the Ministry and umpire, but as a lawyer
I say they have no alternative. The
wording of the Section is so strict and
restrictive that if persons can make a cup
of tea for themselves they are not qualified
to get the blind pension.
She
Take a case that has been ruled, the
case of a lady who has been a dressmaker
all her life and lived by sewing.
has lost the sight of her eyes and is almost
blind, but she can do some other work.
She applied for the pension, but neither
the official of the Ministry nor the umpire
could grant it. I would like to stress
this view to the Government that if
justice is to be done to blind persons and
if this is to be an effective Bill it is absolutely essential to make it more elastic
than the present Section entitling them
to qualify for the pension.
are
There is one great and widespread
grievance in Northern Ireland, though it
does not apply so much to Belfast as to
urban areas. In Belfast there
many landlords, and, consequently,
there is competition between landlords.
But in urban areas the whole town
belongs to one landlord. There is no
competition, and any tenant or prospective tenant has to accept such terms as
he may lay down. If he insists on a
building lease in which there may be a
valuable consideration that lease may be
for sixty or ninety years, and according
to present legislation, although the tenant
may have spent thousands of pounds on
the buildings, when the lease falls in they
go to the landlord, although he has not
contributed a penny, and has drawn his
ground rent during all that period.
Years ago a Committee was set up by
the Government to deal with this question, and, although the majority report,
would not have covered the grievance, the minority report, which I
signed, would have remedied it.
The Town Tenants Bill of 1906, when
introduced in the English House of Commons covered the grievance I complain
of, but an Irish landlord, by means of a
very ingenious amendment, rendered it
practically valueless so far as ground
leases are concerned. I say this Government is a landlord's Government, performing the same work as that landlord
did in the British House of Commons;
otherwise they would do something to
remedy the crying grievance of the expiring leaseholders of Northern Ireland.
It is also strange that although so
much correspondence and discussion has
taken place on the question of the recent
Transport Act, which I think was the
wildest gamble any Parliament has ever
undertaken, there is not a single reference in the Speech to that widespread
grievance. There is not the slightest
hope that the admitted injustices which
have been inflicted on the people by that
Measure will be dealt with. I am sorry
that the Speech is not of a more hopeful
description, and I think it is wrong to
refer to the improvement in trade and
employment in Northern Ireland as being
in any way permanent. It is merely a
matter of temporary increased employment due to the construction of armaments and shipping, and when that comes
to an end, as it must do in a comparatively short time, I shudder to think what
is going to happen to many of the people
of Northern Ireland.
There is no reference in the Speech
to what the Government propose to do
to remedy permanently this growing and
distressing evil of unemployment. It is
a most unhealthy evil, because the thousands of men and women who are willing
and anxious to work and cannot obtain
it become depressed and degenerate in
mind and spirit, and that is what Communism springs from. That is the best
hot-bed to create Communism, and I
think the Government should have made
a more determined effort to grapple with
this problem, because it cannot go on
permanently if the Government of the
country is to be continued.

Captain PERCEVAL - MAXWELL:
The task of a Member of this House who
follows the speeches of the four distinguished Members, who have just spoken,
is not an easy one, for it could not be
expected that they would leave many
points of the Speech untouched.
there are two points to which I should
like to draw attention. We can all agree
and congratulate the country and the
Government on the fact that there has
been some improvement in the trade position in this country, and on the fact that
energetic steps are definitely being taken
to encourage industry, and, if possible,
to bring new industries from across the
water. I think I am justified in saying
that I believe you will find that the future
of industry in this country rests to a great
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Captain Perceval-Maxwell]
extent on the development of our own raw
materials. We do not possess coal in
Ireland, but we do possess, in great
measure, a thriving agricultural industry
and as that industry increases and thrives
its by-products will also increase.
I believe a great deal can de done to
foster and develop industry by encouraging the development of the byproducts of agriculture. I am not the
only one who is of this opinion, for there
has been a tendency quite recently to increase industry in this way. I am informed that a factory for spinning wool
for carpets has been set up in Newry,
and that at Castleisland there is a factory
for weaving carpets. I believe also that
the Newforge Company have started a
factory for producing pig and fowl meal,
and if industry can be developed on
those lines I believe you have the real
secret of industrial progress in this
country. For our raw materials we cannot afford to rely to any great extent on
countries outside our own border. We
have only to look around Europe to find
what difficulties have arisen through a
possible shortage of raw materials owing
to war scares, and if we are to encourage
industry at home we must provide not
only the raw materials necessary for that
industry, but also houses and social
services for the people who are going to
be engaged in those industries. We must
have efficient drainage and water schemes,
and all the concomitants that go to the
maintenance of a population in an industrial centre.
Some time ago I did suggest in this
House that the Government should undertake an inquiry as to how the water
supply of Northern Ireland could be
diverted to areas in which it was most
needed. I understand that a committee
has now been set up to consider this
question in relation to a small area, but
there are other areas where industries
might be set up, and I believe this inquiry should embrace a much wider area
and should consider the desirability of
having one or more water authorities
other than the Belfast water authority
to ensure that water is carried to areas
where it is most needed. There is no
doubt that rural drainage is a matter
that will have to be attended to sooner
or later if industry is to be encouraged.
Then there is the tourist industry,
which is greatly increasing in this
country, and for which provision must
sooner or later be made by providing a
larger number of hotels and other resources for tourists who visit our shores.
I could go on for some time suggesting
other industries that might be developed,
but I do not wish to detain the House
longer than is necessary.
There is a very
large and important industry of the
cellulose type, and we produce many byproducts which might be diverted to that
industry. I see no reason why we should
not produce industrial alcohol in this
country. It is common knowledge that
industrial alcohol can be produced from
things other than molasses, and if for any
reason the importation of the necessary
raw materials was held up, wood, grain,
and other materials could be used to
supply the deficiency.
I am glad to see a reference in the
Gracious Speech to the Committee, which
has finished its labours, relating to
children and young persons. I think
that in the report of that Committee,
the Government will find a great deal
of food for thought. Having considered
the matter from every point of view, I do
hope the Government will find it possible.
to introduce legislation at an early date
to carry out at least some of the findings
of that Committee. Medical science
and child welfare work have increased
enormously in England and Scotland and
in practically every country in the world.
since 1908, when the last Children Act
was passed, and there are many aspects
of the situation in which this country
is still behind other countries. Therefore I hope no pressure of business will
be allowed to interfere with the Governattention
ment giving the
that is
necessary to this important subject.
After all, the children are the guarantees
of a firm and secure State in the future.
The Government has been congratulated on the improvement in agriculture,
but I should like to add that not only is
the Government to be congratulated but
the farmers as well, for I believe that
without the co-operation of the farmers
what the Government has done would
have had relatively little effect. I
believe that on the whole the farmers
have co-operated well with the Government Measures and that they are satisfied
that those Measures have improved their

The first and most important reference

the point we are more concerned about

that took place to our own city. While
reference has been made to the enthusiasm and the spontaneous rejoicing of
our people there is one thing I feel extremely proud of, and that is that the
working-class people on that occasion
showed their deep and sincere loyalty
and love of His Majesty more than any
other section of the community.
I had an opportunity of travelling
over the route the night before the visit
and I was very much struck, going along
streets of the smaller houses, to see the
splendid decorations-decorations which
must have been put up at a tremendous
sacrifice by these people who are in
receipt of small wages and have very
little to spare for providing decorations.
All along the route, particularly in the
working-class quarters, it was remarkable to see the money that had been
spent and how enthusiastic the people
were to show their love and loyalty to
His Majesty. Strange to say, in those
districts where the better-class people
live
some of the houses had not the
semblance of a Union Jack or flag exhibited. I do say if Northern Ireland is
to be kept in the Empire it is the
working-class people we have to depend
upon.

great deal could be done and should be

surface for any person to walk on,
especially a man with thin boots. I was
on one occasion visiting a shipyard
worker who was within a few minutes of
his last. It was in one of these narrow
streets with cobble stones, and when a
country cart passed over the street at
that particular time I thought it was a
terrible hardship. I thought it was a
terrible hardship on a man whose last
few minutes in life were passing that he
should be placed in such an uncomfortable position by the noise of this traffic.
In my opinion, such streets as these
should be re-surfaced either with concrete
or tar macadam, especially when the
Government now contribute 75 per cent.
of the wages cost.

The reference in the Speech to the

promote physical training schemes. I am

In this connection might I point out to
the Government that Belfast, and in fact
all towns in Northern Ireland, are very
much in need of baths. I am informed
that there is no better means of healthy
exercise than swimming. I understand
that every limb in the body is in movement when a person is swimming.
The Government could do a great deal
in the matter of physical training by
encouraging local authorities in Belfast
and provincial towns to provide better
baths. We in Belfast are very much
in need of more and better baths.
People have too far to travel to the
existing baths in the City, and I know
that the Corporation have the question
under consideration at the present time.
The difficulty is the heavy cost and the
fact that the ratepayers are bitterly
complaining of the high rates, but if the
Government would come to the rescue
and offer financial assistance for the
erection of more and better baths I am
convinced that it would be money well
spent.
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
25 NOVEMBER 1937
The Government express their satisfaction that they have been able to
restore the cuts in teachers salaries, and
they now propose to make the necessary
arrangements to enable teachers to
surrender part of their pension to provide a pension for the widow or daughter
on the death of the pensioner. I should
like to see the Government going a little
further and finding positions for the
young men and women when they finish
their training at the Stranmillis Training
College. When there is a vacancy in any
school in Belfast it is heartrending to see
the large number of applicants there are
for the position. Why should it be
possible for both a married woman and
her husband to be drawing at the same
time either a principal's or a teacher's
salary while there are young people who
have finished their training unable to
find positions in schools. In my opinion
married women should be prohibited
from continuing as teachers in schools,
and I hope the Government will take this
matter up without delay. If her husband is in good employment surely the
married teacher should be able to stay
at home and allow her husband to provide for herself and family, and thus
give the young unemployed teachers an
opportunity of getting appointments.
Gratification is expressed in the Speech
that persons of small means who are outside compulsory contributory insurance
schemes are now able to take advantage
of the voluntary system. That is a great
step forward. I can look back to the
time when the insurance scheme was
first put into operation in 1912.
that time I represented the engineers
union in Manchester, and I remember we
had a considerable amount of discussion
about the rules to be introduced by the
society to deal with this insurance
scheme. Since that day insurance has
been extended to an enormous degree,
and great improvements in the scheme.
have been made. The contributory pension scheme for persons of 65 years of age
has been introduced, as well as widows
and orphans pensions, but notwithstanding this great progress I believe there is
still a gap to be bridged.
In my opinion there should be something in the nature of family allowances
provided. At the present time very
careful consideration is being given to
the declining birth rate. It is generally
recognised that this is a very serious
problem, especially for a country like
Great Britain which has Colonies to
populate. Our birth rate is steadily
declining, and in the course of a number
of years the population will be considerably lower than it is at the present time.
Is this not partly, at any rate, due to
the fact that we have adopted a standard rate of wages for all classes of
workers? Both the Government and
local authorities recognise a standard
rate of wages for all workers, and even
the trade unions themselves recognise a
minimum rate. The trade union movement said in effect that all workers are
not equally capable and valuable, and
the more capable and intelligent tradesman should sacrifice something of his
worth for the sake of his less fortunate
brother.
But it is hardly fair to insist on a
standard rate of wages for all workers,
for this reason. The single man, without family responsibilities, who is in
receipt of the standard rate of wages,
can live in luxury. The married couple
who have only one or two children are
not so badly off, but the poor man who
has a wife and six or eight children to
provide for on the standard rate of wages
is in an unfair position. I do think
there is an opportunity here for the
Government to do something useful
through the insurance scheme, even if
they require a little higher contribution,
by giving the man with more than two
children an allowance of, say, 10s. a
month for each child. It is possible that
that might solve the problem of the
declining birth rate, because if a man
believes he is going to be deprived of the
pleasures and luxuries of life if his
family grows he is very likely to invoke
the practice of birth control.
The Government have already recognised the justice of this proposal, because
in connection with unemployment benefit
they allow so much for a man and his
wife and give a certain allowance for
each child. They thus take into consideration the size of a family in assessing the amount of benefit to be granted,
and the large family man is encouraged
by the provision that is specificially made
for each of his children. As I have indicated, the dice is heavily loaded against
the poor man receiving the standard rate
of wages who has a wife and five or six
children to provide for.

[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
In conclusion, I should like to express
the opinion that the Gracious Speech
holds out the promise of some very beneficial legislation during the present

most careful and favourable consideration when it comes before this House.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I desire briefly to
refer to the large number of unemployed
in Northern Ireland, and particularly to
the delay that takes place on the part
of the Ministry of Home Affairs in
approving of relief schemes submitted to
them by local authorities. Two months
ago the Belfast Corporation passed a
relief scheme for street improvements,
and appointed a deputation to wait on
the Ministry of Home Affairs with a view
to securing the grant of 75 per cent. of
the wages cost. The total cost of the
scheme was £238,430, the grant was
estimated to be £88,404, and the net
cost to the Corporation £150,000. The
delay that has taken place has prevented
many of our unemployed obtaining work
on the scheme before the festive season.
If the Ministry had met this deputation
from the Corporation immediately after
it was appointed, at the present time.
there would have been 200 men employed
on street improvement in Belfast, and
that would have been very helpful. The
full scheme will give employment to 450
men for two years.
I think it is very
essential that these schemes should be
expedited by the Government. The
Ministry in giving their grant will have
naturally a set-off or saving. They will
save in the payment of unemployment
benefit and also in exceptional distress
relief. I would appeal to this House to
urge the Ministry to speed up the scheme
placed before it by the Corporation.
I also wish to support the hon.
Senator McAllister in the matter of
Blind Persons' Act. I certainly agree it
is rather drastic and many hardships
exist owing to the test the afflicted persons are put to. Our whole-hearted
sympathy should go out to these afflicted
members of the community. Another
point raised by the learned Senator Lesto
lie referred to farmers suffering from a
shortage of cattle. I can hardly agree
with the hon. Senator in that respect.
The conditions relating to loans
farmers are very drastic. Take, for instance, the clearing of shrubbery from
grass land and the improvement of
drainage. There is a stipulation that the
valuation of £75 or over disqualifies for
a loan. I think that is hardly fair. You
will get a farmer with 100 to 120 acres,
where the valuation very often exceeds
£75. And I am very sorry to say my
experience does not agree with the statement of the Deputy Leader of the House
some time ago that the valuation of a
30-acre farm might be £4. I would be
delighted if the farm I own was based
on that calculation. I feel that the
valuation limit should be removed, because if you take the small farmer you
will find that he can do all that work
during the winter period. It is the farm
of 100 to 150 acres that requires this
work to be done, and in which the farmer
is in a position to give employment. You
are not getting the employment in the
rural districts that you should be getting.
I
Another drawback to the scheme is the
loan period is very short, five years.
respectively suggest that should be extended to 10 years to give encouragement
to the farmer to take advantage of the
loan. Very little advantage has been
taken of these loans up to the present.
And in applying for these loans the
farmer has to get two solvent sureties,
which are usually two neighbouring
farmers. We all know the farmers of
this country have very peculiar ideas.
They do not wish neighbouring farmers
to know the circumstances they are in,
or that it is necessary for them to secure
a loan. Moreover, the farmer hides his
losses very often from his neighbour and
covers them up. That shows the mentality of the farmer, and that there is
no inducement for him to take advantage
of the loan. I feel that a lien on the
deeds of the land should be sufficient for
the Ministry without requiring two
sureties.
In regard to Senator Joseph Cunningham's appeal for the provision of baths,
I was on a committee of the Belfast Corporation, and I feel we are very far
behind in Belfast in regard to the provision of swimming baths. I feel the

Government should help the Corporation
in providing additional swimming ponds
in the city. I think the farming community should be put into a healthy and

community is healthy, eventually all
other industries will follow into that
healthy state. It will put the farmer
in a position to increase the wages of the
farm labourer, which is very necessary at

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I join with
the other Members of the Senate in
offering my congratulations to the hon.
and gallant Member who proposed the
Motion, and, like Senator Cunningham,
I am very pleased to see that two younger
Members of this Chamber were selected
for proposing and seconding the Motion,
and especially that the proposer came
from the historic city of Derry by right
of being its Mayor. We all regret that
Sir Roland Nugent was unable to be
present owing to illness, but our good
Friend, Senator Leslie, who also keeps
us right on many occasions rose to fill the
position admirably with his usual
courtesy and tact.
I join with Senator Joseph Cunningham with regard to the recent visit of
their Majesties to the city of Belfast.
It certainly was one of the greatest sights
that ever I have seen in my life-to see
streets within a mile of this House, streets
of working-class houses and workingclass people with very little of a margin
to spend in any direction, beautifully
decorated for the King's visit. It
showed their intense loyalty and devotion
to their Majesties, and we hope we will
have another visit on a near future
occasion from the same illustrious people.
While we have in the city of Belfast
and Northern Ireland generally a considerable improvement in trade we all
regret we have not it in the same measure
as industry across the water. Some of
those firms are working to full capacity,
and are not able to take any further
orders. Our own firms have been and
are fairly busy, and it may be that they
have been offered armament orders, but
the question of scrapping plant and replacing it with new plant is one that
gives great concern to the heads of local
engineering firms. If it is only going to
be a matter of a few months' work it is
not worth the expense attached to it and
the dislocation of their regular trade.
But we must be progressing when the
register of unemployed has fallen by over
7,000 people.
There is one portion of the Speech that
I would like to draw Members' attention
to. It is the number of people who have
been placed in employment through the
exchange services. The labour exchanges
have been subject to a good deal of criticism. I have myself criticised them on
many occasions, and I am very pleased
to see they have placed such a number
of people in employment as some 28,000.
I was also glad to see that the local authorities are availing themselves of the improved facilities for undertaking works
of public utility. Going through the
country we all see the widening of roads
and the improvement of corners to make
travelling safer. That is all in the right
direction. The linen trade was getting
along splendidly, much better than it
has been for many years, but unfortunately the war in the East between
Japan and China has caused a little bit
of a set-back, and that may entail some
of our firms locally having to go on
short time. That is not the fault of the
Government. It is not the fault of the
manufacturers. It is a fault that can
only be attributed to human nature.
I was
I also join with Senator M'Allister and
Senator Maguire with regard to the proposed changes relating to the Blind
Persons Act.
a member of a
committee of the Belfast Corporation
some years ago, and one of the sections
of the Act, as far as my memory serves
me, was that a person to get a pension
must be totally incapacitated by blindness so that he cannot follow his occupation. That is all right. One naturally saw that if a person could not follow
his occupation he was entitled to the
pension. But those in authority came up
against our committee on many occasions,
and said these persons were capable of
doing other work. Well, if a woman has
been in sewing or working in any of our
factories where it was white work, that
measure of blindness might prevent her
doing that work. It may be there are
some instances in which the afflicted
persons could do other work. But how
are they going to get that other work
when we have so many people unemployed who are possessed of all their
faculties and ready to take the work

Mr. LESLIE: Hear, hear.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I am very
glad to see there is a proposal to assist
housing in urban areas. We see little
cottages which are very picturesque, but
undoubtedly very unhealthy and unsanitary, and in moving throughout the
country it is a delight to see a number
of little houses going up in which the
working classes and people in better
circumstances can have proper housing
accommodation. I hope when the Government are framing their legislation in this
direction that there will be no irksome
regulations made, and that they will take
into consultation the local authorities and
see what is best in every area.
I was a
member of the first housing committee
of the Belfast Corporation, and I remember the regulations that were sent
from Dublin to Belfast with regard to
housing, and those regulations were so
severe that the first houses erected by
the Belfast Corporation cost £1,500
apiece. The material that had to be put
into them was such that no ordinary man
would put them into a house if he were
building it for himself. These houses are
not intended to last for ever, and to
provide extraordinarily dear commodities.
in the building of the houses the Belfast
ratepayers were burdened, and are carrying that burden to this day. They have
had to cut portion of the losses, but the
loss is still there to be faced. That loss
was entailed by the regulations that were
made by those in authority in Dublin.
Therefore I hope that when the regulations are being made by the department
they will make them in consultation with
the local authorities.
I welcome the proposal to amend the
Shops Act. I believe that many of the
traders who are exempted under the existing Shops Act would be very glad to
see legislation introduced that would
afford them some little facility to get out
into God's fresh air at some time of the
evening earlier than they can do at the
present time. If one trader in a district
refuses to close he keeps all the traders
in the district open. In one branch of
business a trader in York Street refused
to close, with the result that he kept all
similar traders open from the Antrim
Road to Corporation Street, down York
Street and Shore Road. That should not
be. The confectionery trade and tobacconist and stationery trades would be
willing to close much earlier than they do
at the present time, but they are subject
to what their neighbour does, and they
must either follow suit or lose business.
I hope this fact will be borne in mind
when the Act is being amended.
I am whole-heartedly in sympathy with
the physical training proposed for the
young people, and the proposal to allow
school-teachers to surrender some portion
of their pension in order to provide a
pension for their wife or daughter. We
all know the hardships that arise when
a pension dies with the pensioner, and his
wife and family are left practically penniless. I have great pleasure in supporting the Motion for the Address.

and people are not so ready to help them.

whole. When the wage earner is earning wages the business man can sell the

We have thousands of men who cannot

I read in the Press a short time ago
that across in England there is a suggestion or movement to do something for
the aged worker, that is, the man over
65 years of age.
We are all well aware
that a great number of men over 65
years of age are capable of working. But,
according to law, when these men come
up to 65 years of age they have to cease
the employment in which they are engaged and go off on a pension of 10s a
week. It seems to me rather hard that
a man who is capable of working and
doing a good day's work when he comes
to 65 should have to go on 10s a week.
I would like to see something done for

across the water is I do not know, but
there is going to be something done for
these people in the way of providing
plots of land or something. The suggestion is to give these men something to do
to earn something extra to the 10s a
week.
Then there is the agricultural labourer
mentioned in the Speech. We passed a
Bill some time ago, and I supported that
Bill, and the reason I supported it was
that I had in mind a certain class of
agricultural labourer, what might be
called the casual labourer.
see
I can
that this man is only employed at certain
times, in the spring of the year, at hay
time and harvest time. And then in the
winter there was nothing for him to do.
In my young days a man like that would
have found employment the year round.
Threshing was done with a flail, and he
could clean corn. But that has been
done away with by machinery.
The
thresher comes to the farmer and threshes
and cleans his corn, and there is no work
left. I supported the Bill, but at the
same time I had at the back of my mind
the idea that this might be abused, and
I find in some cases it has been abused.
Last May during the hiring time men
engaged for six months.
I have been informed by more than
one or two farmers that they could not
get farm labourers to engage for six
months. That was a hardship to a great
many farmers. I know the country
pretty well, and there are aged men and
women that require a younger man about
the place during the night. They require
the hired man on the premises lest anything should go wrong with the horses.
These people, as far as I understand,
have been deprived in the past summer of
a servant boy to remain on the premises.
They all want day work so as to qualify
for the dole and go off and enjoy themselves during the winter time. That is
an abuse. I was afraid of that abuse, and
I find it takes place at the present time.
It is up to the Minister of Labour to see
that single young men will engage for
six months so that these people depending upon their labour could have their
services during the six months.

have risen and the farmer has got to

45 National Health Insurance Bill- 7 DECEMBER 1937

Second Reading
Consolidated Fund Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Mr. Long.)
National Health Insurance (Juvenile
Contributors and Young
Persons) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The object of this Bill
can be stated in a few words.
It proposes to apply to Northern Ireland the
provisions of a Bill (of which hon. Members have received copies) at present
before the Parliament of the United
Kingdom. That Bill provides for bridging a gap which at present exists in the
arrangements for the medical treatment
of workers. Up to the date of leaving
the public elementary school a child is
under the care of the school medical
officers, but on taking up employment
he does not become entitled to medical
benefit under the National Health Insurance Scheme until he reaches the age of
16. Under the provisions of the Bill
the intervening period will be covered,
and as soon as he enters insurable employment he will receive medical benefit.
His title to medical benefit will hold
good in any case until the age of sixteen
and a half, and, if after that age he
remains insurably employed, he will continue to receive the benefit under the
ordinary provisions of the National
Health Insurance Scheme.
A further point of some importance
is that provision may be made by regulations enabling the period of juvenile
membership of an approved society to be
counted towards the qualifying period
for such additional treatment benefits
as dental benefit and ophthalmic benefit,
when the juvenile contributor becomes an
ordinary insured person.
Insurance practitioners will, under the
Bill, be able to obtain any information
they may require from the school medical
records of young persons whom they
accept for treatment under the medical
benefit scheme. Such records may often
be of great value when medical attention
is subsequently required.
The provisions of the United Kingdom
Bill are set out more fully in the White
Paper already supplied to hon. Members,
and I think they will commend themselves
as covering, so far as medical benefit is
concerned, a period of life hitherto unprovided for, when medical attention
might be successful in arresting developments which would have serious results
at later ages.
The Bill adopts the procedure followed
in the National Health Insurance Acts
of 1928, 1932, and 1935, in applying to
Northern Ireland, with whatever adaptations may be necessary, the provisions of
the United Kingdom Measure. The
Health Insurance Scheme is in force as a
single scheme throughout the United
Kingdom. The principal Act is the
National Health Insurance Act, 1936, a
Westminster Act, which repealed and reenacted not only the Acts passed by the
Parliament of the United Kingdom in
relation to health insurance, but also
those passed by the Parliament of Northern Ireland and the Orders in Council
made under them.
Hon. Senators will appreciate that the
extension of the United Kingdom
Measure to Northern Ireland is the most
convenient way of effecting an amendment
in what is administratively a United
Kingdom scheme, and is in accordance
with the policy which has been approved
by both Parliaments on many occasions
in the past. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a First time."
Mr. LESLIE: I certainly approve
whole-heartedly of the object of the Bill
before us, or rather perhaps not so much
the object of the Bill as the object which
the Bill desires to effect by applying
another Bill, that is, the Bill at present
before Parliament in England. But I
could have wished that we could have
taken the matter up a little later, that is
to say, when we have before us exactly
what the United Kingdom Measure is
going to do. As it is all we have seen is
the Bill as passed through Committee in
the House of Commons at Westminster. I
do not know whether it is probable that
any alteration will be made.
I am inclined to think, on the whole, that it is
improbable there will be any change. But
I could have wished that instead of following the procedure to which the Parliamentary Secretary has alluded which was
adopted three or four times before as
regards insurance matters we could have
had a Bill of our own. Then I think we
could have kept a rather closer eye on any
adaptations made.
47 National Health Insurance Bill—

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Leslie]
It will be observed the Parliamentary
Secretary alluded to it-that the effect
of the proposed Measure will be to cast
yet another burden on the teachers in our
elementary schools. I do not know
whether that will be very popular
amongst them or whether it will give very
much trouble; but the fact remains that
that additional burden is being placed
upon them, and it is, to some extent,
camouflaged by the method adopted in
the Bill before us.
Then as regards procedure of the Bill
I confess I am in some difficulty. We
have, of course, to consider not only the
Bill as we have it, but the United
Kingdom Bill and the old Act of 1936 as
it will be when the United Kingdom Act
has come into force. That is to say, we
are really considering a built-up Measure
without having the foundations before us
in the text of our own Bill. It makes
things a little hard to follow and it may
be that that is what gives rise to the
difficulty which I am about to point out.
The first Sub-clause of the first Clause
of the Bill limits itself as I understand it
entirely to the Bill now before the Parliament of the United Kingdom. But then
we go on in our next Sub-clause to provide the adaptation of Part X of the main
Insurance Act of 1936. Of course, as the
House knows, it is perfectly open to us
to do anything we like with Part 10 of
the National Health Insurance Act, 1936,
because it provided that it should be
treated as an Act passed before the
appointed day, and, therefore, it is
subject to amendment by this Parliament.
But I cannot myself make out from
the text that we have taken any power to
make any adaptations in Part 10. The
power to adapt is limited to the United
Kingdom Bill as it is at present, and
does not go so far as adapting Part 10 of
the Act of 1936. It is quite within our
power to adapt Part 10, but I think we
have to take power in this Bill to do it,
and I do not see that we have accomplished
it. It might be better to put something
on the Paper at the next stage to clear
up that little matter, because the Parliamentary Secretary might not be disposed
to enter into a somewhat lengthy explanation on the subject this afternoon. There
cannot be any doubt as to the intention
of the Bill, but I do find some difficulty
in following the expression of that intention.
Any difficulty that might occur is to be
found in relation to the second class of
juvenile contributor, the one who does
not join an approved society. Reference
to the United Kingdom Bill will show
that special provisions are necessary there
for the purpose of those young people,
and I think that Part 10, to which I have
so often referred, will also want amending. I think it will be convenient to
put something on the Paper at the next
stage which will enable an explanation of
an apparent difficulty to be given.
I should also like to know what are the
Acts of 1936 referred to at the top of the
third page of the Bill. I can myself find
only one, the National Health Insurance
Act, 1936. The only other Act of that
year dealing with insurance was the
Widows', Orphans' and Old Age Contributory Pensions Act, which did in one
Section make some reference to the
National Health Insurance Committee,
but I do not think that would entitle that
particular Measure to be called one of the
National Health Insurance Acts. The
point is of some importance, because if
there is some other Act dealing with
health insurance we ought to be able to
find it. Of course it may be a misprint.
Apart from those rather side issues
which I have mentioned I do not think
there should be any difficulty about the
Bill at all. I think it will fill what has
been called the gap, and I do hope particularly that everything that is possible
will be done in the way of giving dental
treatment to those young contributors. I
believe that the period from 14 to 16
is a period in which attention to dental
matters is of great importance as dealing
with the health and general well-being of
the subject throughout after life. There
is, of course, no suggestion in the Bill
that benefit in money is to be paid to the
juvenile contributor. The whole benefit
he will receive is medical benefit, and
that, I think, was made clear in another
place, and certainly should be remembered by anybody considering the Bill. As
I said at the beginning, I support the
Bill.
Sir JAMES WILTON: I would like to
congratulate the Government on having
introduced this Bill. Almost everyone
who has any connection with education

New Industries (Development) (No. 2)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The primary object of this
Bill is to augment the facilities already
made available to industrial undertakings
by previous legislation, and its provisions.
can best be made clear to hon. Members
by reference to these existing facilities.
Hon. Members will recall that under
the new Industries Act passed in July
last, financial assistance by way of both
loans and grants was offered to any industrial undertaking established in the Province during the currency of the Act,
provided that the undertaking was considered likely to provide and maintain
additional employment to a substantial
extent. Grants can be made under that
Act in respect of the site in the form
of annual payments over a maximum
period of 20 years, and, in respect of
buildings leased for the purpose of the
undertakings, in the form of annual
payments over a maximum period of five
years. Loans can be offered to cover the
purchase price of existing buildings, the
cost of adapting existing buildings to the
requirements of the undertaking and the
cost of the erection of new premises. These
loans are free of interest for the first five
years, and then become repayable with
interest over period agreed upon
between the Ministry of Finance and the
undertaking concerned.
The main object of the existing Act
was, therefore, to free an eligible undertaking from the financial burden involved
in the acquisition of premises and site
during the initial stages of its development.
The Bill now before the House provides
for further financial assistance to new
industrial undertakings over and above.
the assistance already obtainable towards
the cost of premises. In the first place,
contributions may be made towards the
income-tax payable during the first five
years of the existence of the undertaking.
Secondly, loans or guarantees, covering
principal and interest, are made available
towards the cost of the equipment and
other capital requirements. Hon. Members will observe that, while these loans
resemble those already available in respect of premises in that repayment does
not commence until after the first five
years, they will not be free of interest
during the initial stages.
In addition to widening the facilities
available to undertakings newly established, the Bill also provides assistance for
extensions of existing undertakings.
While it would clearly be inadvisable to
offer financial assistance towards every
minor extension of an existing undertaking, it is just as clearly unfair to exclude
an existing undertaking from the benefits
of the Act where an extension would provide additional and permanent employment to the same extent as would be
afforded by a newly established undertaking. Extensions of existing undertakings,
therefore, which are likely to create
sufficient employment to meet the statutory requirements will also become eligible.
for loan or guarantee facilities in respect
of their capital requirements. The provision for increasing the maximum sum
available for loans naturally follows from
the extension of the facilities for new industrial enterprises and needs no further
explanation.
It is of paramount importance in legislation of this type to avoid endangering
Road and Railway Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
the interests of existing undertakings and
the Act passed in July of this year seemed
at that time to have gone as far as possible without running this risk. The experience gained in the administration of
that Act, however, has shown that there
is ample room for an extension of the
facilities offered to industrial enterprise
without invading the province of existing
undertakings, and it is on the basis of this
experience that this Bill has been introduced.
Opinions may differ as to the method,
but no one, I am sure, will disagree with
the objective of this Bill, namely, to
broaden the basis of industry in the Province and so to aid in solving the problem
of unemployment. We are favoured with
the presence of the Minister of Commerce,
who will explain any points that may be
raised in the course of the debate on the
Bill. I beg to move, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed."(Mr. Long.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Ordered: That the Bill be read the
Third time on Thursday next.
Road and Railway Transport Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Mr. LONG: The purpose of this
Measure is to enable the Northern Ireland
Road Transport Board to meet interest
charges out of capital where necessary
during the first five years of their
working. The extension of this facility.
to the Board does not invoke any new
principle as such an arrangement is generally necessary to tide undertakings of the
kind over their first few years, when the
costs of reorganisation must be faced
before the economies due to the large
scale of their operations can be effected.
Hon. Members will remember that
similar provision was made, and was
found necessary, in the case of the Electricity Board.
The task of preparing the scheme regulating the arrangement whereby the
traffic receipts of the Board are to be
pooled with those of the railway companies
operating in Northern Ireland in order
that the pooled receipts may be divided
between the Board and the companies in
the proportion set out in the Road and
Railway Transport Act of 1935, has been
difficult and protracted, and has, I understand, only recently been completed.
In the absence of an agreed pooling
scheme it has not been possible to complete the preparation of the Board's
accounts for the first year of its operation,
but the Board felt that its position in
respect of the year was such that payment
of interest was justifiable.
During the second year of their operation, that is, the year ended 30th September last, the Board acquired a great
many undertakings, and, as hon.
Members are aware, the method of payment by the Board for undertakings
transferred to them was in part by
transport stock issued by them, and in
part by money borrowed under temporary
borrowing powers conferred upon them
by Section 20 of the 1935 Act.
I am informed that the Board do not
expect the revenue from the second year's
working of the undertaking to suffice to
pay either the interest on the stock, or
the interest due on borrowings.
Although, as I have explained, the
Board were uncertain as to the result
of their first year's working, they have
paid the interest on the stock for the
year, and some doubt has been raised
as to the legality of such payment, which,
it is possible, in so far as it was not paid
out of revenue, was not authorised under
the Road and Railway Transport Act,
1935.
The powers which it is proposed to
accord to the Board under the Bill may
be exercised only to such extent as may
be approved by the Ministry of Finance
and the Ministry of Home Affairs. I beg
to move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I should like to
be informed whether it is a fact, as it
appears to be from the speech of the
Parliamentary Secretary, that the Transport Board are going to pay no interest
on loans for the second year. Is it a
fact that the Board have been working
at a loss?

with lengthy explanations. The supplies.
for which the authority of Parliament is
sought are as follows:-To defray the expenses of the Government incurred in
connection with the Coronation celebrations and visit of their Majesties the King
and Queen to Northern Ireland, £26,950;
to a survey of the condition of the Lagan
Navigation, £250; grant-in-aid to the
Lagan Navigation Company to enable
them to proceed with urgent repair to the
banks of the canal, £1,500; to necessary
preliminary expenditure in connection
with the Northern Ireland exhibit at the
Scottish Empire Exhibition to be held in
Glasgow next year, £1,000; to advertising
the facilities offered to industrialists
establishing new enterprises in Northern.
Ireland, £1,000, and grant-in-aid to the
Belfast Harbour Commissioners towards
the cost of construction and maintenance
of the Belfast Harbour Airport, £750. I
beg to move, "That the Bill be now read
a Second time."

Misuse of Unemployment Benefit 60
Question to Minister-
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 14th December, 1937.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Question to Minister.
MISUSE OF UNEMPLOYMENT
BENEFIT.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether, under the
Unemployment Insurance Acts, there is
any means of ensuring that the money
which a man is entitled to draw on behalf of his wife and children is used for
the benefit of the wife and children, and
is not drunk or gambled away while the
family are left in starvation.
In asking the question I should like to
point out that guardians find a great deal
of trouble with regard to this matter.
They find in a good many instances that
where the unemployed man is drawing
the dole, he is simply drinking and
gambling it. He draws the dole not only
for himself but also for his wife and
children, and he goes and spends that
and gives nothing to either wife or
children. What inspired the question
was the fact that a short time ago I had
a visit from a lady whose husband was
drawing the dole and had been drawing
it for a considerable time. He had been
drawing for himself, the wife, and six
children, and she told me that although
he had been drawing it religiously every
week she never got a halfpenny. She had
got to rely on neighbours and charitable
societies for a living. I do think the
Government should devise some ways and
means whereby in a case like that the
Iwife should be able to draw at least her
portion of the
the allowances and the
children's, so that they would get the
benefit the Government provides.
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM: I
am very glad that this question has been
asked by the hon. Senator. It seems to
me that in a case where the dole is given
for the children and wife, the husband
is acting as a trustee, and if he will not
spend the money for the object for which
we have voted it, I think there should
be some remedy. I am perfectly sure
that is not the universal case. The lawbreaker, fortunately, happens to be in
the minority. In a case where a man is
working, if he neglects his wife and
family the law steps in and says "you
must provide for your wife and family."
I think it is far more necessary for the
man to provide for his wife and family
out of money which Parliament has voted
him for that purpose.
As I say, I am
very glad that Mr. McLaughlin has raised
this question.
Mr. LONG: Under the Unemployment
Assistance Act, where it is necessary to
protect the interests of persons dependent
on an applicant for unemployment assistance, there is power to withhold payment
of the allowance from the applicant personally, and either pay it to another
member of his household or to issue it
in kind.
Unemployment benefit is payable as of
right, subject to the satisfaction of
certain specific conditions, in return for
the payment of insurance contributions,
and the Unemployment Insurance Act
gives no direct power to require a
claimant to use in any particular way
the benefit received by him.
The continued receipt by the claimant
of an increase of benefit in respect of his
children is, however, contingent on his
contributing at least that amount to their
maintenance, and the same applies to the
payment of increased benefit in respect
of a claimant's wife, if she is not living
with her husband.
In further explanation, might I say
that to be entitled to increased benefit
(popularly known as dependants' benefit)
in respect of his children, a claimant must
prove that he is wholly or mainly maintaining them. If he is living apart from
his wife he must similarly prove that he
is wholly or mainly maintaining her, in
order to be entitled to an increase of
benefit in respect of her, but he is not
required to prove maintenance of his wife
if she is living with him.
The Act provides that a claimant shall
not be deemed to be wholly or mainly
maintaining a dependant, unless he contributes towards the maintenance of that
dependant an amount not less than the
amount of the increase of benefit received
in respect of the dependant.
Question to Minister-
14 DECEMBER 1937 Misuse of Unemployment Benefit 62
Mr. HILL DICKSON: Might I ask if
the Government will give the instructions
which the Deputy Leader of the House
has read to those who are responsible for
distributing the dole. We know that in
many cases this money goes to the bookmaker instead of to the wife and children.
I would suggest that the Government
should send a circular setting out these
instructions to all those who administer
unemployment benefit, and that might
help to remedy this evil.
Mr. LONG: If the views of Senator
Hill Dickson are the views of the House,
I will be very pleased to make representations to the Government to that effect.
Mr. LESLIE: I conclude I am right
in imagining that there is nothing in the
Unemployment Insurance Acts, or the
Unemployment Assistance Acts which, in
any way, encroaches on the old common
law rule that a man is bound to maintain
his wife and children. Therefore, I
presume, that a man who is getting insurance benefit or assistance is still liable,
if he does not maintain his wife and
children, to be brought before a court
of summary jurisdiction and dealt with
as the law provides. I presume that is
still the case.
Mr. LONG: That is so.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: I sincerely trust
the Government will adopt the suggestion
of Senator Hill Dickson, because at the
present time there is abuse of the dole.
I know that for a fact, because I have a
good deal to do with the administration
of a coal relief fund in Armagh, and I
know that a great many men
are not
alone drinking the allowance given to
their wives and children but are also
gambling it. Therefore, I hope the
Government will do all they can to put
a stop to this abuse. I quite understand
that what Senator Leslie has said about
the common law is correct, but a wife
does not always wish to bring her husband
before a court.
Sir JAMES WILTON: Might I ask
if there is any procedure for putting into
force the powers which the Ministry of
Labour have in regard to persons who
commit the abuses complained of? Personally, I have not heard of any case
where a complaint has been made, but
I can understand that there would be
great reluctance on the part of a wife or
child to make such a complaint. I would
suggest that some means might be found
by which the putting into force of the
powers of the Ministry, where necessary,
should be taken out of the hands of the
wife and children.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Whilst in sympathy with the views expressed, I have
a certain hesitation in wholly supporting
the suggestions that have been made in
the absence of any evidence as to the
administration that is to be responsible
for putting the powers into force.
I am
opposed to placing in the hands of any
branch of the Civil Service powers that
should be exercised only by a court of
justice. What Senator Leslie has said
regarding the common law rule is quite
true. If a wife is separated from her
husband those proceedings would be taken
and would probably succeed, but if the
wife is living in the house with her
husband the abuse might be equally bad.
I suggest that if any change is to be
made it should be made through Parliament, and not through any regulations,
because the Ministry might take the view
that this was an indication that they were
to tighten up the administration of this
relief. I would like an assurance from
the Government that any steps taken will
have the approval of Parliament.
Mr. LONG: I should like to disabuse
the mind of Senator McAllister that there
will be any tightening up in the administration of unemployment benefit
or
assistance. I shall unofficially make
inquiries and communicate with the
hon. Senator who has put the question
down and see if anything can be done.
National Health Insurance (Juvenile
Contributors and Young
Persons) Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Mr. Long.)
CLAUSE 1 (Application to Northern
Ireland of Act passed by United Kingdom
Parliament).
The following Amendments stood on
the Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Long:-
63 Contributory Pensions Bil!

THE SENATE
Adjournment
to
Clause 1, sub-clause (1), leave out "In
the event of any Act (in this Act referred
as "the United Kingdom Act")
being," and insert "Whereas an Act (in
this Act referred to as "the United
Kingdom Act") has been."
leave out
"then in that event" and insert "There-
Clause 1, sub-clause (1),
fore."
Mr. LONG: The two amendments
on the Paper are drafting amendments,
rendered necessary by the fact that the
United Kingdom Measure referred to in
the Bill received the Royal Assent on
Thursday last, the 9th instant.
When
our Bill was introduced it was impossible
to forecast, with any degree of accuracy,
what progress would be made at Westminster with the United Kingdom
Measure. Hon. Members will see that
the Bill, as drawn, provides that "in the
event of any Act being passed" by the
United Kingdom Parliament to amend
the National Health Insurance Act, 1936,
in certain particulars, that Act may be
applied to Northern Ireland either by
Act of our Parliament or by Order in
Council. It refers to a future event.
Now that the United Kingdom Measure
is law the words used are inappropriate,
and in order to make our Bill effective
we must refer to an Act which has been
passed. Otherwise it could be argued
that an Order in Council made under our
Act could only apply to a future Act,
that is, one passed after our Bill received
the Royal Assent. I beg to move the
amendments on the Paper.
Mr. LESLIE: I rise, perhaps rather
unnecessarily, to lay emphasis on what
the Parliamentary Secretary has just
said. That is the importance of passing
these amendments in order to prevent our
Act being construed as applying to an
Act passed by the Imperial Parliament
after the date of our Act. That is the
crux, as I understand it, of the whole
situation. The amendments
absolutely necessary.
Amendments agreed to.
are
Resolved, "That this Bill do now pass."
-(Mr. Long.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill with
some amendments to which they desire
their concurrence.

the Clerk of the Crown that the Right
Hon. John Hanna Robb, K.C., of
6 Deramore Park, Belfast, was on the
15th day of December, 1937, duly elected
a Senator to serve in the Parliament of
Northern Ireland in the room of the
Right Hon. the Viscount Charlemont,
resigned.

THE SENATE
A PROCLAMATION
FOR DISSOLVING THE FOURTH
PARLIAMENT OF NORTHERN
IRELAND AND DECLARING THE
CALLING OF ANOTHER.
WHEREAS it is provided by section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said
Act, that the Governor of Northern
Ireland shall, in His Majesty's name,
summon, prorogue and dissolve the
Parliament of Northern Ireland:
And whereas it is desirable that the
present Parliament of Northern Ireland
should be dissolved and that a new
Parliament should be called:
Now therefore I, James Albert
Edward, Duke of Abercorn, Knight
of the Most Noble Order of the Garter,
Knight of the Most Illustrious Order of
Saint Patrick, Governor of Northern
Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish
and declare His Majesty's Pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the date
of this Proclamation, dissolved, and that
the Senators and Members of the House
of Commons be discharged from further
Attendance thereat:
And I do Hereby make known to all
His Majesty's loving subjects His Royal
Will and Pleasure to call a new Parliament of Northern Ireland, and do
further declare that I have taken Order
that Writs shall be issued forthwith in
His Majesty's name under the Great
Seal of Northern Ireland, in due Form
and according to Law, for calling a
Parliament of Northern Ireland to meet
at Stormont, Northern Ireland, on
Tuesday the First day of March next,
and for causing the Senators and
Commons who are to serve in the said
Parliament to be duly returned to and
give their Attendance in His Majesty's
said Parliament on Tuesday the First
day of March next, which Writs are to
be returnable in due course of Law.
Given this Twentieth day of January,
in the year of our Lord one thousand
nine hundred and thirty-eight.
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
INDEX.
